The Second Proof: Independence of Physical and Psychological Differences
Second proof from lack of correlation between physical and psychological differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that soul substances are different, which uses the observation that two people with very different physical constitutions can have similar psychological traits, and vice versa, showing that physical and psychological domains are independent.

Core Idea
Physical similarity does not guarantee psychological similarity; therefore the soul is not a product of the body.

The second proof strengthens the case for the difference of soul substances by considering interpersonal comparisons. We often see two individuals who are very different in their bodily composition yet share similar levels of perception and character, while others with similar bodies have very different minds. This lack of correlation suggests that the psychological realm operates on its own principles, independent of physical temperament. Let's explore this argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic text for the second proof and its translation. Notice the comparative structure: two cases of similarity/difference.
2
Identify the two observations
The proof presents two scenarios: (1) two people differ in bodily composition but are similar in psychological traits, and (2) two people are similar in temperament but differ in psychological traits. These are the key observations.
This is a double argument showing lack of correlation.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
From these observations, the author infers that equality in temperament can exist without equality in psychological states, and difference in temperament can exist without difference in psychological states. Therefore, the two domains—physical and psychological—are separate principles with no causal connection.
The conclusion is that each domain is 'a principle in itself' (أصل بنفسه).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوية وأما القائلون باختلاف جواهر النفوس . فقد احتجوا عليه بوجوه اعتبارية الحجة الأولى : إن الإنسان الواحد قد ينتقل من المزاج الحار ، إلى المزاج البارد ، وكذا القول في الرطب واليابس ، وأما قوة الإدراك ، وقوة الأخلاق ، فهي باقية بحالها من أول العمر إلى آخره، من غير تفاوت أصلا ، مثل إنسان صفراوي المزاج استولى عليه الحمى البلغمية أو الاستسقاء أو الفالج . ومثل انسان بلغمي المزاج استولى عليه حمى الغب والرسام الحار ، فإن كل واحد من هؤلاء يبقى على المقدار الأول الذي كان له من الإدراك والخلق ، ولو كان الاختلاف الحاصل في قوة الإدراك والفعل ، معللا باختلاف الأمزجة . لوجب أن يحصل اختلاف حال الإدراك والخلق ، عند اختلاف أحوال الأمزجة. ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا : أن ذلك ليس لأجل الأمزجة ، بل لاختلاف ماهيات النفوس . الحجة الثانية : إنا نرى إنسانين يختلفان في التركيب البدني ، والتأليف المزاجي جداً. مع أنهما يتشابهان في قوة الإدراك والخلق جسداً . ولما رأينا : أن المساواة في المزاج ، قد تنفك عن المساواة في الأحوال النفسانية ، والاختلاف في المزاج قد ينفك عن الاختلاف في الأحوال النفسانية ، علمنا : أن كل واحد من هذين البابين : أصل بنفسه ، ولا تعلق لأحدهما بالآخر الحجة الثالثة : إن الأعضاء البدنية آلات للنفوس في الأعمال المختلفة ، وكون الآلة صالحة لا يقتضي حصول ملكة تلك الأفعال في ذوات الفاعلين ألا ترى أن النجار ، إذا كان وقدومه شديد الصلاحية للنحت ، وكان ومنشاره شديد الصلاحية للنشر ، وكانت مثقبته شديدة الصلاحية
Strong. And as for those who maintain the difference of the substances of souls, they have argued for it with considered proofs. The first proof: A single person may move from a hot temperament to a cold one, and similarly regarding wet and dry, but the strength of perception and the strength of character remain in their state from the beginning of life to its end, without any variation at all, such as a person of choleric temperament who is overcome by phlegmatic fever, dropsy, or paralysis, and such as a person of phlegmatic temperament who is overcome by tertian fever and hot delirium; each of these remains on the original measure of perception and character that he had. And if the variation occurring in the strength of perception and action were caused by variation of temperaments, then a variation in the state of perception and character would necessarily occur when the states of temperaments vary. But since the matter is not so, we know that this is not due to temperaments, but rather to the difference of the essences of souls. The second proof: We see two individuals who differ greatly in bodily composition and temperamental structure, while they are similar in the strength of perception and character, and vice versa. And since we see that equality in temperament may be free of equality in psychological states, and difference in temperament may be free of difference in psychological states, we know that each of these two domains is a principle in itself, and there is no connection between them. The third proof: Bodily organs are instruments for souls in various actions, and the suitability of the instrument does not necessitate the possession of the faculty of those actions in the agents themselves. Do you not see that the carpenter, if his adze is extremely suitable for carving, and his saw is extremely suitable for cutting, and his drill is extremely suitable [for drilling]...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs from those who argue that the substances of souls are essentially different from one another. The first proof uses the observation that a person can undergo drastic changes in physical temperament (e.g., from hot to cold, or from dry to wet) yet the strength of their perception and character remains constant throughout life. If perception and character were caused by temperament, they would change with temperament. Since they do not, the cause must be the difference in soul essences. The second proof notes that two people can have very different bodily compositions yet possess similar perception and character, and conversely similar compositions can produce different psychological traits. This independence shows that physical constitution and psychological states are separate principles with no causal link. The third proof (incomplete) argues that bodily organs are merely instruments for the soul. Just as a carpenter's tools being sharp does not imply that he has the skill, similarly the fitness of organs does not by itself produce the soul's faculties. The proof is cut off before completion.

Key Terms
جواهر النفوس
مزاج
إدراك
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a person's psychological traits are directly determined by their physical constitution (e.g., 'sanguine' personality from blood dominance).
Correction: The second proof shows that two people with very different physical constitutions can share similar psychological traits, and vice versa, disproving a direct causal link between body and mind.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude from the observation that two people with different temperaments can have similar perception and character?
A) That the soul is the same in all people.
B) That the physical and psychological domains are independent of each other.
C) That temperament determines all psychological traits.
D) That perception depends on bodily organs.

Key Takeaway
The lack of consistent correlation between physical constitution and psychological traits proves that the soul operates on its own independent principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's intelligence or character based on their physical appearance or health? How does this proof call that habit into question?
?
Think of a person you admire for their perception and character. Does their physical makeup matter in that admiration? What does that tell you about the true source of their worth?
?
If the soul is independent of the body, what responsibility do you have to develop your inner self, regardless of your physical circumstances? How will you act on that today?

The Third Proof: Instruments Do Not Imply Faculty
Third proof from the instrument-faculty distinction (incomplete).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the difference of soul substances, which argues that bodily organs are mere instruments for the soul and their suitability does not guarantee the soul's faculties, as illustrated by the carpenter analogy.

Core Idea
Having a good instrument does not mean one has the skill; similarly, the body's fitness does not create the soul's abilities.

The third proof approaches the issue from a different angle: the relationship between instruments and the skills they serve. Just as a carpenter's sharp tools do not themselves carve wood—the carpenter's skill is separate—so too the body's organs are instruments for the soul's actions. The suitability of the organs does not produce the soul's faculties; rather, the soul possesses its own distinct essence that enables it to use those instruments. This argument further establishes the independence of the soul from the body. However, note that the passage is cut off at the end of the page, so the proof is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof passage
Read the Arabic text for the third proof (as far as it goes) and its translation. Notice the analogy with the carpenter.
2
Understand the instrument-faculty distinction
The proof distinguishes between an instrument (آلة) being suitable for a task and the agent (الفاعل) possessing the faculty (ملكة) to perform that task. The suitability of the instrument does not by itself give the agent the skill.
Example: a carpenter's adze may be extremely sharp, but that doesn't make him a good carver.
3
Apply to the soul-body relationship
Similarly, the bodily organs (like eyes, brain) may be perfectly suited for perceiving, but that does not necessitate that the soul has the faculty of perception. The soul's faculties are its own, not generated by the body's instrumentality.
The proof is incomplete; it likely concludes that the soul's essence is the source of faculties, not the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوية وأما القائلون باختلاف جواهر النفوس . فقد احتجوا عليه بوجوه اعتبارية الحجة الأولى : إن الإنسان الواحد قد ينتقل من المزاج الحار ، إلى المزاج البارد ، وكذا القول في الرطب واليابس ، وأما قوة الإدراك ، وقوة الأخلاق ، فهي باقية بحالها من أول العمر إلى آخره، من غير تفاوت أصلا ، مثل إنسان صفراوي المزاج استولى عليه الحمى البلغمية أو الاستسقاء أو الفالج . ومثل انسان بلغمي المزاج استولى عليه حمى الغب والرسام الحار ، فإن كل واحد من هؤلاء يبقى على المقدار الأول الذي كان له من الإدراك والخلق ، ولو كان الاختلاف الحاصل في قوة الإدراك والفعل ، معللا باختلاف الأمزجة . لوجب أن يحصل اختلاف حال الإدراك والخلق ، عند اختلاف أحوال الأمزجة. ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا : أن ذلك ليس لأجل الأمزجة ، بل لاختلاف ماهيات النفوس . الحجة الثانية : إنا نرى إنسانين يختلفان في التركيب البدني ، والتأليف المزاجي جداً. مع أنهما يتشابهان في قوة الإدراك والخلق جسداً . ولما رأينا : أن المساواة في المزاج ، قد تنفك عن المساواة في الأحوال النفسانية ، والاختلاف في المزاج قد ينفك عن الاختلاف في الأحوال النفسانية ، علمنا : أن كل واحد من هذين البابين : أصل بنفسه ، ولا تعلق لأحدهما بالآخر الحجة الثالثة : إن الأعضاء البدنية آلات للنفوس في الأعمال المختلفة ، وكون الآلة صالحة لا يقتضي حصول ملكة تلك الأفعال في ذوات الفاعلين ألا ترى أن النجار ، إذا كان وقدومه شديد الصلاحية للنحت ، وكان ومنشاره شديد الصلاحية للنشر ، وكانت مثقبته شديدة الصلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the soul's faculties (like perception) are generated by the body's organs themselves (e.g., the brain produces thought).
Correction: The third proof argues that organs are merely instruments; the faculty belongs to the soul, not to the instrument. Having a suitable organ does not create the faculty.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the third proof use to illustrate that the suitability of an instrument does not imply possession of the faculty?
A) A painter and his brush.
B) A carpenter and his tools.
C) A writer and his pen.
D) A musician and his instrument.

Key Takeaway
Bodily organs are instruments, not the source of faculties; the soul's faculties stem from its own distinct essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you relying on your physical health and energy to perform good deeds, while neglecting the cultivation of sincerity and devotion in your soul? How will you train your soul's faculty of sincerity today?
?
When you pray, do you focus more on the physical movements and posture than on the presence of your soul before Allah? This passage shows that the body is only an instrument. What would it mean for you to pray with your soul fully engaged?
?
You may have all the tools for learning—books, teachers, time—but if your soul lacks the desire for truth, those tools are useless. What will you do today to ignite that desire within your soul?

The Three Primary Faculties of the Soul
Al-Razi enumerates the three primary faculties of the soul: rational, irascible, and appetitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three primary faculties of the soul as classified by al-Razi, their hierarchy, and how they give rise to all moral traits.

Core Idea
The soul's three faculties form a hierarchy of nobility, branching into all moral traits.

Al-Razi presents a foundational classification of the soul's powers that underlies Islamic ethical theory. This passage enumerates the three faculties from which all moral traits arise. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for grasping how human actions are analyzed in kalam and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the classification sink in.
Notice the concise, authoritative style.
2
Identify the three faculties
Locate the three faculties: rational, irascible, and appetitive. Note their associated bodily locations and order of nobility.
The order is most noble (rational), middle (irascible), lowest (appetitive).
3
Reflect on the branching claim
Consider the statement that all moral traits branch from these three. How might this classification help in analyzing character?
This connects ethics to the soul's constitution.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to verify your grasp of the hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن الحكماء بينوا في كتب الأخلاق: أن القوى الأصلية النفسانية: ثلاثة. أشرفها: القوة النطقية الدماغية، وأوسطها القوة الغضبية القلبية، وأدونها القوة الشهوانية الكبدية. وجميع الأخلاق والصفات إنما تتشعب من هذه الثلاثة.
Know that the philosophers explained in the books of ethics that the original psychic faculties are three: the most noble is the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-nutqiyya) which is cerebral, the middle is the irascible faculty (al-quwwa al-ghadabiyya) which is cardiac, and the lowest is the appetitive faculty (al-quwwa al-shahwaniyya) which is hepatic. And all moral traits and attributes branch from these three.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three primary faculties of the soul according to the philosophers. Al-Razi states that the philosophers have explained in ethics books that the original psychic faculties (al-quwa al-asliyya al-nafsaniyya) are three. He orders them by nobility: the highest is the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-nutqiyya), which is located in the brain (dimagh). The middle is the irascible faculty (al-quwwa al-ghadabiyya), located in the heart (qalb). The lowest is the appetitive faculty (al-quwwa al-shahwaniyya), located in the liver (kabid). He concludes that all moral traits and attributes branch from these three. This classification is foundational for understanding human psychology and ethics in Islamic philosophy and kalam.

Key Terms
القوى الأصلية النفسانية
القوة النطقية الدماغية
القوة الغضبية القلبية
القوة الشهوانية الكبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three faculties are separate substances.
Correction: They are powers of the same soul, not separate substances.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what are the three primary faculties of the soul, in order of nobility?
A) Appetitive, irascible, rational.
B) Irascible, rational, appetitive.
C) Rational, irascible, appetitive.
D) Rational, appetitive, irascible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi classifies the soul's faculties into three—rational, irascible, appetitive—from which all moral traits arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three faculties—rational, irascible, or appetitive—most often drives your daily decisions? Be honest: when you act, do you follow reason, anger, or desire? What does that say about the state of your soul?
?
Al-Razi says all moral traits branch from these three. Reflect on a recent moral failure you had. Which faculty was at its root? What would it take to retrain that faculty?
?
The rational faculty is considered the most noble. But do you actually prioritize reason in your life? When was the last time you let reason override your anger or desire? If you can't remember, what does that reveal about your spiritual priorities?

Classification of the Cognitive Faculty
Al-Razi classifies cognitive faculty into intellect and acquired sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's classification of the cognitive faculty into intellect (root) and acquired sciences (branch), including the divisions of acquired sciences and the possible states of perfection.

Core Idea
The cognitive faculty has two parts: intellect as root, acquired sciences as branch.

Al-Razi begins his analysis of cognitive differences by dividing the cognitive faculty into two fundamental parts. Intellect is the root, while acquired sciences—both rational and practical—form the branch. This classification sets the stage for understanding how people differ in knowledge and practical wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on classification
Read the Arabic text describing the two divisions of the cognitive faculty and the subdivisions of acquired sciences.
Note that intellect varies in strength and weakness, while acquired sciences split into rational/transmitted and practical.
2
Understand the four states
Identify the four possible states: perfect in both sciences, deficient in both, perfect in theoretical but deficient in practical, and the opposite.
3
Reflect on the rarity of perfection
Al-Razi notes that perfection in both is rare. Consider why this might be and how it relates to human temperament.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نشير إلى بعض أنواع الاختلافات الواقعة في كل واحدة من هذه الثلاثة (٢) ونذكر منها ما يصلح أن يكون سبباً مزاجياً. فنقول: أما القوة الإدراكية. فعلى قسمين: أحدهما كالأصل. والثاني كالفرع. وهو العلوم المكتسبة.
أما الأول: وهو العقل، فمراتب الناس فيه مختلفة بالقوة والضعف والكثرة والقلة. وهو ظاهر.
وأما العلوم المكتسبة. فهي قسمان.
أحدهما: العلم بأنواع العلوم العقلية والنقلية.
والثاني: علم بإصلاح أمر المعاش، والإنسان قد يكون كاملاً فيهما وهو نادر، وقد يكون ناقصاً فيهما، وقد يكون كاملاً في تحصيل العلوم، إلا أنه يكون ناقصاً في علم إصلاح المعيشة، وقد يكون بالضد منه.
فأما مراتب العلوم النظرية: فهي كثيرة من وجوه:
الأول: إن الإنسان قد يكون قادراً كاملاً في جميع أنواع العلوم، وهو نادر جداً، وقد يكون مقصراً في الكل، وهو غالب جداً، وقد يكون كاملاً في البعض، ناقصاً في البواقي، وهذا أيضاً مختلف الحال. فقد يكون الإنسان كامل القدرة على تحصيل نوع دقيق غامض من العلوم، وقد يكون عاجزاً عن تحصيل نوع آخر خسيس ضعيف (۱) وقد رأيت أقواماً كانوا أقوياء في المنطق والهندسة، فلما خاضوا في النحو والتصريف ضعفت عقولهم، وتبدلت أذهانهم، بل قد رأيت جماعة كانوا أقوياء في الهندسة وضعفاء في الحساب، مع شدة القرب بينهما.
والنوع الثاني من الاختلاف في هذا الباب: أن فيهم من يكون السريع الحفظ بطيء النسيان. وهذا هو القسم الأشرف. وفيهم من يكون بطيء الحفظ سريع النسيان، وهو القسم الأخس، ومنهم من يكون سريع النسيان، أو بطيء الحفظ بطيء النسيان. وهذان القسمان متوسطان.
والنوع الثالث: الاختلاف الحاصل بحسب الفهم. فإن الإنسان إما أن يفهم الشيء فهماً صحيحاً. وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. أما الذي يفهم فهماً صحيحاً. فيعتبر حاله من وجهين:
أحدهما: أن هذا الفاهم قد يقدر على الاستنباط، وقد لا يقدر. والقادر على الاستنباط قد يقدر عليه في الأشياء العالية، وقد يقدر عليه في الفروع الخسيسة الضعيفة.
وثانيهما: أن الإنسان قد يكون بحيث إذا علمته مسألة تعلمها سريعاً، إلا أن تلك المسألة التي تعلمها، إذا اختلطت بالكلمات الأجنبية، وامتزجت بالمقدمات التي لا تناسبها، فإن ذلك الإنسان لا يعرف أن هذه المسألة هي تلك المسألة التي تعلمها وأحكمها، وقد يكون بحيث إذا تعلم مقدمة وعرفها، ثم اختلطت تلك المقدمة بألف مقدمة أجنبية غريبة عنها، فإنه في الحال يعرفها، ويعلم أنها هي تلك المقدمة التي عرفها أولاً، وكنت أسمي القسم الأول بالخاطر الضعيف، والثاني بالخاطر القوي.
And we will point to some types of differences occurring in each one of these three (2) and mention from them what is suitable as a temperamental cause. We say: As for the cognitive faculty (al-quwwa al-idrakiyya), it is of two divisions: one is like the root, and the second like the branch, which is the acquired sciences. As for the first—the intellect—people's ranks in it differ in strength and weakness and abundance and scarcity, and that is apparent. As for the acquired sciences, they are two divisions: one is knowledge of the types of rational and transmitted sciences, and the second is knowledge of managing the livelihood. Man may be perfect in both—which is rare—or deficient in both, or perfect in acquiring the sciences but deficient in the science of managing livelihood, or the opposite. As for the ranks of the theoretical sciences, they are many in respects: First: man may be fully capable in all types of sciences—very rare—or deficient in all—very common—or perfect in some, deficient in others, and this also varies. He may be fully capable of acquiring a precise, subtle type of science, or incapable of acquiring another base, weak type (1). And I have seen people who were strong in logic and geometry, but when they delved into grammar and morphology their intellects weakened and their minds changed. Indeed, I have seen a group who were strong in geometry and weak in arithmetic, despite the strong closeness between them. The second type of difference in this chapter: among them are those who are quick to memorize and slow to forget—this is the most noble division. Among them are those who are slow to memorize and quick to forget—this is the lowest division. And among them are those who are quick to forget, or slow to memorize and slow to forget; these two divisions are intermediate. The third type: the difference arising according to understanding. Man either understands a thing correctly or does not. As for one who understands correctly, his condition is considered from two aspects: one: this understander may be capable of deduction or not. The one capable of deduction may be capable in lofty things or in lowly, weak branches. The second: man may be such that if you teach him a question he learns it quickly, but if that question becomes mixed with extraneous words and mingled with premises that do not suit it, he does not recognize that this question is the one he learned and mastered. And he may be such that if he learns a premise and knows it, then that premise becomes mixed with a thousand extraneous premises foreign to it, he immediately recognizes it and knows that it is the premise he knew first. And I used to call the first division the weak intuition (al-khatir al-da'if) and the second the strong intuition (al-khatir al-qawi).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the differences in the cognitive faculty (al-quwwa al-idrakiyya). Al-Razi begins by stating that he will indicate some types of differences in each of the three faculties (rational, irascible, appetitive) but focuses on temperamental causes. He then turns to the cognitive faculty and divides it into two: the intellect (al-'aql) as the root, and the acquired sciences (al-'ulum al-muktasaba) as the branch. Intellect varies in strength, weakness, abundance, and scarcity. Acquired sciences are further divided into rational and transmitted sciences, and practical sciences for managing livelihood. He notes four possible states of perfection regarding these. Then he discusses theoretical sciences in three types of differences (wujuh). The first type: people vary in their ability to master all sciences (rare), be deficient in all (common), or be perfect in some and deficient in others, with further variation even between close fields like geometry and arithmetic. The second type concerns memory: four categories based on speed of memorization and forgetting, with the best being quick to memorize and slow to forget. The third type concerns understanding: correct understanding varies by ability to deduce and by the strength of intuition (khatir) in recognizing a learned idea amidst extraneous material. Al-Razi distinguishes two kinds of intuition: weak and strong. This passage provides a nuanced psychology of learning.

Key Takeaway
The cognitive faculty has two components: intellect (root) and acquired sciences (branch), and people vary in both across rational and practical domains.

Differences in Mastering Sciences
Al-Razi describes the first type of difference in theoretical sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of difference in theoretical sciences: people's varying ability to master all, some, or none of the sciences, and how even close fields can be mastered differently.

Core Idea
People differ greatly in their capacity to master different sciences, even closely related ones.

Al-Razi now delves into the theoretical sciences, identifying the first type of difference: people can be perfect in all sciences (very rare), deficient in all (common), or perfect in some and deficient in others. He illustrates with his own observations of individuals excelling in logic and geometry but struggling in grammar, or strong in geometry but weak in arithmetic.

Guided Path
1
Read about the first type of difference
Read the Arabic passage describing the three categories of people regarding mastery of theoretical sciences.
2
Notice the rarity and commonality
Al-Razi emphasizes that being perfect in all sciences is very rare, while being deficient in all is very common.
3
Consider the examples
Reflect on the examples of people strong in logic and geometry but weak in grammar and morphology, or strong in geometry but weak in arithmetic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نشير إلى بعض أنواع الاختلافات الواقعة في كل واحدة من هذه الثلاثة (٢) ونذكر منها ما يصلح أن يكون سبباً مزاجياً. فنقول: أما القوة الإدراكية. فعلى قسمين: أحدهما كالأصل. والثاني كالفرع. وهو العلوم المكتسبة.
أما الأول: وهو العقل، فمراتب الناس فيه مختلفة بالقوة والضعف والكثرة والقلة. وهو ظاهر.
وأما العلوم المكتسبة. فهي قسمان.
أحدهما: العلم بأنواع العلوم العقلية والنقلية.
والثاني: علم بإصلاح أمر المعاش، والإنسان قد يكون كاملاً فيهما وهو نادر، وقد يكون ناقصاً فيهما، وقد يكون كاملاً في تحصيل العلوم، إلا أنه يكون ناقصاً في علم إصلاح المعيشة، وقد يكون بالضد منه.
فأما مراتب العلوم النظرية: فهي كثيرة من وجوه:
الأول: إن الإنسان قد يكون قادراً كاملاً في جميع أنواع العلوم، وهو نادر جداً، وقد يكون مقصراً في الكل، وهو غالب جداً، وقد يكون كاملاً في البعض، ناقصاً في البواقي، وهذا أيضاً مختلف الحال. فقد يكون الإنسان كامل القدرة على تحصيل نوع دقيق غامض من العلوم، وقد يكون عاجزاً عن تحصيل نوع آخر خسيس ضعيف (۱) وقد رأيت أقواماً كانوا أقوياء في المنطق والهندسة، فلما خاضوا في النحو والتصريف ضعفت عقولهم، وتبدلت أذهانهم، بل قد رأيت جماعة كانوا أقوياء في الهندسة وضعفاء في الحساب، مع شدة القرب بينهما.
والنوع الثاني من الاختلاف في هذا الباب: أن فيهم من يكون السريع الحفظ بطيء النسيان. وهذا هو القسم الأشرف. وفيهم من يكون بطيء الحفظ سريع النسيان، وهو القسم الأخس، ومنهم من يكون سريع النسيان، أو بطيء الحفظ بطيء النسيان. وهذان القسمان متوسطان.
والنوع الثالث: الاختلاف الحاصل بحسب الفهم. فإن الإنسان إما أن يفهم الشيء فهماً صحيحاً. وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. أما الذي يفهم فهماً صحيحاً. فيعتبر حاله من وجهين:
أحدهما: أن هذا الفاهم قد يقدر على الاستنباط، وقد لا يقدر. والقادر على الاستنباط قد يقدر عليه في الأشياء العالية، وقد يقدر عليه في الفروع الخسيسة الضعيفة.
وثانيهما: أن الإنسان قد يكون بحيث إذا علمته مسألة تعلمها سريعاً، إلا أن تلك المسألة التي تعلمها، إذا اختلطت بالكلمات الأجنبية، وامتزجت بالمقدمات التي لا تناسبها، فإن ذلك الإنسان لا يعرف أن هذه المسألة هي تلك المسألة التي تعلمها وأحكمها، وقد يكون بحيث إذا تعلم مقدمة وعرفها، ثم اختلطت تلك المقدمة بألف مقدمة أجنبية غريبة عنها، فإنه في الحال يعرفها، ويعلم أنها هي تلك المقدمة التي عرفها أولاً، وكنت أسمي القسم الأول بالخاطر الضعيف، والثاني بالخاطر القوي.
And we will point to some types of differences occurring in each one of these three (2) and mention from them what is suitable as a temperamental cause. We say: As for the cognitive faculty (al-quwwa al-idrakiyya), it is of two divisions: one is like the root, and the second like the branch, which is the acquired sciences. As for the first—the intellect—people's ranks in it differ in strength and weakness and abundance and scarcity, and that is apparent. As for the acquired sciences, they are two divisions: one is knowledge of the types of rational and transmitted sciences, and the second is knowledge of managing the livelihood. Man may be perfect in both—which is rare—or deficient in both, or perfect in acquiring the sciences but deficient in the science of managing livelihood, or the opposite. As for the ranks of the theoretical sciences, they are many in respects: First: man may be fully capable in all types of sciences—very rare—or deficient in all—very common—or perfect in some, deficient in others, and this also varies. He may be fully capable of acquiring a precise, subtle type of science, or incapable of acquiring another base, weak type (1). And I have seen people who were strong in logic and geometry, but when they delved into grammar and morphology their intellects weakened and their minds changed. Indeed, I have seen a group who were strong in geometry and weak in arithmetic, despite the strong closeness between them. The second type of difference in this chapter: among them are those who are quick to memorize and slow to forget—this is the most noble division. Among them are those who are slow to memorize and quick to forget—this is the lowest division. And among them are those who are quick to forget, or slow to memorize and slow to forget; these two divisions are intermediate. The third type: the difference arising according to understanding. Man either understands a thing correctly or does not. As for one who understands correctly, his condition is considered from two aspects: one: this understander may be capable of deduction or not. The one capable of deduction may be capable in lofty things or in lowly, weak branches. The second: man may be such that if you teach him a question he learns it quickly, but if that question becomes mixed with extraneous words and mingled with premises that do not suit it, he does not recognize that this question is the one he learned and mastered. And he may be such that if he learns a premise and knows it, then that premise becomes mixed with a thousand extraneous premises foreign to it, he immediately recognizes it and knows that it is the premise he knew first. And I used to call the first division the weak intuition (al-khatir al-da'if) and the second the strong intuition (al-khatir al-qawi).

Differences in Memory: Four Categories
Al-Razi classifies memory differences into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's classification of people based on memory: four categories combining speed of memorization and retention.

Core Idea
Memory differences yield four types, with the best being quick to memorize and slow to forget.

Al-Razi turns to the second type of difference in theoretical knowledge: memory. He identifies four categories based on the speed of memorization (quick/slow) and the speed of forgetting (quick/slow). The most noble is quick memorization with slow forgetting; the lowest is slow memorization with quick forgetting; the intermediate categories are quick forgetfulness or slow memorization combined with slow forgetting.

Guided Path
1
Read the memory classification
Read the Arabic passage listing the four types and their ranking.
2
Identify the noble and base
Note which combination al-Razi calls the most noble (ashraf) and which the lowest (akhass).
3
Understand the intermediate types
The two intermediate types: quick to forget (regardless of memorization speed?) and slow to memorize with slow to forget.

Source Text

Differences in Understanding and Intuition
Al-Razi analyzes differences in understanding and introduces concepts of strong and weak intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's analysis of differences in correct understanding, including deduction ability and the distinction between weak and strong intuition (khatir).

Core Idea
Correct understanding varies in deduction ability and in recognizing ideas when mixed with extraneous material.

Al-Razi discusses the third type of difference: understanding (al-fahm). He first distinguishes correct understanding from incorrect. Then, for those who understand correctly, he considers two aspects: their ability to derive (istinbat) and their capacity to recognize a learned idea when it is mixed with extraneous words. This leads to the concepts of weak and strong intuition (khatir).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on understanding
Read the Arabic text from 'والنوع الثالث' to the end, noting the distinctions.
2
Understand the two aspects
First aspect: ability to deduce, and whether in lofty or lowly matters. Second aspect: ability to recognize a learned premise when mixed with foreign material.
3
Distinguish weak and strong intuition
Al-Razi coined terms: weak intuition (al-khatir al-da'if) for those who fail to recognize mixed material, and strong intuition (al-khatir al-qawi) for those who succeed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نشير إلى بعض أنواع الاختلافات الواقعة في كل واحدة من هذه الثلاثة (٢) ونذكر منها ما يصلح أن يكون سبباً مزاجياً. فنقول: أما القوة الإدراكية. فعلى قسمين: أحدهما كالأصل. والثاني كالفرع. وهو العلوم المكتسبة.
أما الأول: وهو العقل، فمراتب الناس فيه مختلفة بالقوة والضعف والكثرة والقلة. وهو ظاهر.
وأما العلوم المكتسبة. فهي قسمان.
أحدهما: العلم بأنواع العلوم العقلية والنقلية.
والثاني: علم بإصلاح أمر المعاش، والإنسان قد يكون كاملاً فيهما وهو نادر، وقد يكون ناقصاً فيهما، وقد يكون كاملاً في تحصيل العلوم، إلا أنه يكون ناقصاً في علم إصلاح المعيشة، وقد يكون بالضد منه.
فأما مراتب العلوم النظرية: فهي كثيرة من وجوه:
الأول: إن الإنسان قد يكون قادراً كاملاً في جميع أنواع العلوم، وهو نادر جداً، وقد يكون مقصراً في الكل، وهو غالب جداً، وقد يكون كاملاً في البعض، ناقصاً في البواقي، وهذا أيضاً مختلف الحال. فقد يكون الإنسان كامل القدرة على تحصيل نوع دقيق غامض من العلوم، وقد يكون عاجزاً عن تحصيل نوع آخر خسيس ضعيف (۱) وقد رأيت أقواماً كانوا أقوياء في المنطق والهندسة، فلما خاضوا في النحو والتصريف ضعفت عقولهم، وتبدلت أذهانهم، بل قد رأيت جماعة كانوا أقوياء في الهندسة وضعفاء في الحساب، مع شدة القرب بينهما.
والنوع الثاني من الاختلاف في هذا الباب: أن فيهم من يكون السريع الحفظ بطيء النسيان. وهذا هو القسم الأشرف. وفيهم من يكون بطيء الحفظ سريع النسيان، وهو القسم الأخس، ومنهم من يكون سريع النسيان، أو بطيء الحفظ بطيء النسيان. وهذان القسمان متوسطان.
والنوع الثالث: الاختلاف الحاصل بحسب الفهم. فإن الإنسان إما أن يفهم الشيء فهماً صحيحاً. وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. أما الذي يفهم فهماً صحيحاً. فيعتبر حاله من وجهين:
أحدهما: أن هذا الفاهم قد يقدر على الاستنباط، وقد لا يقدر. والقادر على الاستنباط قد يقدر عليه في الأشياء العالية، وقد يقدر عليه في الفروع الخسيسة الضعيفة.
وثانيهما: أن الإنسان قد يكون بحيث إذا علمته مسألة تعلمها سريعاً، إلا أن تلك المسألة التي تعلمها، إذا اختلطت بالكلمات الأجنبية، وامتزجت بالمقدمات التي لا تناسبها، فإن ذلك الإنسان لا يعرف أن هذه المسألة هي تلك المسألة التي تعلمها وأحكمها، وقد يكون بحيث إذا تعلم مقدمة وعرفها، ثم اختلطت تلك المقدمة بألف مقدمة أجنبية غريبة عنها، فإنه في الحال يعرفها، ويعلم أنها هي تلك المقدمة التي عرفها أولاً، وكنت أسمي القسم الأول بالخاطر الضعيف، والثاني بالخاطر القوي.
And we will point to some types of differences occurring in each one of these three (2) and mention from them what is suitable as a temperamental cause. We say: As for the cognitive faculty (al-quwwa al-idrakiyya), it is of two divisions: one is like the root, and the second like the branch, which is the acquired sciences. As for the first—the intellect—people's ranks in it differ in strength and weakness and abundance and scarcity, and that is apparent. As for the acquired sciences, they are two divisions: one is knowledge of the types of rational and transmitted sciences, and the second is knowledge of managing the livelihood. Man may be perfect in both—which is rare—or deficient in both, or perfect in acquiring the sciences but deficient in the science of managing livelihood, or the opposite. As for the ranks of the theoretical sciences, they are many in respects: First: man may be fully capable in all types of sciences—very rare—or deficient in all—very common—or perfect in some, deficient in others, and this also varies. He may be fully capable of acquiring a precise, subtle type of science, or incapable of acquiring another base, weak type (1). And I have seen people who were strong in logic and geometry, but when they delved into grammar and morphology their intellects weakened and their minds changed. Indeed, I have seen a group who were strong in geometry and weak in arithmetic, despite the strong closeness between them. The second type of difference in this chapter: among them are those who are quick to memorize and slow to forget—this is the most noble division. Among them are those who are slow to memorize and quick to forget—this is the lowest division. And among them are those who are quick to forget, or slow to memorize and slow to forget; these two divisions are intermediate. The third type: the difference arising according to understanding. Man either understands a thing correctly or does not. As for one who understands correctly, his condition is considered from two aspects: one: this understander may be capable of deduction or not. The one capable of deduction may be capable in lofty things or in lowly, weak branches. The second: man may be such that if you teach him a question he learns it quickly, but if that question becomes mixed with extraneous words and mingled with premises that do not suit it, he does not recognize that this question is the one he learned and mastered. And he may be such that if he learns a premise and knows it, then that premise becomes mixed with a thousand extraneous premises foreign to it, he immediately recognizes it and knows that it is the premise he knew first. And I used to call the first division the weak intuition (al-khatir al-da'if) and the second the strong intuition (al-khatir al-qawi).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the differences in the cognitive faculty (al-quwwa al-idrakiyya). Al-Razi begins by stating that he will indicate some types of differences in each of the three faculties (rational, irascible, appetitive) but focuses on temperamental causes. He then turns to the cognitive faculty and divides it into two: the intellect (al-'aql) as the root, and the acquired sciences (al-'ulum al-muktasaba) as the branch. Intellect varies in strength, weakness, abundance, and scarcity. Acquired sciences are further divided into rational and transmitted sciences, and practical sciences for managing livelihood. He notes four possible states of perfection regarding these. Then he discusses theoretical sciences in three types of differences (wujuh). The first type: people vary in their ability to master all sciences (rare), be deficient in all (common), or be perfect in some and deficient in others, with further variation even between close fields like geometry and arithmetic. The second type concerns memory: four categories based on speed of memorization and forgetting, with the best being quick to memorize and slow to forget. The third type concerns understanding: correct understanding varies by ability to deduce and by the strength of intuition (khatir) in recognizing a learned idea amidst extraneous material. Al-Razi distinguishes two kinds of intuition: weak and strong. This passage provides a nuanced psychology of learning.

Key Terms
الفهم
الاستنباط
الخاطر القوي
الخاطر الضعيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Understanding is a single ability; if you understand something, you can always recognize it.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even correct understanding varies: one may fail to recognize a learned idea when it is mixed with unrelated material.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi call the ability to recognize a learned premise even when mixed with a thousand foreign premises?
A) Weak intuition (al-khatir al-da'if)
B) Strong intuition (al-khatir al-qawi)
C) Correct understanding
D) Deduction (al-istinbat)

Key Takeaway
Understanding varies not only in the ability to learn and deduce, but also in the ability to recognize ideas when they are contextually mixed with other material.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an argument that seems to challenge your faith, do you panic and feel lost, or can you see beneath the confusion and recognize the truth you already know? Al-Razi calls the first weak intuition and the second strong. Which describes you most honestly?
?
You can memorize an ayah quickly, but can you still hold onto its meaning when someone surrounds it with doubts or combines it with false premises? How will you train yourself to have strong intuition?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between those who can deduce in lofty matters and those only in lowly ones. In your own religious learning, do you aim for surface-level answers or do you push yourself to derive deeper insights? What one step will you take this week to strengthen your deduction ability?

The Three Brain Cavities and Their Ideal Temperaments
Describes the three brain cavities and their ideal temperaments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars correlated the three brain cavities (imagination, thinking, memory) with specific temperaments for optimal function.

Core Idea
Each brain cavity has a distinct ideal temperament for optimal cognition.

This passage from al-Matalib al-‘Aliyah presents a medieval Islamic physiological theory that links mental faculties to physical temperaments in the brain. It identifies three brain cavities responsible for imagination, thinking, and memory, and explains the ideal balance of moisture, dryness, heat, and cold for each. Understanding this helps us appreciate how early scholars integrated philosophy and medicine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cavities
Read the passage and note that it names three brain cavities: anterior for imagination, middle for thinking, posterior for memory.
2
Learn the temperament for imagination
Focus on the first cavity: it must be moist for easy impression, but not too moist or too dry. Moderate moisture yields good imagination and quick learning.
Childhood is best for learning due to moist brain spirits.
3
Learn the temperament for thinking
The middle cavity must be hot because thinking involves movement. Excessive heat causes confusion; deficient heat causes dullness; moderate heat gives straight thought and quick deduction.
4
Learn the temperament for memory
The posterior cavity must be dry for retention. If dryness is lacking, memory weakens and forgetfulness prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التذكر . فالأول : موضع التخيل . والثاني : موضع التفكر . والثالث : موضع . فالتجويف الأول يجب أن يكون مائلاً إلى الرطوبة ، لتنطبع فيها صور الأشياء بسهولة ، فإن كان هذا البطن ، عظيم الرطوبة ، ضعفت تخيلاته ، لأن الرطب يكون سريع الأخذ ، سريع الترك. وإن كان مائلاً إلى اليبس ، ضعفت أيضاً هذه التخيلات، لأن اليابس يطيء الأخذ ، بطيء الترك . أما إذا كان معتدلاً ، كان جيد التخيل ، سريع التعلم لما يسمعه ويقرؤه ، وتورده عليه حواسه . ولذلك صار التعليم في وقت الصبا أحسن من غيره ، لأن في وقت الصبا تكون الأرواح الدماغية رطبة فيسهل قبولها لتلك الصور . ثم كل ما ازداد السن ، ازداد اليبس المانع من زوال الصور . وأما الأرواح الحاصلة في التجويف الأوسط ، فيجب أن تكون مائلة إلى الحرارة ، لأن الفكر إنما يتأتى بإلحاق شيء بشيء وذلك نوع حركة . والحركة إنما تتم بالحرارة ، فالفكر لا يتم إلا بالحرارة . ثم تقول : إن كانت هذه الحرارة كثيرة ، كان ذلك الروح شديد الالتهاب، فكانت تلك الأفكار مشوشة وإن كانت تلك الحرارة ناقصة قليلة ، كان الفكر فى النقصان أو البطلان ، وكان صاحبه بليداً ، غليظ الطبع . وأما إن كان معتدلاً في الحرارة والبرودة ، كان صاحبه مستقيم الفكر ، سريع الجواب ، حسن الاستنباط . وأما الأرواح الحاصلة في التجويف المؤخر ، فيجب أن تكون مائلة إلى اليبس ، لأن الحفظ لا يتم إلا باليبس . فإن قلت هذه الصفة ضعف الحفظ ، وغلب النسيان
Memory. The first is the place of imagination. The second is the place of thinking. The third is the place. The first cavity must incline towards moisture, so that images of things are impressed in it easily. If this ventricle is very moist, its imaginations are weak, because moisture takes quickly and leaves quickly. If it inclines towards dryness, these imaginations are also weak, because dryness takes slowly and leaves slowly. But if it is moderate, it is good in imagination, quick in learning what it hears, reads, and the senses bring to it. Therefore, education in childhood is better than others, because in childhood the brain spirits are moist, so they easily accept those images. Then as age increases, dryness increases, preventing the removal of images. As for the spirits in the middle cavity, they must incline towards heat, because thinking occurs by attaching one thing to another, which is a kind of movement. Movement occurs through heat, so thinking only occurs through heat. Then you say: if this heat is excessive, that spirit is intensely inflamed, and those thoughts are confused. If that heat is deficient and little, thought is in deficiency or nullity, and its possessor is dull and coarse-natured. But if it is moderate in heat and cold, its possessor is straight in thought, quick in response, good in deduction. As for the spirits in the posterior cavity, they must incline towards dryness, because retention only occurs through dryness. If this quality is diminished, retention weakens and forgetfulness prevails.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage describes the three brain cavities (ventricles) and the ideal temperament for each. It begins by identifying the first cavity as the place of imagination (التخيل), the second as the place of thinking (التفكر), and the third as the place of memory (التذكر). Then it explains that the first cavity should be moist so that images are easily impressed. If it is too moist, imaginations are weak; if too dry, they are also weak; only when moderate does it produce good imagination and quick learning. It notes that childhood is best for learning because the brain spirits are moist. The middle cavity should be hot because thinking involves movement, which requires heat. Excessive heat causes confusion; deficient heat causes dullness; moderate heat produces straight thought and quick deduction. The posterior cavity should be dry because retention (memory) requires dryness; if dryness is lacking, memory weakens and forgetfulness dominates. The passage presents a physiological explanation for differences in mental faculties based on the temperament of each brain cavity.

Correction: The author grounds each mental faculty in a specific brain cavity with a particular temperament, showing the integration of physical and mental.

Key Takeaway

The middle cavity requires moderate heat for clear thinking. How does your lifestyle—diet, sleep, anger—affect the temperature of your thoughts? Where do you need to cool down or warm up to think straight?
?
If the posterior cavity's dryness is essential for retention, what are you filling your mind with that you want to retain for the hereafter? Are you actively choosing to store good knowledge rather than letting forgetfulness prevail?

The Worm-Like Body and Mental Quickness
Explains how a worm-like body in the brain canal affects the flow of imagination to thought

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a worm-like body in the brain canal regulates the flow of imagination to thought, affecting a person's quickness of understanding.

Core Idea
A worm-like body in the brain controls the speed of thought.

This passage explains the second cause of differences in mental faculties: a worm-like anatomical structure in the brain that acts like a valve. When it extends, it opens a canal allowing spirit and images to pass to the thinking power; when it contracts, it blocks. The temperament of the brain influences its movement, leading to variations in quickness of understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's description of the mechanism sink in.
The worm-like body is not a literal worm but an anatomical structure.
2
Identify the two states
Notice that the worm-like body has two states: extension (becoming thin) and contraction (becoming thick). Each state affects the canal differently.
Extension opens the canal; contraction blocks it.
3
Trace the effect on spirit and images
When the canal opens, the spirit carries imagined images to the thinking power. When blocked, the images cannot reach the thinking power.
This connects the mechanical action to mental function.
4
Understand the role of temperament
The speed of the body's movement depends on the brain's temperament. A thick, cold substance leads to slow movement; a quick, sharp substance leads to fast movement.
This explains individual differences in mental quickness.
5
Contemplate the implications
Reflect on how this physiological model explains why some people are slow or quick in understanding and response.
This is a cause-based explanation from classical Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسبب الثاني : إنه قد يحصل في المجرى الذي بين التجويف المقدم ، إلى التجويف المتوسط : جسم شبيه بالدودة ، وله قوة أن يمتد تارة ويقصر أخرى . فإذا امتد ودق ، انفتح المجرى الذي بين التجويف المقدم وبين التجويف المتوسط ، فنفذت الروح من التجويف المقدم إلى التجويف المتوسط ، وتأدت الصور المتخيلة إلى القوة الفكرية ، وأما إذا انقبض هذا الجسم الشبيه بالدودة ، فحينئذ يقصر ويغلظ ويسد المجرى ، فلم تنفذ الروح من التجويف المقدم إلى التجويف المتوسط ، فحينئذ يمتنع وصول الصور الخيالية إلى القوة المفكرة . إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : حركة هذا الجسم الشبيه بالدودة في الامتداد ، والانقباض : مختلفة في الناس ، على قدر أمزجة الأدمغة . فإن كان جوهره غليظاً بارداً ، كانت حركته بطيئة فلم ينفتح المجرى بسرعة ، فلم تتأد الروح من التجويف المقدم إلى التجويف المتوسط بسرعة ، فيكون هذا الإنسان بطيء الفهم قليل الفطنة متأخر الجواب . وإن كان هذا الجسم سريع الحركة حادها ، كان هذا الإنسان سريع الفطنة ، سريع الجواب ، حاد الفهم
The second cause: there may occur in the canal between the anterior cavity and the middle cavity a worm-like body, which has the power to extend sometimes and contract another. When it extends and becomes thin, the canal between the anterior and middle cavities opens, and the spirit passes from the anterior cavity to the middle cavity, and the imagined images are conveyed to the thinking power. But when this worm-like body contracts, then it shortens and thickens and blocks the canal, so the spirit does not pass from the anterior cavity to the middle cavity, and then the arrival of the imaginary images to the thinking power is prevented. If you know this, we say: the movement of this worm-like body in extension and contraction varies among people according to the temperaments of the brains. If its substance is thick and cold, its movement is slow, so the canal does not open quickly, and the spirit does not pass from the anterior cavity to the middle cavity quickly, so this person is slow in understanding, little in intelligence, delayed in response. If this body is quick and sharp in movement, this person is quick in intelligence, quick in response, sharp in understanding.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the second cause for differences in mental faculties: a worm-like body in the canal between the anterior (imagination) and middle (thinking) cavities. The author explains that this body can extend (become thin) or contract (become thick). When it extends, the canal opens, allowing the spirit (الروح) to carry imagined images (الصور المتخيلة) to the thinking power (القوة الفكرية). When it contracts, it blocks the canal, preventing images from reaching the intellect. The speed and quality of this movement depend on the brain's temperament. If the body is thick and cold, movement is slow, resulting in slow understanding and slow responses. If it is quick and sharp, the person is quick-witted and sharp in understanding. This is a physiological explanation for variation in intelligence and quickness of mind.

Key Terms
جسم شبيه بالدودة
مجرى
امتداد
انقباض
روح
قوة فكرية
أمزجة الأدمغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The worm-like body is an actual worm living in the brain.
Correction: It is an anatomical structure described metaphorically as worm-like due to its shape and movement.

Checkpoint
What happens when the worm-like body contracts?
A) The canal opens and spirit flows
B) The canal closes and imagination is blocked from thinking
C) The spirit becomes hot
D) Memory improves

Key Takeaway
The speed of mental processing depends on the movement of a worm-like body in the brain canal, which is influenced by brain temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

After learning that your mental quickness may be determined by the temperament of a worm-like body in your brain, can you truly accept that this is part of Allah's creation and not a moral failing? The Skull Shape and Its Effect on Imagination and Memory
Explains how the shape of the skull affects imagination and memory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how medieval physiology linked skull morphology to mental faculties, specifically the front protrusion for imagination and the back protrusion for memory.

Core Idea
The shape of your skull reveals the strength of your imagination and memory.

In this passage, the author discusses the third cause among the brain causes: the shape of the skull. He describes the ideal shape using an analogy of a pressed sphere, and correlates the front and back protrusions with imagination and memory respectively. Let's read the original text and explore this medieval physiological theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through without pausing. Then read the English translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the analogy of a sphere pressed from the sides.
2
Identify the ideal skull shape
Find the description of the good shape: a sphere with protrusions front and back, flat on the sides.
3
Correlate protrusions with faculties
The passage states: front protrusion for imagination (تخيل), back protrusion for memory (تذكر). Note the consequences when each is missing.
4
Reflect on the theory
Think about how this theory attempts to explain individual differences in mental abilities through physical traits. Compare to modern neuroscience.
This is not phrenology; it's a medieval theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب الثالث من الأسباب الدماغية : شكل قحف الرأس ، فإن الشكل الجيد أن يكون القحف شبيهاً بكرة غمز عليها من الجانبين ، حتى يظهر نتوء في القدام وفي الخلف ، ولطاء من الجانبين. فهذا الشكل موافق لحال الدماغ فأما إذا فقد نتوء القدام ، ضعف التخيل . وإن فقد نتوء الخلف ضعف التذكر ، وإن فقد النتوء ان جميعاً ، فقد التخيل والتذكر
The third cause among the brain causes: the shape of the skull. The good shape is that the skull is like a sphere pressed from the sides, so that a protrusion appears in the front and in the back, and flatness on the sides. So this shape is suitable for the state of the brain. As for if the front protrusion is lost, imagination weakens. If the back protrusion is lost, memory weakens. If both protrusions are lost, imagination and memory are lost.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage discusses the third cause: the shape of the skull (شكل قحف الرأس). The ideal shape is compared to a sphere that has been pressed from the sides, resulting in a protrusion (نتوء) in the front and back, and flatness (لطاء) on the sides. This shape is appropriate for the brain. The author then correlates the protrusions with faculties: the front protrusion is associated with imagination (التخيل), and the back protrusion with memory (التذكر). If the front protrusion is missing, imagination weakens; if the back is missing, memory weakens; if both are missing, both faculties are lost. This provides a physical basis for variation in these mental faculties based on cranial morphology.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage describes phrenology.
Correction: This is a medieval physiological theory linking skull shape to mental faculties, not the 19th-century pseudoscience of phrenology which mapped personality traits to bumps on the skull.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

?

The Fourth Cause: Head and Body Size
Enumerates four combinations of head and body size and their effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relative sizes of the head and body affect mental and physical strength according to this classical theory.

Core Idea
Proper proportion between head and body leads to strength and perfection.

This passage examines the fourth cause influencing strength and weakness: the sizes of the head and body. It considers four possible combinations and their effects on the heart and brain, linking physical proportion to functional perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice the four cases the author lists.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the cases presented.
2
Identify the four combinations
List the four possible relative sizes of head and body mentioned: both large, both small, head small body large, and the opposite (head large body small).
The fourth case is not elaborated but implied.
3
Understand the effects on heart and brain
For each case, note what the author says about the strength of the heart (قلب) and brain (دماغ).
The author correlates physical size with strength of faculties.
4
Reflect on the meaning of 'perfection'
Consider that 'perfection' (كمال) here refers to physical strength and balance, not moral virtue.
This avoids a common misunderstanding.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب الرابع : الرأس والبدن . إما أن يكونا كبيرين أو صغيرين ، أو يكون الرأس صغيراً والبدن كبيراً ، أو بالعكس . أما الأول وهو أن يكون الرأس والبدن كبيرين ، فحينئذ يكون القلب قوياً والدماغ قوياً . وههنا يحصل الكمال وأما إن كانا صغيرين فههنا يحصل النقصان ، وأما إن كان الرأس صغيراً والبدن كبيراً ، فحينئذ يكون القلب قوياً حاداً ويكون الدماغ صغيراً ، لا يقوى
The fourth cause: the head and body. Either both are large or both small, or the head is small and the body large, or the opposite. As for the first, that the head and body are large, then the heart is strong and the brain is strong. Here perfection is obtained. As for if both are small, then deficiency occurs. As for if the head is small and the body large, then the heart is strong and sharp, and the brain is small, not strong.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth cause: the relative sizes of the head and body. It considers four possibilities: (1) both large: the heart (القلب) and brain (الدماغ) are strong, resulting in perfection (الكمال). (2) both small: deficiency (النقصان) occurs. (3) head small and body large: the heart is strong and sharp, but the brain is small and not strong (لا يقوى). The opposite case (head large, body small) is mentioned but not elaborated, possibly due to the text being cut off. The author correlates physical size with strength of the heart and brain, implying that proper proportion between head and body is important for optimal faculties.

First Argument: Heart as First Organ through Sperm Composition
Sanusi presents the first analogical argument for the heart being the first organ formed, based on sperm composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogical reasoning Sanusi uses to argue that the heart is the first organ to form, based on the elemental composition of semen.

Core Idea
The heart forms first because it arises from the subtle elements at the center of the sperm sphere.

Sanusi presents the first of two analogical arguments for the heart being the first organ. This argument draws on the elemental theory of the four natures (fire, air, water, earth) and observations of semen. By understanding how the subtle parts aggregate and become the heart, we see how divine wisdom organizes the body. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the entire passage in Arabic, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: it starts with a claim, then gives evidence, then explains the process.
Pay attention to the key terms in Arabic.
2
Identify the main claim and evidence
The claim is that the heart is the first organ. The evidence is based on the composition of semen. What is the key observation about semen's whiteness? How does cold affect it?
This establishes that fiery and airy elements dominate.
3
Understand the aggregation process
Sanusi explains that similar parts attract each other. Why do the subtle parts go to the center? Why do the dense parts surround them? Connect this to divine wisdom.
Think about why the subtle parts are placed in the middle.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The center of the sphere becomes the heart. How does this argument lead to the anatomists' statement? Reflect on the overall reasoning.
This argument is one of two aspects; the next lesson will cover the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن أول عضو يتخلق من البدن ، هو القلب وأما القياس فمن وجهين الأول : إن المني جسم مركب من الطبائع الأربعة ، والهوائية والنارية غالبة عليه . والدليل عليه : أن بياض الرطوبات ، إنما يحصل بسبب اختلاط الأجزاء الهوائية بها ، كما يكون في البرد ، فوجب أن يكون بياض المني لهذا السبب . ويتأكد ما ذكرناه : بأن المني إذا ضر به البرد رق ، وزال بیاضه ، مع أن البرد أولى بالتكثيف . وذلك يدل على أن بياضه ، إنما كان لأجل أنه اختلط به أجزاء كثيرة من الأجزاء النارية والهوائية ، فلما ضر به البرد ، فارقته تلك الأجزاء النارية والهوائية ، فزال بياضه . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه تعالى قدر بحكمته : أن الأجزاء الأرضية والمائية الموجودة في المني ، تصير مادة للأعضاء ، وأما الأجزاء الهوائية والنارية التي في المني ، فإنه تعالى يجعلها مادة للأرواح الإنسانية إلا أن تلك الأجزاء الكثيفة واللطيفة تكون مختلطة بعضها بالبعض في أول الأمر ، لكن الجنسية علة الضم ، فلا جرم تنضم الأجزاء اللطيفة بعضها إلى البعض ، والأجزاء الكثيفة بعضها إلى البعض. ولما كان اللطيف سريع التحلل ، اقتضت الحكمة الإلمية جعل تلك الأجزاء اللطيفة في وسط ذلك الجسم ، وجعل تلك الأجزاء الكثيفة محيطة بها ، وصوناً لها . وعند هذا يصير جرم المني كالكرة المستديرة ، ويكون باطن تلك الكرة مملوءاً من تلك الأجزاء اللطيفة الهوائية والنارية ، ويكون ظاهرها متولداً من تلك الأجزاء الكثيفة . وذلك الموضع الذي صار موضعاً لتلك الأجزاء اللطيفة ، هو الموضع الذي إذا استحكم ، صار قلباً . فلهذا السبب قال أهل التشريح : ( أول الأعضاء حدوثاً ، هو القلب ، وآخرها موتاً هو القلب ) .
That the first organ to be formed from the body is the heart. As for the analogy, it is from two aspects: first: that the semen is a body composed of the four natures, and the airy and fiery dominate it. The evidence for this: that the whiteness of fluids only occurs due to the mixing of airy parts with them, as happens in cold, so it must be that the whiteness of semen is for this reason. What we have mentioned is confirmed: that when cold strikes semen it becomes thinner, and its whiteness disappears, even though cold is more deserving of thickening. That indicates that its whiteness was only because many fiery and airy parts were mixed with it, and when cold struck it, those fiery and airy parts left it, so its whiteness disappeared. And when this is established, we say: He, exalted, decreed by His wisdom that the earthly and watery parts present in semen become matter for the organs, and as for the airy and fiery parts in semen, He, exalted, makes them matter for the human spirits, except that those dense and subtle parts are mixed with one another in the first instance, but similarity is the cause of aggregation, so necessarily the subtle parts join together, and the dense parts join together. And since the subtle is quick to dissolve, divine wisdom required placing those subtle parts in the middle of that body, and placing those dense parts surrounding them, as protection for them. At this point, the semen becomes like a sphere, and the interior of that sphere is filled with those subtle airy and fiery parts, and its exterior is generated from those dense parts. And that place which becomes a place for those subtle parts is the place which, when it solidifies, becomes the heart. For this reason, the anatomists said: 'The first of the organs to occur is the heart, and the last of them to die is the heart.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two analogical arguments (al-qiyas min wajhayn) for the heart being the first organ to form. It begins by stating that semen is composed of the four elements (natures) with a predominance of air and fire. The evidence for this predominance is the whiteness of semen, which is attributed to the mixing of airy parts, similar to the whiteness of cold fluids. The argument is strengthened by the observation that cold makes semen thinner and loses its whiteness, whereas normally cold thickens; this indicates that the whiteness was due to the fiery and airy parts that depart when cold strikes. Once this is established, the author explains that by divine wisdom, the dense (earthy and watery) parts become the matter of the organs, while the subtle (airy and fiery) parts become the matter of the human spirits (al-arwah al-insaniyya). Initially all parts are mixed, but similarity causes aggregation: subtle parts join together and dense parts join together. Because the subtle is quickly dissipated, wisdom dictates placing the subtle at the center and the dense around them as protection. Thus the semen becomes a sphere with a center of subtle elements, and that center, when solidified, becomes the heart. This is why anatomists state that the heart is first to form and last to die.

المني
الطبائع الأربعة
الأجزاء اللطيفة
الأجزاء الكثيفة
الحكمة الإلهية
أهل التشريح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is purely philosophical speculation without empirical basis.
Correction: Sanusi grounds his argument in observable phenomena (whiteness of semen, effect of cold) and contemporary anatomical knowledge, integrating elemental theory with empirical observation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Sanusi uses elemental theory and empirical observation to argue that the heart originates from the light elements at the center of the sperm sphere.

Second Argument: Vital Heat and the Heart's Primacy
Sanusi presents the second argument for the heart's primacy using vital heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second argument for the heart's primacy based on vital heat and how it establishes the soul's unity and the heart as the absolute principal organ.

Core Idea
The heart is the first organ because it gathers vital heat needed for life.

Sanusi continues his demonstration of the heart's primacy by appealing to a well-known medical principle: the body's formation depends on vital heat, which must be concentrated to be effective. He identifies the heart as the natural gathering place of this heat, leading to the conclusion that the heart is formed first. This argument also reinforces the unity of the soul and establishes the heart as the absolute principal organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for flow, then the literal translation. Notice the logical connectors like 'فوجب' (so it is necessary).
2
Identify the key premise about vital heat
Find the statement that the body is not formed except by vital heat. Understand that 'الحرارة الغريزية' is a technical term for innate heat essential for life.
3
Trace the logical deduction
Follow the argument: vital heat must be gathered to be strong → its gathering place is the heart → heart's formation must precede other organs → therefore soul is one and heart is first organ.
Note how each step uses 'فوجب' to indicate necessary connection.
4
Consider the role of medical experiments
The passage cites medical experiments showing the soul's first attachment is to the spirit (الروح). Reflect on how this empirical support strengthens the rational argument.
This shows integration of empirical observation in kalam.
5
Reflect on the conclusion about the absolute principal organ
The final conclusion is that the heart is 'العضو الرئيسي المطلق' (the absolute principal organ). Think about what 'absolute' means here—primacy in origin and function.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان أن أول الأعضاء حدوثاً هو القلب : إن البدن لا يتكون ولا يتولد إلا بواسطة الحرارة الغريزية، وهذه الحرارة إنما تقوى وتكمل إذا كانت مجتمعة ، ومجمعها هو القلب . فوجب أن يكون تكون القلب سابقاً على تكون سائر الأعضاء. فثبت : أن النفس واحدة ، وثبت : أن أول الأعضاء حدوثاً هو القلب . ودلت التجارب الطبية : على أن المتعلق الأول للنفس هو الروح ، فوجب أن يكون تعلق النفس بالقلب ، قبل تعلقها بسائر الأعضاء ، فوجب أن يكون العضو الرئيسي المطلق ، هو القلب
And the second aspect in clarifying that the first of the organs to occur is the heart: that the body is not formed nor generated except by means of the vital heat, and this heat only becomes strong and complete when it is gathered, and its gathering place is the heart. So it is necessary that the formation of the heart precedes the formation of the rest of the organs. So it is established that the soul is one, and it is established that the first of the organs to occur is the heart. And medical experiments indicated that the first object of attachment of the soul is the spirit, so it is necessary that the soul's attachment to the heart is before its attachment to the rest of the organs, so it is necessary that the absolute principal organ is the heart.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the heart being the first organ. It states that the body is generated only through vital heat (al-harara al-ghariziyya), which must be concentrated to be effective, and its concentration point is the heart. Therefore, the heart's formation must precede that of other organs. The argument then asserts that this also establishes that the soul (al-nafs) is one entity. Medical experiments confirm that the soul's first attachment is to the spirit (al-ruh), and since the spirit is in the heart, the soul's attachment to the heart precedes attachment to other organs. Thus the heart is the absolute principal organ (al-'udw al-ra'isi al-mutlaq).

Key Terms
الحرارة الغريزية
القلب
النفس
الروح
العضو الرئيسي المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The heart is the seat of the soul itself.
Correction: The argument states the soul attaches to the spirit which resides in the heart, not that the heart is the soul's seat.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The heart's primacy is established by its role as the concentrator of vital heat, making it the first organ formed and the absolute principal organ.

Third Proof: Animal Soul Attached to Heart Makes It a Source of Perception
Sanusi presents the third proof that the heart becomes the source of perception due to the animal soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the animal soul's attributes—being sensitive and voluntary-moving—transfer to the heart upon attachment, making it the source of perception.

Core Idea
The animal soul's attributes transfer to the heart, making it the seat of perception.

Sanusi presents a concise proof based on a premise about the animal soul. He argues that if the animal soul must be sensitive and voluntary-moving, then when it attaches to the heart, the heart necessarily inherits those qualities and becomes the source of perception. This proof relies on a causal relationship between the soul and its substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the short proof carefully. Note the conditional structure: premise, attachment, conclusion.
2
Identify the agreed-upon premise
Find the statement about the animal soul that is 'not disputed'. What attributes does it have?
The premise is that the animal soul is sensitive and moves by will.
3
Follow the implication
When the soul attaches to the heart, the heart becomes sensitive and moving by will, thus a source of perception.
This is a direct transfer of attributes.
4
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: Is this a deductive proof? What does it assume about the soul-heart relationship?
It assumes the soul imparts its properties to the organ it attaches to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لا نزاع في أن النفس الحيوانية يجب كونها حساسة متحركة بالإرادة ، فإذا تعلقت النفس الحيوانية بالقلب ، وجب أن تكون تلك النفس حساسة متحركة الإرادة ، وذلك يقتضي أن يصير القلب منبعاً للإدراك
The third proof: there is no dispute that the animal soul must be sensitive and moving by will. So when the animal soul attaches to the heart, that soul must be sensitive and moving by will, and that necessitates that the heart becomes a source of perception.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the heart as the source of perception. It begins with a premise that is agreed upon: the animal soul (al-nafs al-hayawaniyya) must be sensitive (hassasa) and capable of voluntary movement (mutaharrika bi al-irada). When this animal soul attaches to the heart, these attributes—sensitivity and voluntary movement—are imparted to the heart. Consequently, the heart becomes a source (manba') of perception. The argument is brief and relies on the accepted nature of the animal soul; once it is attached to the heart, the heart necessarily becomes the organ through which perception and voluntary movement occur.

النفس الحيوانية
القلب
الإدراك

Misconception: The animal soul is the same as the rational soul or the soul that thinks.
Correction: The animal soul (al-nafs al-hayawaniyya) is a lower principle of life and motion shared with animals, not the rational soul responsible for intellect.
