The Fourth Objection: Accidental Distinction and Its Refutation
An objection and its refutation through two branches of argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidental distinctions cannot ground the distinction of souls without leading to infinite regress or circularity, and how the refutation relies on the material nature of accidents.

Core Idea
Accidental distinctions depend on matter, so they cannot apply to immaterial souls.

The fourth objection attempts to save the distinction of souls by appealing to accidental attributes. But as the author shows, accidents belong to material beings; applying them to immaterial substances generates impossible consequences. Let's trace the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection carefully. Understand what the objector is proposing: that souls differ by accidental attributes not essential to their quiddity.
Note the key term 'عارض غير لازم' (accidental attribute not necessary).
2
Follow the first branch of the refutation
The author assumes the difference in matter is due to essence. See how this leads to the absurdity of actually infinite parts already existing before division.
This branch shows the impossibility of essential difference in matter.
3
Examine the second branch
Now consider if matter differs by a separable accident. This leads to dependence on another matter, causing either infinite regress (تسلسل) or circularity (دور). Both are impossible.
Understand why both infinite regress and circularity are rejected in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الرابع : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل واحد منها يمتاز عن الآخر بسبب عارض غير لازم للماهية ؟ قوله : ( الاختلاف في العوارض إنما يكون بسبب المادة ، قلنا : هذا محال . لأنه لو كان كذلك، لكان امتياز هذا الجزء عن ذلك الجزء الآخر منه لأجل الامتياز في المادة ولو كان كذلك، لكانت مادة هذا الجزء إما أن تكون مخالفة لمادة الجزء الآخر إما في الماهية أو في العوارض . فإن كان الأول، كان هذا الامتياز حاصلا قبل القسمة . والحال في كل مادة غير الحال فيما يخالفها . فيلزم أن يكون الجسم مؤلفاً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل . وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني : وهو أن يقال إن مادة هذا الجزء تمتاز عن مادة الجزء الآخر بسبب عارض مفارق، فحينئذ يلزم افتقار تلك المادة إلى مادة أخرى، ولزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور وهما محالان.
The Fourth Objection: Why is it not permissible to say that each one of them is distinguished from the other due to an accidental attribute not necessary to the essence? His statement: 'Difference in accidents only occurs due to matter.' We said: This is impossible. Because if it were so, the distinction of this part from that other part would be due to the distinction in matter. And if it were so, the matter of this part would either be different from the matter of the other part either in essence or in accidents. If the first, then this distinction would have existed before the division. And the state of each matter is different from the state of what differs from it. It would then follow that the body is composed of actually infinite parts, which is impossible. And if the second: that is, it is said that the matter of this part is distinguished from the matter of the other part due to a separable accident, then the dependence of that matter on another matter would be necessary, and either an infinite regress or a circle would follow, both of which are impossible.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the Fourth Objection against the argument for the individuality of souls and its refutation. The objector asks: Why can we not claim that each soul is distinguished from others by an accidental attribute that is not essential to the quiddity? The author responds by stating the rule: differences in accidents only occur due to matter. He then examines two possibilities. First, if the matter of one part differs from the matter of another part in essence, then the distinction would have existed before the division of the body, implying an actually infinite number of parts, which is absurd. Second, if the matter of this part is distinguished from that part by a separable accident, then that matter would require another matter to be distinguished, leading to either an infinite regress or a circular dependency, both impossible. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
عارض (accident)
ماهية (essence/quiddity)
مادة (matter)
امتياز (distinction)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Souls could be distinguished by accidental attributes independent of matter.
Correction: Accidents belong to material substances; thus distinguishing souls by accidents would require them to be material, leading to absurdities like infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the Fourth Objection, what would be the result if the distinction in matter were due to a difference in essence?
A) The distinction would occur after division.
B) The distinction would have existed before division, implying an actually infinite number of parts.
C) The accident would be inseparable.
D) The soul would be material.

Key Takeaway
Accidental distinctions cannot apply to immaterial souls without leading to infinite regress; therefore soul distinctness must be essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument proves that accidental distinctions cannot ground the uniqueness of your soul. What accidental attributes (looks, wealth, status) do you secretly rely on to feel distinct from others? Will you examine your heart and identify one such attachment to uproot today?
?
If your soul's identity is essential and not dependent on any accident, then no loss of worldly qualities can diminish who you truly are. Which loss frightens you most? How can you begin to anchor your self-worth in your essence rather than in fleeting things?
?
The refutation shows that without essential distinction, infinite regress follows. Reflect on a habit of comparison: when you compare yourself to others, do you compare by accidents or by essence? Commit to a specific action this week to see others as souls with essential dignity, not as competitors in accidents.

Objection Five: Circularity with Transmigration
An objection that the argument presupposes refutation of transmigration, leading to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Fifth Objection argues that the proof for the soul's temporal origination is circular because it assumes the rejection of transmigration, which itself relies on temporal origination.

Core Idea
A good proof must not assume what it tries to prove – but that is exactly what this objection claims.

In this objection, the opponent concedes a key premise but then raises a troubling possibility: perhaps the soul existed in previous bodies. The critic argues that to rule this out, the author would need to refute transmigration, but that refutation itself depends on proving the soul's temporal origination – the very point at stake. This would make the argument circular.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector first grants the premise that distinction requires matter, then introduces the transmigration possibility. Follow the logic step by step.
Notice that the objector uses the phrase 'sallamnā' to concede a point.
2
Identify the circularity claim
Find how the objection argues that rejecting transmigration depends on proving the soul's temporal origination, creating a circle.
The key phrase is 'then circularity occurs, and it is invalid.'
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on why circularity is a serious flaw. How might a defender of the original argument respond to break the circle?
This prepares you to evaluate the strength of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الخامس : سلمنا : أن الامتياز بالعوارض لا يحصل إلا بالمادة . فلم قلتم : إنه لم توجد قبل هذا البدن مادة أخرى ؟ وبيانه : أنه يحتمل أن يقال : هذه النفس كانت قبل تعلقها بهذا البدن متعلقة بيدن آخر لا إلى أول وعلى هذا التقدير، فإنه لا يمكنكم إبطال هذا الاحتمال، إلا بإبطال القول بالتناسخ، فيكون هذا الدليل مبنياً على الدليل الدال على إبطال القول بالتناسخ . لكن دليلكم في إبطال القول بالتناسخ متفرع على القول بحدوث النفس على ما سيأتي شرحه . وحينئذ يقع الدور، وهو باطل.
The Fifth Objection: We grant that distinction by accidents only occurs through matter. But why do you say that no other matter existed before this body? Explanation: It is possible that it be said: This soul, before its attachment to this body, was attached to another body ad infinitum. On this assumption, you cannot refute this possibility except by refuting the doctrine of transmigration. Thus this argument would be based on the argument proving the nullity of transmigration. But your argument for nullifying transmigration is based on the doctrine of the temporal origination of the soul, as will be explained later. Then circularity occurs, and it is invalid.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the Fifth Objection. The objector concedes that distinction requires matter, but questions why no other matter could have existed before the present body. The soul might have been attached to an infinite series of previous bodies (transmigration). To reject this, one must refute transmigration. However, the refutation of transmigration itself depends on proving the soul's temporal origination (ḥudūth al-nafs), which is the very point in question. Hence the argument becomes circular and invalid.

Key Terms
تناسخ
حدوث النفس
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against transmigration can be established independently of the soul's temporal origination.
Correction: The objection shows that the refutation of transmigration itself relies on proving the soul's temporal origination, so it cannot be used to prove it without circularity.

Checkpoint
What does the Fifth Objection claim the argument leads to?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circularity
C) Distinction by accidents
D) Material composition

Key Takeaway
This objection challenges the argument for the soul's temporal origination by showing it leads to a vicious circle when combined with the assumption of transmigration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself using an argument that assumes exactly what you are trying to prove? How can you guard against circular reasoning in your own understanding of Islamic theology?
?
This objection shows that even a seemingly strong argument can collapse if it depends on a premise that it itself tries to prove. What is one belief you hold that might be vulnerable to such a critique?
?

Objection on Soul's Eternality and First Refutation
Objection and partial refutation on soul's eternality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Seventh Objection against soul's post-eternity and the first part of its refutation focusing on the insufficiency of self-awareness for distinction.

Core Idea
Self-awareness alone cannot explain the soul's distinction after death if it didn't before birth.

This lesson presents a clever objection that aims to show a symmetry between pre-eternity and post-eternity. The objector argues that if the soul cannot be pre-eternal due to lack of distinguishing features, then it cannot be post-eternal either. We will examine the objection, the imagined scenario of embryonic souls, and the beginning of Al-Sanusi's reply, which turns the argument on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its explanation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how the objector draws a parallel between pre-eternity (azal) and post-eternity (abad).
The key phrase is 'بعينه يمنع من كونها أبدية'.
2
Understand the embryonic soul example
The objector imagines souls that separated from bodies when they were embryos. These souls have no acquired knowledge or moral qualities, yet they remain distinct. How can that be?
This forces a dilemma: either they perish or they have distinction without cause.
3
Examine the proposed response and the first refutation
The defender claims self-awareness suffices for distinction. The author rejects this with a first argument: why wouldn't that same self-awareness suffice for pre-eternity?
The text cuts off after this first point.
4
Reflect on the symmetry argument
Consider why the objector's symmetry seems compelling and how the first refutation begins to undermine it. What might the second refutation be?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض السابع : إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن النفوس لا يمكن كونها أزلية، فهو بعينه يمنع من كونها أبدية، مع أن مذهبكم إنها أبدية . وبيانه هو أن النفوس المفارقة أبدانها، كل واحدة منها، تبقى ممتازة عن الغير، بسبب ما حصل لها من العلوم والأخلاق [ فنقول : نفترض الكلام في النفوس التي فارقت أبدانها حال ما كانت تلك الأبدان أجنة في بطون الأمهات، فههنا لم يحصل لتلك النفوس من العلوم والأخلاق شيء فوجب أن تحكموا، إما بأنها تفسد وتفنى، أو بحصول الامتياز من غير سبب . وكل ذلك عندكم باطل . فإن قالوا : كل واحدة منها قد حصل لها شعور بهويتها المعينة وذاتها المخصوصة . وهذا القدر يكفي في حصول الامتياز. فنقول : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول : أن نقول : وإن جاز هذا، فلم لا يكفي هذا القدر في حصول الامتياز في جانب الأزل ؟
The Seventh Objection: If what you have mentioned proves that souls cannot be eternal (azaliyya), then it exactly prevents them from being eternal (abadiyya), while your doctrine is that they are eternal (abadiyya). Explanation: The souls that have separated from their bodies, each one of them, remains distinct from others due to the knowledge and moral qualities they have acquired. [We say: We suppose the case of souls that separated from their bodies when those bodies were embryos in the wombs of mothers. Here, those souls have not acquired any knowledge or moral qualities. So you must either judge that they corrupt and perish, or that distinction occurs without a cause. Both are false in your view.] If they say: Each one of them has obtained awareness of its specific identity and particular self, and this is sufficient for distinction. We say: This is false from two aspects: First: We say: Even if this is allowed, why is this not sufficient for distinction in the aspect of pre-eternity (al-azal)?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the Seventh Objection, which argues that the reasoning used to deny the soul's pre-eternity also denies its post-eternity (survival after death). The author's view is that souls are eternal in the future (abadiyya). The objector imagines the case of souls that separated from bodies when those bodies were still embryos—these souls have no acquired knowledge or moral qualities. Yet they remain distinct. So either they perish or they have distinction without cause, both unacceptable. The imagined response is that awareness of self is sufficient. The author then begins to refute this, starting with the first of two arguments: if self-awareness suffices for distinction after death, why does it not suffice for distinction before birth (pre-eternity)? The text cuts off here, so the second aspect is missing.

Misconception: The objector's argument is only about pre-existence and doesn't apply to post-existence.
Correction: The objection explicitly shows that the same reasoning symmetrically applies to post-eternity, using the example of embryonic souls.

Refutation of Self-Awareness Argument for Soul's Eternity
Refutes soul's eternity via self-awareness distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to the argument for the soul's eternity based on self-awareness: that distinguishing souls by their self-awareness implies each has a distinct quiddity, which undermines the assumption of identical souls needed for pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Self-awareness makes souls essentially different, not identical.

This lesson unpacks a sophisticated refutation of an argument that claims the soul's self-awareness proves its eternity. The objection shows that if each soul is aware of its own specific essence, then souls cannot be identical in quiddity—a crucial assumption for the proof. Follow the logical steps to see how the argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the objection become clear.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the opponent's premise
Notice the opponent's claim that the soul's knowledge of its essence is identical to the essence itself. This is the starting point of the objection.
3
Understand the consequence of self-awareness distinction
If each soul is distinguished by awareness of its specific self, then each soul's quiddity must be different from others.
This is the core logical step: distinction implies essential difference.
4
See how this invalidates the proof
Realize that the proof for eternity required souls to be identical in quiddity. The objection shows the opposite, so the proof fails.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this objection leverages internal inconsistency in the opponent's own framework to refute the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن عندكم : علم النفس بذاتها المخصوصة . عين ذاتها المخصوصة . وتقولون : إن هذه الصورة : العقل والعاقل والمعقول : واحد . وإذا كان كذلك ، فالقول بأن كل واحدة منها تمتاز عن الأخرى ، بسبب شعورها بذاتها المخصوصة ، يقتضي [ امتياز كل واحدة منها بنفس (۱) ] ذاتها المخصوصة ، وهذا يقتضي أن تكون ماهية كل واحدة منها ، مخالفة لماهية الأخرى . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد بطل [ أصل (۲) ] هذا الدليل . فهذا تمام الكلام في الاعتراض على هذا الدليل .
Second: According to you: knowledge of the soul by its specific essence is the same as its specific essence. And you say: This form – intellect, intellecter, and intelligible – is one. If so, then saying that each one of them is distinguished from the other by reason of its awareness of its specific self requires [that each one of them is distinguished by its own (1)] specific essence, and this requires that the quiddity of each one of them is different from the quiddity of the other. If this is established, then the [foundation (2)] of this proof has become invalid. This completes the discussion on objecting to this proof.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against an argument for the eternity of souls based on self-awareness. The objection argues that if each soul is distinguished by its awareness of its own specific essence, this implies that the essence of each soul is unique and different from others. Therefore, the souls are not identical in quiddity, which undermines the premise that they are all the same kind of being – a key assumption in the argument for their pre-eternity. The conclusion is that the entire proof is invalidated.

Four Proofs That the Soul Survives Bodily Decline
Four proofs that the soul does not diminish with the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four empirical observations that demonstrate the soul's independence from the body and its survival after death.

Core Idea
Bodily weakness does not weaken the soul—it often perfects it.

The author presents four concrete examples from everyday experience showing that the soul thrives precisely when the body declines. These inductive arguments build a strong case that the soul is not a mere function of the body but a substance that survives bodily death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Read the opening sentence to understand what the author is arguing against. The claim is that the soul diminishes when the body weakens; the author says it is not so.
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Examine the first two proofs
The first proof involves deep thinking and immersion in ideas; the second involves ascetic practices. Both cause bodily decline but lead to soul perfection.
Notice the pattern: bodily harm → soul benefit.
3
Analyze the third proof about sleep
Sleep temporarily disables the senses and movement but allows the soul to connect with the unseen, showing its distinct capacity.
This proof highlights the soul's activity independent of the body.
4
Consider the fourth proof about aging and intellect
From middle age onward, the body withers while the intellect grows, as seen in prophets and wise advisors. This challenges any dependence of the soul on bodily health.
This proof is based on common observation of human development.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that since bodily decline never causes soul-decline but rather enhances it, the soul must not die when the body dies.
This is a strong inductive argument for the soul's immortality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه ليس الأمر كذلك ، لوجوه :
الأول : إن المواظبة على الأفكار الغامضة، والاستغراق فيها يوجب نقصاناً شديداً في البدن ، ولم يلزم منه حصول النقصان في النفس البتة ، بل يوجب الكمال العظيم في النفس .
الثاني : إن المواظبة على الرياضة، وتجويع النفس والانقطاع عن المحسوسات والجسمانيات ، يوجب النقصان الشديد في البدن ، وذلك لا يوجب نقصان النفس ، بل يوجب حصول الكمال الشديد للنفس .
الثالث : إن النوم يوجب نقصان حال البدن ، فإنه يزول عنه حسر السمع والبصر ، والقدرة على المشي والأخذ ، ولا يوجب نقصان حال النفس ، بل يوجب كمال حال النفس . فإنها في هذا الوقت قدرت على الاتصال بعالم الغيب .
الرابع : إن جسد الإنسان من أول الكهولة يأخذ في الانتقاص والذبول ، وعقله من هذا الوقت لا يأخذ في الانتقاص والذبول ، بل كمال عقله يبتدىء في التزايد من هذا الوقت. ولهذا السبب قيل : إن بعثة أكثر الأنبياء إنما وقعت في هذا الوقت . وكان ملوك العجم لا يختارون الرجال للمحادثات المهمة ، إلا الذين يكونون بين الأربعين وبين الخمسين قالوا : لأنه في هذا الوقت يتكامل العقل ولم يحصل في البدن نقصان ظاهر . فثبت : أن النفس لو ماتت بموت البدن ، لوجب أن يظهر النقصان فيها ، بسبب نقصان البدن . لكن لم يظهر النقصان فيها بسبب نقصانه بل قد بينا في هذه المواضع الأربعة : أنه يحصل الكمال فيها ، بسبب نقصان البدن . فوجب القطع بأن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن .
And we only say: it is not like that, for reasons: First: Indeed, persistence in deep thoughts and immersion in them causes severe deficiency in the body, and no deficiency in the soul results from that at all, rather it causes great perfection in the soul. Second: Indeed, persistence in spiritual exercise, starving the soul, and cutting off from physical sensations and corporeal things causes severe deficiency in the body, and that does not cause deficiency of the soul, rather it causes the attainment of intense perfection for the soul. Third: Indeed, sleep causes deficiency in the state of the body, for the sense of hearing and sight and the ability to walk and grasp depart from it, and it does not cause deficiency in the state of the soul, rather it causes perfection in the state of the soul. For at this time it is able to connect with the world of the unseen. Fourth: Indeed, the body of man from the beginning of youth begins to decrease and wither, and his intellect from this time does not begin to decrease and wither, rather the perfection of his intellect begins to increase from this time. For this reason it was said: The sending of most prophets occurred only at this time. And the kings of the Persians did not choose men for important consultations except those who were between forty and fifty. They said: Because at this time the intellect is perfected and no apparent deficiency has occurred in the body. So it is established: If the soul died with the death of the body, then deficiency would necessarily appear in it due to deficiency of the body. But deficiency does not appear in it due to its deficiency; rather we have shown in these four points that perfection occurs in it due to deficiency of the body. So it is necessary to decide that the soul does not die with the death of the body.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents four inductive proofs that the soul is not harmed by bodily decline. The author first states that the claim being refuted is false, then offers four observations. Each observation shows a case where the body weakens but the soul strengthens, contradicting the idea that the soul depends on bodily health. The first proof: intense intellectual effort weakens the body but perfects the soul. The second: ascetic practices weaken the body but perfect the soul. The third: sleep temporarily disables bodily senses yet allows the soul to connect with the unseen, indicating its independence. The fourth: from middle age onward, the body decays while the intellect grows, as seen in the timing of prophethood and wise counsel. The author concludes that since bodily decline never causes soul-decline but rather enhances it, the soul must survive bodily death.

Key Terms
نفس (soul)
بدن (body)
كمال (perfection)
نقصان (deficiency)
عقل (intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is simply a function of the body and thus decays with it.
Correction: The author shows empirical counterexamples where the soul is perfected precisely when the body is weakened, such as during deep thought, asceticism, sleep, and aging.

Key Takeaway
The soul's perfection during bodily decline proves its independence and survival beyond the death of the body.

The Fourth Proof: The Soul's Immortality Based on Degrees of Existence
Proof via ontological hierarchy that the soul survives bodily death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hierarchy of existence—from accidents to bodies to abstract substances—establishes that the soul cannot die with the body.

Core Idea
The soul's existence is ontologically stronger than body and accidents, so bodily death cannot affect it.

This passage presents a logical argument that ranks accidents, bodies, and abstract substances by their strength of existence. Since the soul is an abstract substance, its existence is too strong to be nullified by the body's death. The author first states the conclusion, then unpacks the premises that justify it, showing that accidents are weaker than bodies and bodies weaker than the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's conclusion
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument: that the soul does not die with the body because accidents are weaker than bodies, and bodies weaker than the abstract soul.
The conclusion is stated at the beginning.
2
Examine the first premise
Understand why accidents are weaker than bodies: accidents depend on bodies for existence, while bodies are self-subsistent. The author calls this nearly self-evident.
Accidents cannot exist without bodies.
3
Examine the second premise's first aspect
The second premise claims bodies are weaker than abstract substances. The first aspect given is that spatial things (bodies) need places, spaces, and directions, while abstract substances (souls) are independent of them.
The passage cuts off here, but this establishes the hierarchy.
4
Reflect on the hierarchy
Consider how this hierarchy insulates the soul: since the soul is stronger than bodies, the death of the body cannot cause the soul's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن : أن الأعراض القائمة بالأجسام، أضعف وجوداً من تلك الأجسام . والأجسام أضعف وجوداً من الموجود المجرد القائم بالنفس ، فيلزم أن تكون الأعراض أضعف بكثير من الموجود المجرد . وإذا كان كذلك ، وجب القطع بأن موت البدن لا يوجب موت النفس . فتفتقر ههنا إلى بيان مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : إن الأعراض القائمة بالأجسام، أضعف في الوجود من تلك الأجسام . وهذا يقرب من أن يكون بديهياً ، لأن الأعراض محتاجة إلى تلك الأجسام ، ويمتنع دخولها في الوجود ، إلا في تلك الأجسام . وأما تلك الأجسام فإنها قائمة بأنفسها وغنية عن تلك الأعراض . وهذا يوجب القطع بأن الأعراض أضعف وجوداً من الأجسام .
وأما المقدمة الثانية في بيان أن الأجسام أضعف وجوداً من الجوهر المجرد : فبيانها من وجوه :
الأول : إن المتحيزات محتاجة إلى الأمكنة والأحياز والجهات ، والجوهر المجرد غني عنها .
The fourth proof: That the soul does not die with the death of the body: That the accidents subsisting in bodies are weaker in existence than those bodies. And bodies are weaker in existence than the abstract existent subsisting in the soul, so it follows that accidents are much weaker than the abstract existent. And if that is so, then it is necessary to decide that the death of the body does not cause the death of the soul. So we need here to explain premises: The first premise: Indeed, the accidents subsisting in bodies are weaker in existence than those bodies. And this is nearly self-evident, because accidents need those bodies, and it is impossible for them to come into existence except in those bodies. As for those bodies, they subsist by themselves and are independent of those accidents. And this necessitates the decision that accidents are weaker in existence than bodies. As for the second premise, in explaining that bodies are weaker in existence than the abstract substance: Its explanation is from aspects: First: Indeed, spatial things need places, spaces, and directions, while the abstract substance is independent of them.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents a deductive argument for the soul's immortality based on degrees of ontological strength. The author first states the conclusion: accidents are weaker than bodies, and bodies are weaker than the abstract existent (the soul), so accidents are much weaker than the soul. Hence bodily death cannot cause the soul's death. He then explains two premises. The first premise: accidents depend on bodies for existence, while bodies are self-subsistent, so accidents are weaker. This is nearly self-evident. The second premise: bodies are weaker than abstract substance. To prove this, he begins the first aspect: spatial entities (like bodies) require places and directions, whereas the abstract substance is independent of them. The passage cuts off here, but the argument establishes a hierarchy of existence that insulates the soul from bodily corruption.

Correction: The passage shows the soul is an abstract substance that is ontologically stronger than bodies, not a dependent accident.

The Fifth Proof: Induction from Enduring Relics
The author presents the fifth proof from empirical observation of enduring pious relics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how empirical observation of enduring pious relics provides inductive proof for the soul's survival after death.

Core Idea
Connectedness to the divine grants endurance; disconnectedness to matter brings decay.

In this passage, the author presents a unique argument from empirical observation. He notes that objects associated with pious individuals—such as clothes and prayer rugs—survive for centuries, while the possessions of worldly kings quickly perish. This inductive pattern serves as evidence for the rational soul's immortality when illuminated by divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the author's examples and reasoning sink in before interpreting.
Notice the concrete examples: buildings, clothes, the Prophet's cloak.
2
Identify the inductive pattern
The author lists several observations showing that things connected to piety last longer than worldly things. Identify the common factor: connection to the divine presence.
3
Extract the general principle
From the examples, the author derives a principle: anything connected to the divine endures proportionally; anything connected to matter perishes quickly. State this principle in your own words.
This principle is the key inference from the induction.
4
Apply the principle to the rational soul
The author argues that if the soul is illuminated by knowledge of the Necessary Existent, it must endure eternally. See how the prayer rug analogy reinforces this: even a weak rug lasted 120 years due to distant connection, so the soul, being the vessel of divine light, deserves even more permanence.
The prayer rug example is a powerful rhetorical tool: if a rug can last that long, how much more the soul?
5
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Consider why this inductive argument might be convincing. It relies on observable, repeatable patterns rather than abstract reasoning alone. Ask yourself if you find it persuasive and why.
This proof is often cited in Islamic theology as an empirical argument for the soul's immortality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل بناء بني للدنيا مثل قصور الملوك والأكابر، فإنه ينهدم في أسرع مدة مع غاية قوته. وإنا نرى أهل الزهد والطاعة والعلم، يبقى ذكرهم بعد موتهم دهراً داهراً وزماناً طويلاً. وأيضاً: نرى أن ثياب أهل الزهد والطاعة والعلم تبقى بعد موتهم دهراً داهراً وزماناً طويلاً وتبقى آثارهم وبلياتهم زماناً طويلاً، مع أنها تكون في غاية الرخاوة والضعف. ولقد رأيت سجادة متخذة من الخرق الضعيفة، بقيت بعد موت صاحبها مائة وعشرين سنة، بل نقول: بقاء ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، بقي نيفاً وستمائة سنة. وأما ثياب الملوك والسلاطين فإنها مع غاية شرفها وصفاقتها، تفنى في أقل مدة. وأيضاً: نرى الرجل العالم أو الزاهد مع فقره وغاية ضعفه التفات الناس إليه، يبقى ذكره بعد موته مئات وألوفاً من السنين، وأما الجبابرة والسلاطين فإنه لا يبقى ذكرهم في الدنيا إلا أياماً قلائل. ثم إنها تنقرض وتزول، ولا يبقى منهم في الدنيا لا أثر ولا خبر. وعدم. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: هذا الاستقراء يدل على أن كل ما له تعلق بحضرة الجلال، فإنه يبقى بحسب قوة ذلك التعلق، وكل ما له تعلق بعالم الجسم والهيولى، فإنه يكون سريع الدثور والفناء. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن النفس الناطقة إذا أشرقت بنور معرفة واجب الوجود، وتقوت بتلك القوة التي لا نهاية لها في العدة والشدة والمرة، وجب أن تبقى بقاء مصوناً عن الفساد، دائماً بدوام السبع الشداد. وتمام التقريب: إني لما رأيت نظرت تلك السجادة المركبة من تلك الخرق السخيفة، وأنها بقيت مدة مائة وعشرين سنة، قلت في نفسي: امتياز هذه الخرق السخيفة بهذا البقاء عن سائر الثياب الصفيقة النفيسة، إنما حصل لأجل أنه اتفق أنه وقع عليها رجل ذلك الرجل الذي كان مدبر بدنه: نفساً أشرقت بنور معرفة الله تعالى فلأجل هذا التعلق البعيد، وجدت هذه الخرق الضعيفة: هذا البقاء الطويل. فإذا صار هذا التعلق البعيد سبباً للحصول هذا البقاء، فما ظنك بجوهر النفس الناطقة، مع أنها هي الظرف والوعاء لنور الجلال، وضوء عالم الإله.
And every building built for the world, like the palaces of kings and great ones, collapses in the shortest time despite its utmost strength. And we see the people of asceticism, obedience, and knowledge: their remembrance remains after their death for a long time and a great duration. Also: we see that the clothes of the people of asceticism, obedience, and knowledge remain after their death for a long time and a great duration, and their traces and trials remain for a long time, even though they are at the utmost softness and weakness. And I have seen a prayer rug made of weak rags: it remained after the death of its owner for one hundred and twenty years. Rather we say: The survival of the garment of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: it remained for more than six hundred years. As for the clothes of kings and sultans, despite their utmost nobility and thickness, they perish in the shortest time. Also: we see the learned man or the ascetic, despite his poverty and utmost weakness, people turn to him; his remembrance remains after his death for hundreds and thousands of years. As for the tyrants and sultans, their remembrance does not remain in the world except for a few days. Then they become extinct and vanish, and nothing remains of them in the world, neither trace nor news. And non-existence. When you know this, we say: This induction indicates that everything connected to the Presence of Majesty remains according to the strength of that connection, and everything connected to the world of body and matter is quickly obliterated and perishes. If this is established, we say: The rational soul, when it is illuminated by the light of knowledge of the Necessary Existent, and becomes strong with that power which has no end in number, intensity, and force, it must remain with an existence protected from corruption, eternal with the eternity of the seven heavens. And the complete clarification: When I saw and looked at that prayer rug composed of those contemptible rags, and that it remained for one hundred and twenty years, I said to myself: The distinction of these contemptible rags with this endurance over other thick and precious clothes occurred only because it happened that the person of that man who was the manager of his body fell upon it – a soul illuminated by the light of knowledge of Allah Almighty. So because of this distant connection, these weak rags found this long endurance. So if this distant connection became a cause for obtaining this endurance, what do you think of the essence of the rational soul, given that it is the container and receptacle of the light of Majesty and the light of the divine world?
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is the fifth proof (al-hujja al-khamisa) for the survival of the rational soul. It is based on empirical induction (istiqra'). The author begins by contrasting worldly buildings (palaces) that collapse quickly despite strength, with the enduring remembrance of the pious (ascetics, obedient, learned). He then provides specific examples: the clothes of the pious, even though weak, outlast the garments of kings; the Prophet's cloak lasted over 600 years; a prayer rug of rags lasted 120 years. He also notes that the memory of a scholar or ascetic, despite his poverty, lasts centuries, while tyrants are forgotten quickly. From these observations, the author derives a general principle: anything connected to the divine presence (hadrat al-jalal) endures proportionally to the strength of that connection, while anything connected to the material world (body and hyle) perishes quickly. Applying this to the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa), he argues that if it is illuminated by knowledge of the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) and strengthened by that infinite power, it must endure eternally. He reinforces this with the specific case of the prayer rug: its connection to a pious man gave it long life; how much more should the rational soul endure, since it is the very vessel of divine light?

واجب الوجود
تعلق
حضرة الجلال
عالم الجسم
الهيولى
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the endurance of physical objects is merely coincidental and unrelated to the soul's nature.
Correction: The author argues that the pattern is consistent and causally connected: objects endure because of their association with divine light. If a distant connection can preserve a rug, then the soul itself, being the direct receptacle of that light, must endure even more.

The Sixth Proof: Long Life of the Merciful
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah's sixth proof from empirical induction on lifespan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sixth proof uses empirical induction about long life of merciful people to argue for the preservation of the soul.

Core Idea
Merciful people live longer — this isn't coincidence, it's a sign of the soul's nature.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which is based on a striking observation: people who are devoted to God and compassionate to others tend to live long, healthy lives, while the cruel and harmful often die young. The author extends this pattern to animals and then uses it to argue that perfecting the soul's two faculties preserves not only the body but even more so the soul itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the empirical claim at the beginning and how it is supported by examples from humans and animals.
The language is straightforward but the logic is layered.
2
Identify the two types of people and animals
The author contrasts the merciful and the harmful, first in humans, then in animals. See the parallel structure.
This strengthens the inductive argument.
3
Understand the definitions of the virtues
The author defines 'magnifying God's command' as perfecting the theoretical faculty, and 'compassion to creation' as perfecting the practical faculty. Recognize that these are key kalam terms.
These faculties are associated with the rational soul.
4
Trace the conclusion
The induction shows that perfecting these faculties preserves the body. Since the faculties reside in the soul, it is even more fitting that the soul itself is preserved. This is the core of the proof.
This is a form of argument from the lesser to the greater (a minori ad maius).
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is considered a proof for the survival of the soul. How does it differ from other proofs?
It relies on empirical observation rather than purely rational deduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة: إنا نرى أن كل إنسان، كان مقبلاً على التعظيم لأمر الله تعالى، والشفقة على خلق الله تعالى، فإنه يكون عمره طويلاً، ويبقى طول عمره مصوناً عن الآفات والبليات. وكل من كان كثير الإقدام على القتل والإضرار، فإنه يكون عمره قصيراً، ولا بد وأن يقع في نوع من أنواع الآفات. وهذا هو الاستقراء الغالب. بل نقول: الحيوانات السليمة تطول أعمارها، والحيوانات المؤذية تقصر أعمارها. ومعنى التعظيم لأمر الله تكميل القوة النظرية التي للنفس. ومعنى الشفقة على خلق الله: تكميل القوة العملية التي للخلق. فهذا الاستقراء يدل على أن الاشتغال بتكميل هاتين القوتين يوجب إبقاء البدن بحسب الإمكان، فلأن يوجب إبقاء جوهر النفس مع أن محل هاتين القوتين هو جوهر النفس كان أولى.
The sixth proof: We see that every human who devotes himself to magnifying the command of Allah Almighty and showing compassion to the creation of Allah Almighty, his life is long, and throughout his long life he is protected from calamities and trials. And everyone who is much given to killing and harming, his life is short, and inevitably he falls into some kind of affliction. And this is the prevalent induction. Rather, we say: harmless animals have long lives, and harmful animals have short lives. And the meaning of 'magnifying the command of Allah' is perfecting the theoretical faculty of the soul. And the meaning of 'compassion to the creation of Allah' is perfecting the practical faculty of the creature. So this induction indicates that occupying oneself with perfecting these two faculties necessitates preserving the body as much as possible, so it is even more fitting that it necessitates preserving the essence of the soul, given that the locus of these two faculties is the essence of the soul.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is the sixth proof (al-hujja al-sadisa) for the survival of the rational soul. It is also based on empirical induction. The author observes that people who are devoted to magnifying God's command (i.e., righteous actions) and showing compassion to others have long lives free from afflictions. Conversely, those who are prone to killing and harming others have short lives and inevitably suffer. He extends this to animals: harmless animals live long, harmful ones have short lives. Then he explains the meaning of these virtues: 'magnifying God's command' means perfecting the theoretical faculty (al-quwwa al-nazariyya) of the soul, and 'showing compassion' means perfecting the practical faculty (al-quwwa al-‘amaliyya). From this induction, he concludes that perfecting these two faculties preserves the body as much as possible, so it is even more fitting that they preserve the essence of the soul (jawhar al-nafs), since these faculties reside in the soul itself.

القوة العملية
الاستقراء

جوهر النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Long life is purely random or genetic and unrelated to moral character or the soul.
Correction: The proof shows a consistent empirical correlation between merciful character (perfecting theoretical and practical faculties) and long life, indicating that these faculties preserve the body and soul.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The merciful life preserves the soul, for the soul's own faculties, when perfected, sustain both body and soul.

The Seventh Proof: Three Levels of Human Conditions
The seventh proof classifies human conditions and begins an argument on endurance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three levels of human conditions as classified by the seventh proof and how the argument from endurance supports the superiority of spiritual states.

Core Idea
Spiritual conditions outlast bodily ones, proving the soul's endurance.

This passage introduces the seventh proof for the survival of the rational soul. The author, Imam al-Sanusi, divides human conditions into three levels: purely bodily, mixed bodily-spiritual, and purely spiritual. This classification sets up an argument that spiritual conditions are inherently more enduring than bodily ones, a key step in demonstrating the soul's immortality.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the three divisions and their examples.
Pay attention to the order: lowest (bodily), middle (speech), highest (true sciences).
2
Understand each level
Identify the defining characteristics of each level: bodily (purely physical), mixed (speech as both physical and spiritual), and spiritual (true sciences and cognitions).
Speech is considered mixed because it has a physical sound but conveys meaning.
3
Trace the argument from endurance
The author states that bodily things are less receptive to endurance than spiritual things, and that even bodily things associated with spiritual people achieve maximum endurance.
This is the beginning of a proof; the full argument likely continues on subsequent pages.
4
Reflect on the hierarchy
Consider why bodily constructions (mosques, schools) are placed at the lowest level despite their religious significance. The author emphasizes that spiritual knowledge is the highest state.
This addresses a potential misconception about the relative value of physical vs. spiritual works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: إن أحوال الإنسانية على ثلاثة أقسام: فأدونها: الأحوال الجسمانية المحضة. مثل: الأبنية التي تبنى للخيرات، كالمساجد والمدارس والخانقاهات، وأوسطها: الأحوال التي تكون من جهة جسمانية ومن جهة روحانية وهو الكلام، فإنه من حيث إنه صوت يتولد عن الآلات الجسمانية فهو الجسمانيات، ومن حيث إنه يدل على المعاني الروحانية النفسانية كان من الروحانيات. وأعلاها الأحوال النفسانية المحضة، وهي العلوم الحقيقية والمعارف الصادقة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: من الظاهر: أن الجسمانيات أقل قبولاً للبقاء من الروحانيات. ثم إنا نرى كل ما ينسب إلى أصحاب الروحانيات من الجسمانيات، فإنه ينتهي في البقاء إلى أقصى الغايات الممكنة. وإن شئت
The seventh proof: The human conditions are of three divisions: the lowest are the purely bodily conditions, such as buildings erected for good deeds, like mosques, schools, and monasteries; the middle are the conditions that are partly bodily and partly spiritual, namely speech (kalam), for insofar as it is a sound generated by bodily organs, it is bodily, and insofar as it indicates spiritual-psychical meanings, it is spiritual; and the highest are the purely psychical conditions, which are the true sciences and sincere cognitions. When you know this, we say: It is apparent that bodily things are less receptive to endurance than spiritual things. Then we see that every bodily thing attributed to the people of spirituality reaches, in endurance, the utmost possible limits. And if you wish...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is the seventh proof (al-hujja al-sabi‘a) for the survival of the rational soul. It presents a classification (taqseem) of human states into three levels. The lowest level is purely bodily conditions (jismaniyya mahda), exemplified by buildings for good works (mosques, schools, monasteries). The middle level is mixed bodily and spiritual conditions, namely speech (kalam): as a physical sound it is bodily, as conveying spiritual meanings it is spiritual. The highest level is purely psychical/spiritual conditions (nafsaniyya mahda), which are true sciences (al-‘ulum al-haqiqiyya) and sincere cognitions (al-ma‘arif al-sadiqa). The author then argues that bodily things are obviously less capable of endurance than spiritual things. He observes that even physical things associated with spiritual people (like their buildings) achieve the utmost possible endurance. The proof is incomplete in this window, ending with 'and if you wish', but the reasoning points to the superiority of the spiritual and thus the endurance of the soul.

Correction: The author places bodily constructions at the lowest level; the highest level is purely spiritual conditions, i.e., true sciences and sincere cognitions.

The Soul's Permanence: An Analogical Argument
A concluding rhetorical argument for the soul's permanence via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses an analogical argument (awlā) to establish the soul's deserving of permanence based on its noble qualities.

Core Idea
The rational soul's nobility as a vessel for divine light makes it more deserving of permanence than any weak attachment.

This brief passage concludes a larger argument about the soul's permanence. The author presents a rhetorical comparison: if weak attachments can lead to long-lasting effects, then the rational soul, which is the very receptacle of divine majesty and light, must be even more deserving of permanence. This lesson unpacks that analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a finding, a conditional statement, then a rhetorical question.
2
Identify the analogy
Identify the two items being compared: weak holes/distant attachment vs. the rational soul. What is the point of comparison?
The comparison is about causing permanence.
3
Recognise the rhetorical force
The author uses a rhetorical question 'what is your opinion...' to make a stronger case. Understand this as an a fortiori argument: if X, then even more so Y.

Source Text

Answering the Objection: Possibility as Attribute
Refuting the claim that the argument requires possibility to be an existing attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument for the soul's composition from the possibility of corruption does not depend on treating possibility as an existing attribute, but rather on its conceptual dependence on a locus.

Core Idea
Possibility need not be real; its dependence alone proves the soul is composite.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: that the proof for the soul's composition relies on assuming possibility is an existing attribute inhering in a subject. The author clarifies that the proof works even if possibility is merely conceptual—what matters is that the concept of possibility intrinsically depends on something else, leading to the same conclusion about prime matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims the entire proof is built on the premise that possibility is an existing attribute needing a locus. Read the first line of the Arabic to see how the author presents this objection.
The objection is labeled as 'the second question'.
2
See the author's key move
The author immediately denies the need to prove possibility is an existing attribute. He says: 'لا حاجة في تقرير هذا الدليل إلى إثبات أن الإمكان صفة موجودة.' Understand this pivot—he shifts the ground of the argument.
The proof does not depend on the ontological status of possibility.
3
Understand the dependence argument
Whether possibility is existent or non-existent, its concept is not independent; it requires something to attribute it to. The intellect judges that there must be a locus (محل) for this possibility. Trace the logical steps: the possibility of corruption occurs when corruption happens, but the corrupted thing cannot be the locus because it has ceased to exist.
The locus must be something enduring—prime matter.
4
Apply to the soul
The conclusion: if the soul is corruptible, it must be composed of prime matter and form, regardless of whether susceptibility is considered an attribute or not. Note that the argument does not hinge on the reality of the attribute but on the logical need for a substrate.
This upholds the composite nature of the soul against the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : ( إن هذا الكلام مبني على أن الإمكان صفة موجودة مفتقرة إلى المحل ، فنقول : لا حاجة في تقرير هذا الدليل إلى إثبات أن الإمكان صفة موجودة. بل نقول : إمكان الفساد ، سواء كان موجوداً أو معدوماً ، فهو حاصل عند حصول الفساد والمفهوم من هذا الإمكان ، سواء كان موجوداً أو معدوماً ، مفهوم غير مستقل بنفسه ، فلا بد من فرض شيء آخر يحكم العقل بجعل ذلك الإمكان صفة له ، وذلك الشيء لا بد وأن يكون حاصلا عند حصول ذلك الإمكان ، ولما امتنع أن يكون الذي عدم باقياً عند عدمه ، وثبت : أن إمكان الفساد حاصل عند حصول ذلك الفساد ، وجب القطع بأن محل ذلك الإمكان شيء آخر . والذي هو محل إمكان ذلك الشيء هو الهيولي . فثبت : أن النفس لو كانت قابلة للفساد ، فسواء قلنا : إن هذا القبول صفة موجودة ، أو معدومة . فإنه يجب كون النفس مركبة من الهيولى والصورة .
As for the second question: and it is his statement: 'This speech is built on the premise that possibility (imkaan) is an existing attribute in need of a locus.' So we say: There is no need in establishing this proof to prove that possibility is an existing attribute. Rather, we say: The possibility of corruption (imkaan al-fasaad), whether it is existent or non-existent, is present at the occurrence of corruption, and the concept understood from this possibility, whether existent or non-existent, is a concept not independent by itself, so it is necessary to assume something else that the intellect judges to make that possibility an attribute of it, and that thing must be present at the occurrence of that possibility. And since it is impossible that the thing which has ceased to exist remains at its non-existence, and it is established that the possibility of corruption is present at the occurrence of that corruption, it is necessary to judge that the locus of that possibility is something else. And that which is the locus of the possibility of that thing is prime matter (hayula). So it is established that if the soul were susceptible to corruption, then whether we say this susceptibility is an existing attribute or non-existent, then it is necessary that the soul be composed of prime matter and form.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage answers an objection that the previous argument depends on assuming possibility is an existing attribute inhering in a locus. The author responds that the proof does not require establishing that possibility is an existing attribute. Instead, regardless of whether it is existent or non-existent, the concept of possibility is a concept that is not self-subsistent; it must depend on something else. The intellect judges that this possibility belongs to something, and that something must be present when the possibility occurs. Since the corrupted thing is not present at its own non-existence, the possibility cannot inhere in it, so it must inhere in something else—namely, prime matter (هيولى). Thus, if the soul were corruptible, it would have to be composed of matter and form, whether one considers the susceptibility to corruption as an existing attribute or not.

Key Terms
إمكان
صفة موجودة
محل
هيولى
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof for the soul's composition depends on assuming possibility is an actual existing attribute.
Correction: The proof does not require possibility to be an existing attribute; it works on the conceptual dependence of possibility, whether existent or non-existent.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the author's answer to the second objection?
A) Possibility must be proven to be an existing attribute.
B) The proof works regardless of whether possibility is existent or non-existent.
C) The soul is not composed of matter and form.
D) The locus of possibility is the soul itself.

Key Takeaway
The argument for the soul's composition from possibility does not depend on the ontological reality of possibility, but on the logical necessity of a substrate for any dependent concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that possibility, though not existing as a separate thing, still requires a subject. Consider your own possibilities in life—your potentials, your future, your hopes. Do you treat them as if they are self-subsistent, or do you recognize that they depend on something beyond you? What does that dependence reveal about your need for God as the ultimate subject of all that is not independent?
?

Your body will corrupt, but your soul—what does this argument imply about its nature? Are you living today as if your soul is composite and perishable, or as if it is simple and enduring? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed your soul is not made of matter?
?
The objection raised in this passage is a form of intellectual evasion: it tries to avoid the conclusion by attacking a premise. Have you ever used intellectual excuses to avoid facing a truth that demands personal change? What is one truth you have been avoiding, and what would it cost you to accept it today?

The Soul's Transfer Between Bodies: A Rational Problem
Analogy and argument about soul's transfer between bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the soul is a separate substance becomes problematic given that transfer between bodies is not observed.

Core Idea
If the soul were separate, it could transfer bodies—but it doesn't.

The author uses an analogy of switching tools to test the claim that the soul is a simple substance independent of the body. If the soul were truly separate, one would expect it to be able to leave one body and inhabit another, much like switching tools. However, this transfer is not observed in reality, raising a difficulty for the claim of separateness. Yet the author does not definitively reject the idea, instead suggesting a possible compatibility-based explanation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the tool analogy
Read the opening analogy about defective tools and switching. Notice how it illustrates the ability to transfer between tools.
The analogy sets up the expectation for transfer.
2
Apply the analogy to the soul
The author argues: if the soul is separate like a tool, it should be able to transfer bodies. Identify this logical step in the passage.
3
Identify the problem
Since transfer is not observed, the claim that the soul is a separate substance becomes problematic (mushkil). Find the exact Arabic phrase.
The word 'مشكلاً' indicates difficulty, not outright rejection.
4
Consider the possible explanation
The author offers a rational possibility: each soul and body have unique properties (khasā'is) that make them exclusively compatible. Reflect on how this resolves the problem.
This shows the author is not dogmatic but open to deeper explanations.
5
Reflect on human limitation
The passage ends with a reminder that human intellect cannot fully grasp God's secrets. Consider how this humility relates to the argument.
It underscores that the matter may be beyond rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) الثانية . فإذا انقضى زمان ، واتفق أن صارت الآلة الثانية مختلة ، والآلة الأولى صالحة ، ترك الثانية ورجع إلى استعمال الأولى . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو كانت النفس جوهراً مجرداً مبنايناً عن البدن ، لكان يجب أن يصح عليها أن تنتقل من هذا البدن إلى بدن آخر ، ثم بعد حين تعود إلى هذا البدن ، كما بيناه في استعمال الآلات . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، صار قولنا : النفس جوهر مجرد ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني : مشكلاً واعلم : أنه لا يمتنع في العقل أن يكون لكل نفس خاصية ، ولكل بدن خاصية . لا تصلح تلك النفس إلا لذلك البدن ، ولا يصلح ذلك البدن إلا لتلك النفس وصول العقول البشرية إلى أسرار مخلوقات الله سبحانه وتعالى على سبيل التمام والكمال : محال والله أعلم
(1) The second [tool]. When time passes and it happens that the second tool becomes defective while the first tool is sound, one abandons the second and returns to using the first. And if you know this, we say: If the soul were a simple substance separate from the body, then it should be possible for it to transfer from this body to another body, then after a while return to this body, as we explained in the use of tools. And since it is not so, our statement that the soul is a simple substance not a body nor bodily becomes problematic. Know that it is not impossible in reason that each soul has a property and each body has a property. That soul is only suitable for that body, and that body is only suitable for that soul. And the reaching of human intellects to the secrets of the creations of God, glorified and exalted, completely and perfectly is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument concerning the nature of the soul, followed by an objection and a concluding remark. The author begins with an analogy: when one has two tools and the second becomes defective, one returns to the first. This illustrates the ability to switch between tools. Applying this to the soul, the author argues that if the soul were a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) entirely separate from the body, it should be able to transfer from one body to another and back again, just as one switches tools. However, such transfer is not observed in reality; the soul remains attached to one body throughout life and after death does not migrate to another body. Therefore, the claim that the soul is a simple substance neither body nor bodily (jismānī) is rendered problematic (mushkil). The author then preempts a potential objection: it is not rationally impossible that each soul and each body have a unique property (khāṣṣiyya) such that a particular soul is only suited for a particular body and vice versa. This would explain why transfer does not occur. Finally, the author humbly states that human intellects can never fully comprehend the secrets of God's creations, implying that the ultimate truth about the soul's nature lies beyond human reason. The passage thus does not definitively reject the soul's simplicity but highlights a difficulty and leaves the matter to divine knowledge.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author only says the claim becomes 'problematic' (mushkil) and then offers a possible explanation that preserves separateness via unique properties.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The lack of observed soul transfer between bodies challenges the claim of separateness, but unique soul-body properties offer a rational alternative.

Refuting the Sensory Localization Objection
Objection-and-answer: author refutes the claim that sensory localization implies a corporeal soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the corporeality objection by showing that the same reasoning would lead to absurdity and that rational intuition distinguishes between the agent and the instrument.

Core Idea
Sensory localization does not prove the soul is corporeal; the organ is just an instrument.

The objection that sight is specific to the eye and hearing to the ear seems intuitive. But the author challenges this by turning the argument against itself and appealing to a deeper rational intuition. Let's see how he skillfully undermines the corporeality objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent claims that since sight is localized in the eye and hearing in the ear, the powers of perception must be corporeal and located in these organs.
2
Examine the first refutation
The author argues that by the same logic, knowledge and perception would be located in the heart or brain, which would imply that the soul is corporeal—contradicting the opponent's own position of affirming an incorporeal soul.
This is a self-defeating argument.
3
Examine the second refutation
The author appeals to rational intuition: the human being is the seer, not the eye. The eye is merely an instrument for sight.
The relationship between organ and faculty is not settled; it requires further proof.
4
Draw the conclusion
The objection fails to prove corporeality because the organ's role is not necessarily that of the agent; it could be a tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه أولاً : وهو قولهم : « إنا نجد الإبصار مختصاً بالعين ، والسماع مختصاً بالأذن ، من وجوه : الأول : إنا نقول : كما أنا نجد هذه الحالة ، فكذلك نجد الإدراك والعلم من ناحية القلب أو الدماغ، على اختلاف المذهبين فيه . فإن دل ما ذكرتم على أن محل القوة الباصرة هو العين، وعلى أن محل القوة السامعة هو الأذن ، وجب أن يدل ما ذكرناه على أن محل العلم والإدراك هو القلب . وحينئذ يبطل القول بإثبات النفس . الثاني : إن العقلاء ببدائه عقولهم [ يعلمون ] أنه ليس المبصر هو العين ، ولا السامع هو الأذن ، ولا المتكلم هو الحنجرة . بل الإنسان هو المبصر بالعين ، وهو السامع بالأذن، وهو المتكلم بالحنجرة . فعلمنا : أنه ليس الأمر كما زعموا من أن العلم البديهي حاصل بأن الرائي هو العين ، والسامع هو الأذن . بل العلم البديهي حاصل بأن للعين اعتباراً في حصول الإبصار . فأما إنها هي المبصرة ، أو هي آلة للإبصار ، فذلك متوقف فيه إلى قيام الدليل .
And the answer to what they mentioned first: which is their statement: 'We find sight specific to the eye, and hearing specific to the ear.' From aspects: First: We say: Just as we find this state, so we find perception and knowledge from the direction of the heart or the brain, according to the difference of opinions therein. For if what you mentioned indicates that the locus of the seeing power is the eye, and that the locus of the hearing power is the ear, then what we mentioned must indicate that the locus of knowledge and perception is the heart. And then the claim of affirming the soul is invalidated. Second: Rational people by the intuitions of their intellects know that the seer is not the eye, nor the hearer the ear, nor the speaker the larynx. Rather, the human is the seer by the eye, and the hearer by the ear, and the speaker by the larynx. So we know that the matter is not as they claimed—that intuitive knowledge is obtained that the seer is the eye and the hearer is the ear. Rather, intuitive knowledge is obtained that the eye has a consideration in the occurrence of sight. As for whether it is the seer, or is an instrument for sight, that is suspended until the establishment of proof.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues: since sight is specific to the eye and hearing to the ear, these faculties must be corporeal (located in these organs). The author answers with two points. First: by the same logic, knowledge and perception are found in the heart or brain, so the locus of knowledge would be the heart, which would actually negate the existence of an incorporeal soul (since the heart is corporeal). This undermines the opponent's own position. Second: rational intuition shows that the eye is not the seer; rather, the human being sees by means of the eye. The eye is an instrument, not the agent. Thus the objection from sensory specialization fails to prove that the perceiving power is corporeal. The author emphasizes that the relationship of the eye to sight is not decisive—it could be the seer or merely an instrument—and this requires separate proof.

Key Terms
القوة الباصرة
القوة السامعة
إثبات النفس
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies that the eye has any role in sight.
Correction: The author affirms that the eye has consideration ('اعتبار') in sight, but whether it is the seer or an instrument requires further proof.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that the specificity of sight to the eye proves the seeing power is corporeal?
A) By arguing that the eye is not involved in sight at all.
B) By arguing that the same reasoning would make the heart the locus of knowledge, which invalidates the soul.
C) By arguing that the eye is the seer itself.
D) By arguing that the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The objection from sensory localization is refuted by showing it leads to absurdity and by distinguishing between the organ as instrument and the agent as the human soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Organ Damage Objection
Author refutes organ damage objection by distinguishing three causes of impairment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that organ damage impairs function does not prove that the power is corporeal, and how the author distinguishes between agent, instrument, and condition.

Core Idea
Organ damage only proves impairment, not location of the power.

The objector argues that when an organ is damaged, its function fails, so the power must be in that organ. But the author shows this reasoning confuses the agent with the instrument. In this passage, he clarifies that impairment can come from damage to the instrument or conditions, not just the agent. Let's read his concise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection raised, then 'we say' introducing the answer.
The objection is about organ damage causing functional impairment.
2
Identify the three factors
The author lists three reasons for impairment: agent, instrument, and condition. Find each term in Arabic and understand its role.
الفاعل = agent, الآلة = instrument, الشرط = condition.
3
Understand why the inference is fallacious
The objector concluded that the power is the organ itself. But the author shows that impairment can be due to instrument or condition, so the conclusion is not necessary. Ask yourself: does damage to the instrument imply the agent is damaged?
This refutes the objection that function loss proves identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه ثانياً من أن الآفة إذا حلت في عضو، اختل ذلك الفعل : فهو أن نقول : كما يختل الفعل لاختلال الفاعل، فقد يختل أيضاً لاختلال الآلة ولاختلال ما يجري مجرى الشرط الحصول الفعل . فالاستدلال بما ذكرتم على كونه فاعلاً : خطأ .
And the answer to what they mentioned second: that if a defect settles in an organ, that function becomes impaired. So it is that we say: just as the function is impaired due to the impairment of the agent, it may also be impaired due to the impairment of the instrument and due to the impairment of what serves as a condition for the occurrence of the function. So inferring from what you mentioned that it is an agent is an error.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues: when an organ (e.g., the eye) is damaged, its corresponding function (e.g., sight) is impaired. This proves that the power of sight is located in the eye and is therefore corporeal. The author answers by distinguishing between the agent (الفاعل) and the instrument (الآلة) as well as conditions (الشرط). Impairment of a function can result from damage to the agent, the instrument, or a necessary condition. Therefore, damage to the eye does not prove that the power of sight is the eye itself; it could merely be damage to the instrument. Hence the argument is fallacious.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an organ is damaged and the function fails, the power must be located in that organ and is therefore corporeal.
Correction: The failure could be due to damage to the instrument or a necessary condition, not necessarily the agent itself.

Checkpoint

B) The instrument and the condition
C) Only the condition
D) Neither the instrument nor the condition

Key Takeaway
The organ damage objection fails because it mistakes the instrument for the agent; functional impairment can arise from damage to either the agent, instrument, or a necessary condition.

Refuting the Animal Souls Objection
Refutation of the animal incorporeality objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that animal souls must be incorporeal like human souls, using two logical arguments about quiddity and negative qualifications.

Core Idea
Shared abstractness does not imply identical essences; quiddity differs.

The opponent argues that if human souls are incorporeal, then animal souls, sharing similar functions, would also be incorporeal. This would have unwelcome implications. The author responds with two clear arguments, showing that abstractness is a negative attribute and that sharing effects does not entail identity of essence. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the answer structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the two aspects of the refutation.
Notice that the author presents two distinct arguments.
2
Understand the first argument: lack of proof and negative qualification
The first argument says the opponent has no proof that animal souls cannot be abstract. Even if they are, abstractness is a negative qualification, so equality in that doesn't mean total identity.
Key term: 'التجرد قيد عدمي' meaning 'abstractness is a negative qualification'.
3
Understand the second argument: different quiddities can share effects
The second argument posits that animal souls could be corporeal powers while human souls are abstract, and they can share some concomitants and effects without being identical in essence.
This counters the assumption that similar functions imply similar nature.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why this refutation matters. It preserves the distinction between human and animal souls while allowing for shared attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه ثالثاً من أنه يلزم من أن تكون نفوس سائر الحيوانات نفوساً مجردة : من وجهين : الأول : إنهم ما ذكروا دليلاً على أنه يمتنع أن تكون نفوس الحيوانات نفوساً مجردة . ثم لا يلزم من هذا القدر، كونها مساوية للنفوس البشرية في تمام الماهية. لأن التجرد قيد عدمي والاستواء فيه لا يوجب التماثل في تمام الماهية . الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : نفوس سائر الحيوانات قوى جسمانية ونفوس البشر نفوس مجردة . ثم إنهما وإن كانتا مختلفتين بالماهية إلا أنهما يتشاركان في بعض اللوازم والآثار. لما ثبت : أن الاستواء في الصفات والآثار لا يدل على التماثل في الماهية ؟
And the answer to what they mentioned third: that it would follow that the souls of all other animals would be abstract souls. From two aspects: First: They did not mention a proof that it is impossible for the souls of animals to be abstract souls. Then from this much, it does not follow that they are equal to human souls in complete quiddity. Because abstractness is a negative qualification, and equality in it does not necessitate similarity in complete quiddity. Second: Why is it not permissible to say: The souls of other animals are corporeal powers and the souls of humans are abstract souls. Then, even if they differ in quiddity, they share in some concomitants and effects, because it is established that equality in attributes and effects does not indicate similarity in quiddity.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues: if human souls are incorporeal, then animal souls (which share similar functions) would also be incorporeal. This leads to an undesirable consequence according to the opponent. The author answers with two points. First: the opponent has not proven that animal souls cannot be abstract. Even if they were abstract, abstractness is a negative attribute (absence of corporeality), and equality in that does not entail complete likeness in essence (الماهية). Second: it is possible that animal souls are corporeal powers while human souls are abstract; they can share certain effects without being identical in essence. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
نفوس مجردة
قوى جسمانية
تمام الماهية
التجرد قيد عدمي
اللوازم والآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share an attribute (like abstractness), they must be identical in essence.
Correction: Abstractness is a negative qualification, and sharing negative attributes does not entail identity. Also, sharing effects does not imply identical quiddity.

Checkpoint
What is the author's second argument in answering the animal souls objection?
A) Animal souls and human souls are identical in quiddity.
B) Animal souls are necessarily abstract because they share effects.
C) It is possible for animal souls to be corporeal powers while human souls are abstract, sharing some effects.
D) The opponent has proven animal souls are abstract.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that abstractness is a negative qualification and that shared effects do not imply identical essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson argues that negative qualifications (like 'not material') don't make two things the same. In your own life, where have you equated what someone lacks with what they are, rather than seeking their true essence? Commit to one concrete change.
