Refuting the Objection of Form Reception
Author refutes the objection against abstract soul perceiving forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that an abstract soul cannot receive sensory forms by exposing its reliance on a flawed theory of perception.

Core Idea
The objection fails because its premise about perception is false.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that claims an abstract soul cannot receive physical forms like the form of a sphere. This objection, if true, would undermine the soul's ability to perceive. The author provides a concise refutation by pointing out the flaw in the underlying theory of perception and turning the objection back on the objectors.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the objection (fourth in a list) and the author's two-part answer.
The objection is that an abstract substance cannot have forms impressed upon it.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The author says the objection assumes perception requires a physical impression. He claims this theory has already been proven false. Reflect on why this matters.
If the theory of physical impression is false, the objection loses its foundation.
3
Understand the second part of the answer
The author adds that the same difficulty applies to the objectors themselves. Ask: Why would this be a problem for them?
This turns the table: if perception requires physical impression, their own view faces the same objection.

Source Text

Commentary

Therefore, the soul cannot be abstract. The author answers by pointing out that this objection is based on the theory that perception requires a physical impression (like a seal on wax). But that theory has already been shown to be false (presumably earlier in the work). Moreover, the author notes that this same difficulty (how an abstract substance perceives) actually applies to the opponents' own view as well, turning the objection back on them.

Key Takeaway

The Second Argument: Common Sense Knows the Seer is Not the Eye
The author presents the second argument that common sense knows the seer is not the eye.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the soul's existence, which clarifies that common sense only shows the eye's involvement in sight, not that the eye is the seer.

Core Idea
Common sense does not tell us that the eye sees; it only tells us that the eye is involved.

This passage presents the second argument in a series defending the soul's existence. The author argues that our basic intuitions distinguish between the agent of perception and the instrument. He challenges the materialist claim that intuitive knowledge identifies the eye as the seer, showing that true intuitive knowledge only affirms the eye's role, not its agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses three examples: seeing, hearing, speaking.
2
Identify the refuted claim
Find the claim that the author says is false: that intuitive knowledge tells us the seer is the eye. Recognize that he is pushing back against a materialist assumption.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that intuitive knowledge only tells us the eye has a role in sight, but whether it is the seer or an instrument is undecided without proof. Reflect on why this matters for the soul's existence.
This is a key point: the true agent is the human, not the organ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن العقلاء ببدائه عقولهم يعلمون أنه ليس المبصر هو العين ، ولا السامع هو الأذن ، ولا المتكلم هو الحنجرة . بل الإنسان هو المبصر بالعين ، وهو السامع بالأذن، وهو المتكلم بالحنجرة . فعلمنا : أنه ليس الأمر كما زعموا من أن العلم البديهي حاصل بأن الرائي هو العين ، والسامع هو الأذن . بل العلم البديهي حاصل بأن للعين اعتباراً في حصول الإبصار . فأما إنها هي المبصرة ، أو هي آلة للإبصار ، فذلك متوقف فيه إلى قيام الدليل .
The second: Indeed, rational people, by the primary intuitions of their intellects, know that the seer is not the eye, nor the hearer the ear, nor the speaker the throat. Rather, the human is the seer by the eye, and the hearer by the ear, and the speaker by the throat. So we know that the matter is not as they claimed that intuitive knowledge is attained that the seer is the eye and the hearer is the ear. Rather, intuitive knowledge is attained that the eye has a consideration in the occurrence of sight. As for whether it is the seer or an instrument for sight, that is suspended until the establishment of proof.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument in a series defending the existence and nature of the soul. The author begins by asserting that rational people, using the primary intuitions of their intellects (badā'iḥ al-ʻuqūl), know that the seer is not the eye, the hearer is not the ear, and the speaker is not the throat. Instead, it is the human being who sees through the eye, hears through the ear, and speaks through the throat. From this, the author concludes that the common claim—that intuitive knowledge (al-ʻilm al-badīhī) tells us the seer is the eye and the hearer is the ear—is false. True intuitive knowledge only tells us that the eye has a role (iʻtibār) in the process of sight. Whether the eye is itself the seer or merely an instrument (āla) for sight cannot be decided without additional proof. Thus, this argument clarifies the limits of intuitive knowledge, refuting the materialist claim that the organs are the true agents of perception.

Key Terms
العقلاء
بدائه العقول
العلم البديهي
المبصر
الآلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Common sense directly tells us that the eye is the seer.
Correction: The author argues that common sense only tells us the eye has a role in sight, not that it is the seer. The true agent is the human being.

Checkpoint

A) That the eye is the seer.
B) That the eye is an instrument.
C) That the eye has a role in sight, but not whether it is the seer or instrument.
D) That the eye is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Common sense alone cannot determine whether the eye is the actual seer or merely an instrument; that requires further proof.

Answer to the Objection from Organ Damage
The author answers the objection that organ damage proves the organ is the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from organ damage does not prove that the organ is the agent of action, and how the same evidence can be explained by damage to the instrument or condition.

Core Idea
Organ damage does not prove the organ is the agent—it could be the instrument or condition.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that tries to use the physical fact of organ damage to argue that the organ itself is the doer of actions. The objection seems plausible at first, but the author shows that it commits a logical error by ignoring alternative explanations. Let us see how he dismantles this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's concise response.
2
Identify the three factors
The author mentions three things that can cause an action to become disordered: the agent, the instrument, and the condition. Notice how he lists them.
These are the key terms: الفاعل, الآلة, الشرط.
3
Understand the logical error
The objection assumes that because organ damage affects the action, the organ must be the agent. But the author shows that damage to the instrument or condition can also impair the action, so the evidence does not uniquely point to the organ as agent.
This is a fallacy of affirming the consequent.
4
Connect to the larger issue
Consider why this matters: if the organ were the agent, it would imply that the soul is not the true agent. The author defends the soul's role by showing that organ damage is not evidence against it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه ثانياً من أن الآفة إذا حلت في عضو، اختل ذلك الفعل : فهو أن نقول : كما يختل الفعل لاختلال الفاعل، فقد يختل أيضاً لاختلال الآلة ولاختلال ما يجري مجرى الشرط حصول الفعل . فالاستدلال بما ذكرتم على كونه فاعلاً : خطأ
And the answer to what they mentioned secondly, that if an affliction occurs in an organ, that action becomes disordered: so it is that we say: just as the action becomes disordered due to the disorder of the agent, it may also become disordered due to the disorder of the instrument and due to the disorder of what serves as the condition for the occurrence of the action. So drawing evidence on its being an agent from what you mentioned is an error.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection raised by opponents. The objection argued that if an organ is afflicted with a disease, the corresponding action becomes impaired, proving that the organ is the doer (fāʻil) of the action. The author answers by saying that the impairment of an action can be due to the agent's failure, but it can also be due to the failure of the instrument (āla) or of a condition (sharṭ) necessary for the action. Therefore, using the observed correlation between organ damage and loss of function as evidence that the organ is the agent is logically invalid (khaṭaʼ). This shows that the argument from organ damage does not disprove the soul's role as the true agent.

Key Terms
الْآفَة
الْفَاعِل
الْآلَة
الشَّرْط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Organ damage proves that the organ is the agent of the action.
Correction: The author shows that the same evidence can be explained by damage to the instrument or condition, so it does not uniquely prove organ agency.

Checkpoint

A) Because the organ might be the agent but also be affected.
B) Because the action can also be impaired by damage to the instrument or condition.
C) Because the soul is unaffected by organ damage.
D) Because the action is not truly impaired.

Key Takeaway
Organ damage alone does not prove the organ is the agent, because the same effect can result from damage to the instrument or condition.

Answer to the Objection About Animal Souls Being Separate
Author answers objection about animal souls being separate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that animal souls would also have to be separate substances, using two key arguments about the nature of abstraction and the limits of shared attributes.

Core Idea
Abstraction alone does not make two souls identical; it is a negative attribute.

The objection claims that if human souls are separate substances, then animal souls must be too, leading to absurd consequences. The author counters with a two-pronged response. First, the objectors haven't proven it's impossible for animal souls to be separate. Second, even if they were separate, that doesn't make them identical to human souls, because abstraction is a negative attribute and shared qualities do not imply identical essences. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation to understand the objection and the two responses.
Pay attention to the structure: 'first' and 'second'.
2
Identify the two responses
The author gives two distinct responses to the objection. Locate them and note how they are introduced.
3
Understand 'qayd ʻadamī'
The first response introduces the concept of tajarrad (abstraction) as a negative attribute (qayd ʻadamī). Think about why equality in a negative attribute does not require identical essence.
A negative attribute is something that is absent, like 'not being physical'.
4
Reflect on shared attributes
The second response states that two different essences can share some concomitants and effects. Reflect on examples from everyday experience where different things share some properties but are not identical.
5
Review the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding of the first response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه ثالثاً من أنه يلزم من أن تكون نفوس سائر الحيوانات نفوساً مجردة : من وجهين : الأول : إنهم ما ذكروا دليلا على أنه يمتنع أن تكون نفوس الحيوانات نفوساً مجردة . ثم لا يلزم من هذا القدر ، كونها مساوية للنفوس البشرية في تمام الماهية. لأن التجرد قيد عدمي والاستواء فيه لا يوجب التماثل في تمام الماهية . الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : نفوس سائر الحيوانات قوى جسمانية ونفوس البشر نفوس مجردة . ثم إنهما وإن كانتا مختلفتين بالماهية إلا أنهما يتشاركان في بعض اللوازم والآثار. لما ثبت : أن الاستواء في الصفات والآثار لا يدل على التماثل في الماهية ؟
And the answer to what they mentioned thirdly, that it would necessitate that the souls of all animals are separate souls: in two respects: First: They did not mention evidence that it is impossible for the souls of animals to be separate souls. Then from this much it does not necessitate their being equal to human souls in complete essence, because abstraction (tajarrud) is a negative attribute and equality in it does not require similarity in complete essence. Second: Why is it not permissible to say that the souls of animals are physical powers and the souls of humans are separate souls? Then, even if they differ in essence, they share in some concomitants and effects, because it is established that equality in attributes and effects does not indicate similarity in essence.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the third objection, which claims that if human souls are separate (mujarrada) substances, then animal souls would also have to be separate, leading to an absurdity. The author provides two responses. First, the objectors did not prove that it is impossible for animal souls to be separate. Moreover, even if animal souls were separate, this does not make them identical to human souls in essence, because abstraction (tajarrud) is a negative attribute (qayd ʻadamī) and equality in that does not imply full similarity (tamāthul) in complete essence. Second, it is permissible to hold that animal souls are physical powers (quwā jismāniyya) while human souls are separate. Even if they differ in essence, they can share some concomitants (lawāzim) and effects (āthār). The fact that two things share some qualities or effects does not prove they are identical in essence. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
النَّفْس
جَوْهَر مُجَرَّد
التَّجَرُّد
قَيْد عَدَمِي
التَّمَاثُل
الْمَاهِيَّة
اللَّوَازِم
الْآثَار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If souls are abstract, all souls must be identical.
Correction: Abstraction is a negative attribute (lack of physicality). Equality in a negative attribute does not imply identical essence, because two things can share a lack without being alike in their positive nature.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the third objection, what is the first point the author makes about animal souls?
A) They are definitely physical.
B) They are impossible to be separate.
C) The objectors did not prove that animal souls cannot be separate, and even if they are, that does not make them identical to human souls.
D) Animal souls are lower in rank.

Key Takeaway
Shared negative attributes do not make two things identical; the essence of a thing is more than just its lack of certain properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that two people are essentially the same because they share a common lack—like both being non-smokers or both not having anger issues? How does the author’s point about a negative attribute not implying identical essence challenge that assumption? Will you now look beyond what is absent to see their unique positive traits?
?
When you see someone performing the same outward actions as you (praying, fasting, giving charity), do you sometimes think your hearts must be identical before God? How does the lesson’s reminder—that equality in effects does not prove equal essence—force you to examine your own inner reality rather than comparing externals? What concrete step can you take today to focus on your soul’s genuine state before Allah?

Refuting the Objection on Image Reception by a Simple Substance
The author answers the objection that a simple substance cannot receive images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that a simple soul cannot receive images is invalid and how the author refutes it by denying the premise and turning it against the objectors.

Core Idea
Perception does not require physical impression; the objection refutes itself.

A common objection against the soul's ability to know is that a simple, non-composite substance cannot receive images or forms. The author of al-Matālib al-Āliyah tackles this head-on, showing that the objection rests on a mistaken theory of perception. Moreover, he points out that the same objection would embarrass the objectors themselves when they consider universal concepts. Let's examine his concise response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist of the objection and the two-part answer.
The objection is that a simple substance cannot have images impressed in it.
2
Understand the first refutation
The author says the objection is based on the theory that perception requires impression (irtisām). He claims this theory has been shown false. Ask: why would that be a fatal flaw?
If the premise is false, the objection collapses.
3
Understand the second refutation
The author adds that the same objection would also apply to the perception of universal concepts (e.g., universal sphere). If a simple substance cannot receive images, then even opponents cannot explain how they know universals.
This shows the objection proves too much and thus is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه رابعاً من أن النفس التي هي جوهر مجرد، يمتنع ترتسم فيها صورة الكرة . هو أن هذا بناء على أن الإدراك لا يتم إلا بارتسام صورة المعلوم في العالم وقد بان بطلانه . وأيضاً : فهذا الإشكال وارد عليهم في إدراك الكرة الكلية ، والمخروط الكلي
And the answer to what they mentioned fourthly, that the soul, which is a separate substance, cannot have the image of a sphere impressed in it: That is based on the fact that perception is only completed by the impression of the image of the known object in something, and its falsity has become evident. Also, this objection applies to them in the perception of the universal sphere and the universal cone.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth objection: if the soul is a simple, separate substance (jawhar mujarrad), it cannot receive a physical image like that of a sphere, because a simple substance cannot have parts to host the image. The author retorts that this objection is based on the false assumption that perception requires the impression (irtisām) of the image of the known object in the perceiver. That theory has been shown to be false. Furthermore, the same objection would apply to the objectors themselves when they perceive universal (kullī) concepts like the universal sphere or universal cone, which have no physical form. So the objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms
جَوْهَر مُجَرَّد
الِارْتِسَام
الْإِدْرَاك
الْكُلِّي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a simple substance (like the soul) cannot know material objects because it cannot receive physical images.
Correction: The author clarifies that perception does not require physical impression; it is a different kind of cognition. Moreover, even if impression were needed, the same problem would apply to universal concepts, showing the objection is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What false premise does the author identify in the fourth objection?
A) That the soul is composite.
B) That perception requires the impression of an image in the perceiver.
C) That the soul cannot perceive universals.
D) That the sphere is a physical object.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a simple soul cannot receive images fails because it falsely assumes perception requires physical impression, and it would also undermine the perception of universals, refuting itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The objectors themselves perceived universals—forms without physical shape. If you reject the soul's ability to receive non-physical knowledge, you deny your own everyday experience. What universal concepts (justice, truth, beauty) do you rely on daily, yet fail to realize they are non-physical? How will you remind yourself that your soul is equipped for such realities, starting today?

The Eye as an Instrument: Defending the Soul's Role in Vision
Author answers objection about distance and vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the soul as the true seer by distinguishing the eye as an instrument whose state affects vision.

Core Idea
The eye is a tool, not the seer; variation in vision is due to the tool, not the soul.

The objection claims that if the soul were the seer, vision would not vary with distance, since the soul is not physical. The author responds by clarifying the role of the eye: it is not the seer but an instrument (āla) for sight. Just as a tool affects the outcome of an action, the eye's condition influences vision, while the soul remains the true agent. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, noting the structure: first the objection, then the answer with 'we say'.
Notice the logical connector 'an naqūla' signals the author's reply.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms for 'seer', 'soul', 'eye', and 'instrument'. Understand how they relate in the argument.
The author uses 'rā'ī' (seer), 'nafs' (soul), 'ʿayn' (eye), 'āla' (instrument).
3
Analyze the author's reasoning
The author concedes that the eye is not the seer, but says it is an instrument. Then he argues that variation due to distance is not unlikely because instruments can affect outcomes.
This is a key move: distinguishing the agent from the instrument.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that variation proves the eye is the seer. The lesson corrects this by showing that instrument variation is expected.
This is the heart of the author's defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه خامساً : من أنه لو كان الرائي هو النفس، لوجب أن لا يختلف حال الرؤية، بحسب القرب والبعد : أن نقول : العين وإن لم تكن هي الرائية، إلا أنها آلة في حصول الإبصار ولا يبعد أن يختلف حال الإبصار بسبب اختلاف الحال في القرب والبعد بالنسبة إلى الآلة .
And the answer to what they mentioned fifthly, that if the seer were the soul, it would be necessary that the state of vision not differ according to distance and proximity: we say: the eye, even if it is not the seer, is an instrument in the occurrence of sight, and it is not far-fetched that the state of sight differs due to the difference in the state of distance and proximity relative to the instrument.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage answers the fifth objection: if the soul is the seer, then vision should not vary with distance, since the soul is not subject to physical changes. The author replies by clarifying that the eye is not the seer but the instrument (āla) for sight. It is not improbable (lā yubʻad) that the state of sight differs due to the condition of the instrument. Just as a tool can affect the outcome of an action, the physical state of the eye (distance, health) can affect the visual experience, while the true agent (the soul) remains constant. Thus, the objection is unsound.

Key Terms
الرائي
النفس
الآلة
الإبصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If vision varies with distance, the eye must be the seer because the soul is not affected by distance.
Correction: Variation is due to the eye as an instrument; the soul is still the seer, but the instrument's state affects the visual experience.

Checkpoint

A) By denying that vision varies.
B) By claiming the soul is not the seer.
C) By saying the eye is an instrument and its state affects vision.
D) By saying distance does not affect the soul.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the soul as the true seer by showing that the eye is an instrument, and variation in vision is due to the instrument, not the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Objection of Imagining a Winged Square
Author enumerates two points to refute the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection about imagining a winged square by showing that it does not prove the need for a physical substrate and actually supports the immaterial soul.

Core Idea
Imagination handles universals and multiple images without needing a physical medium.

The author addresses the sixth objection, which claims that imagining a composite image like a winged square requires a physical basis. He provides two arguments to show that this objection fails. The first turns the tables by showing that if we can imagine a specific winged square, we can also imagine a universal one, forcing the opponent to accept that the body perceives universals. The second demonstrates that the physical seat of imagination is small, yet we imagine large images, implying multiple images can coexist without issue. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two points
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice that the author gives two distinct arguments (الوجه الأول and الثاني).
The passage is a refutation, so each point undermines the opponent's claim.
2
Understand the first point: from specific to universal
See how the author argues that if we can imagine a specific winged square, we can also imagine a universal one. This implies the body would have to perceive universals, which is absurd.
This point forces the opponent to either deny the ability to imagine universals or accept a bodily perceiver of universals.
3
Understand the second point: the small brain argument
The brain is small, yet we imagine large things like a mountain. The author explores two possibilities: either parts of the image are not impressed, or all parts are impressed in the same spot.
Both possibilities lead to contradictions for the opponent's position.
4
Conclude the overall refutation
See how both points show that the ability to imagine does not require a physical medium, thus the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه سادساً : من تخيل المربع المجنح بالمربعين : من وجوه : الأول : إنه كما يمكننا أن نتخيل مربعاً مجنحاً بمربعين متساويين على سبيل التعيين فكذلك يمكننا تخيل مثل هذا المربع على سبيل الكلية، فيلزم أن يكون المدرك للكليات أيضاً جسماً وعلى هذا التقدير فصاحب الإدراكات الكلية الجزئية هو البدن، فيلزمكم نفي النفس . الثاني : إن موضع التخيل من الدماغ شيء صغير . فإذا انطبعت فيه صورة الجبل فقد انطبع في ذلك الجسم الصغير، جزء من أجزاء تلك الصورة الكبيرة، فالذي يبقى من الصورة الكبيرة، إن لم يرتسم في شيء أصلاً فقد جوزتم أن لا ترتسم الصورة الخيالية في شيء أصلا . وإذا جاز ذلك فجون وال) في كل جزء من أجزاء تلك الصورة مثله . وحينئذ يبطل دليلكم ؟ وإن قلتم : إن بقية أجزاء تلك الصورة الكبيرة ترتسم في ذلك الموضع بعينه، فحينئذ قد اجتمعت خيالات أجزاء تلك الصورة الكبيرة في المحل الواحد، مع أنا نميز بينها بحسب الخيال. وهذا يدل على أن اجتماع الصورة الكبيرة في المحل الواحد، لا يمنع من اقتدار الخيال على التميز بينها . وإذا جاز هذا، فلم لا يجوز ارتسام المربعين المجنحين في محل واحد، مع أنه يبقى الامتياز في الإدراك ؟
And the answer to what they mentioned sixthly, concerning the imagination of a winged square by two squares: in points: First: Just as we can imagine a winged square by two equal squares in a specific way, we can similarly imagine such a square in a universal way, so it would necessitate that the perceiver of universals is also a body, and on this estimation the possessor of universal and particular perceptions is the body, thus necessitating the denial of the soul. Second: The place of imagination in the brain is something small. So if the image of a mountain is impressed in it, there is impressed in that small body a part of the parts of that large image. Then what remains of the large image, if it is not impressed in anything at all, then you have allowed that the imaginary image is not impressed in anything at all. And if that is allowed, then (?) in every part of that image similarly. Then your evidence would be invalid. And if you say: The remaining parts of that large image are impressed in that same place, then the imaginations of the parts of that large image have gathered in one place, while we distinguish between them in imagination. This indicates that the gathering of the large image in one place does not prevent the power of imagination from distinguishing between them. And if this is allowed, why is it not allowed for the impression of the two winged squares in one place, while discrimination remains in perception?
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage answers the sixth objection concerning the ability to imagine a winged square composed of two squares. The objection likely argued that such a mental image requires a physical medium. The author gives two points. First, if we can imagine a specific winged square, we can also imagine a universal winged square, which would require the perceiver of universals to be a body, leading to the absurdity that the body perceives universals and thus the soul is unnecessary. Second, the physical seat of imagination in the brain is very small, yet we can imagine a large mountain. The image of the mountain cannot be entirely impressed in that small space unless we allow that only parts are impressed. If parts are not impressed, then the whole image is not impressed, which would invalidate the need for impression altogether. If the opponent says all parts are impressed in the same spot, then we have multiple images in one place, which is possible since we can distinguish them. Thus, there is no problem with two square images (the winged square) being impressed in the same place while still being distinguishable.

Key Terms
التَّخَيُّل
الْمُرَبَّع الْمُجَنَّح
الْمُدْرِك
الْكُلِّيَات
الدِّمَاغ
الْخَيَال
الِامْتِيَاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imagining a complex image like a winged square requires a physical medium in the brain to hold all parts simultaneously.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to either no impression needed or multiple impressions in one place, which is possible, so no physical medium is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the first point of the answer, if we can imagine a specific winged square, what else must we be able to imagine?
A) A square with three wings.
B) A universal winged square.
C) A winged square in a dream.
D) A mountain.

Key Takeaway
The objection from imagining a winged square fails because it does not necessitate a physical substrate; instead, it supports the immaterial nature of the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a memory of a vast landscape, do you feel that image is physically stored in your brain? After reading this lesson, how do you reconcile the smallness of your brain with the vastness of your imagination? Does that cause you to reconsider what you truly are?

The opponent assumed that imagining a winged square requires a physical basis. In what areas of your life do you assume that your spiritual or intellectual abilities are limited by your physical body? Will you continue to let that assumption hold you back?
?
If the soul can perceive universals and complex images without a physical medium, then what does it mean for you to be a soul? How should that understanding change your priorities in this life?

The Eye as an Instrument: Answering the Distance Objection
The author answers the fifth objection that if the soul sees, vision would not vary with distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's role as the true perceiver can be reconciled with the fact that vision varies with distance, because the eye serves as an instrument whose condition affects sight.

Core Idea
The eye is an instrument; the soul is the true seer.

This lesson presents the author's response to an objection against the soul being the perceiver. The objection argues that if the soul truly sees, then vision should not change based on distance. The author clarifies that the eye, though not the seer itself, functions as an instrument for sight. Just as an instrument affects the outcome, the eye's condition can explain why vision differs with proximity and distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author first states the objection and then gives the answer.
The passage begins with a fragment from a previous discussion; the main answer starts at 'والجواب عما ذكروه خامساً'.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'النفس' (soul), 'الرائي' (seer), 'الإبصار' (sight), and 'آلة' (instrument). Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Understand the role of an instrument
The author draws an analogy: the eye is like a tool. A tool does not perform the action itself but is used by the agent. Similarly, the soul uses the eyes to see, and the condition of the eyes affects the quality of vision.
This analogy refutes the objection that variation disproves the soul's role.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument strengthens the position that the soul is the true perceiver. The variability of sight does not undermine the soul's agency; it simply shows that instruments have limitations.
This answer preserves the unity of the perceiver while explaining empirical observations.

This passage provides the answer to the fifth objection against the soul being the perceiver. The objection states that if the soul itself is the seer, then vision should not vary with distance. First Argument: Universal Winged Square
The author presents the first argument: imaginary universals imply corporeal perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ability to imagine a universal winged square leads to the absurdity that the body perceives universals, which would negate the soul.

Core Idea
Imagining universals shows that imagination cannot be purely corporeal.

The first argument of the author's reply tackles the objection head-on. If imagining a specific winged square proves corporeality, then imagining a universal winged square would also be corporeal, leading to the unacceptable conclusion that the body perceives universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without stopping. Notice how the author starts with a conditional statement.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the logical structure
The author argues: if imagining a specific winged square implies imagination is corporeal, then the same would apply to a universal winged square. That would make the body the perceiver of universals, which contradicts the opponent's own belief in the soul.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why the opponent cannot accept that the body perceives universals. This undermines their objection.
The opponent wants to prove the soul is corporeal, but this argument shows their method leads to denying the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه سادساً : من تخيل المربع المجنح بالمربعين : من وجوه : الأول : إنه كما يمكننا أن نتخيل مربعاً مجنحاً بمربعين متساويين على سبيل التعيين فكذلك يمكننا تخيل مثل هذا المربع على سبيل الكلية ، فيلزم أن يكون المدرك للكليات أيضاً جسماً وعلى هذا التقدير فصاحب الإدراكات الكلية الجزئية هو البدن ، فيلزمكم نفي النفس الثاني : إن موضع التخيل من الدماغ شيء صغير . فإذا انطبعت فيه صورة الجبل فقد انطبع في ذلك الجسم الصغير ، جزء من أجزاء تلك الصورة الكبيرة ، فالذي يبقى من الصورة الكبيرة ، إن لم يرتسم في شيء أصلاً فقد جوزتم أن لا ترتسم الصورة الخيالية في شيء أصلا . وإذا جاز ذلك فجوزوا في كل جزء من أجزاء تلك الصورة مثله . وحينئذ يبطل دليلكم ؟ وإن قلتم : إن بقية أجزاء تلك الصورة الكبيرة ترتسم في ذلك الموضع بعينه ، فحينئذ قد اجتمعت خيالات أجزاء تلك الصورة الكبيرة في المحل الواحد ، مع أنا نميز بينها بحسب الخيال. وهذا يدل على أن اجتماع الصورة الكبيرة في المحل الواحد ، لا يمنع من اقتدار الخيال على التميز بينها . وإذا جاز هذا ، فلم لا يجوز ارتسام المربعين المجنحين في محل واحد ، مع أنه يبقى الامتياز في الإدراك ؟ الثالث : إن الإنسان ربما طاف في العالم ، ورأى البلاد والجبال والبحار والمفاوز ، فلو وجب أن يحصل لكل جزء من أجزاء هذه الصورة جزء معين من الدماغ ، فهذا بعيد . لأن الجزء الصغير من الدماغ لا يفي بذلك ، وإن لم يجب هذا الامتياز فقد بطل قولكم .
And the answer to what they mentioned sixthly: about imagining a square with wings by two squares: from several aspects: The first: just as we can imagine a winged square by two equal squares specifically, so too we can imagine such a square universally, which would necessitate that the perceiver of universals is also a body, and on this assumption the possessor of universal and particular perceptions is the body, so you must deny the soul. The second: the place of imagination in the brain is something small. So if the image of a mountain is imprinted in it, then only a part of that large image is imprinted in that small body, so what remains of the large image, if it is not imprinted in anything at all, then you have allowed that the imaginary image is not imprinted in anything at all. And if that is allowed, then allow in every part of that image the like. Then your proof is invalid. And if you say: the remaining parts of that large image are imprinted in that same place, then the imaginings of the parts of that large image have gathered in one place, while we distinguish between them in imagination. This indicates that the gathering of a large image in one place does not prevent imagination from distinguishing between them. And if this is allowed, why is it not allowed for the two winged squares to be imprinted in one place, while distinction remains in perception? The third: a person may travel the world and see lands, mountains, seas, and deserts. If it were necessary that for each part of this image a specific part of the brain occurs, this is far-fetched because the small part of the brain cannot contain that, and if this specification is not necessary, then your statement is invalid.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage answers the sixth objection that imagining a winged square made of two squares requires imagination to be corporeal. The author gives three arguments. First, if we can imagine a specific winged square, we can also imagine a universal winged square, which would make the perceiver of universals a body, leading to the absurdity that the body perceives universals and thus the soul is negated. Second, the brain is small; if a large image like a mountain is imprinted, only a part fits. The opponent either says the rest is not imprinted, which allows that no image is imprinted, invalidating their proof, or says all parts are imprinted in the same small place, which means many images coexist. Since we can distinguish them, coexistence does not hinder distinction, so the winged squares can also coexist. Third, when traveling, a person sees many large scenes, but the brain cannot have a specific part for each detail. So the opponent's assumption fails.

Key Terms
تخيل
المربع المجنح
الكليات
الجزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the ability to imagine a specific composite image (like a winged square) proves that imagination is a bodily faculty.
Correction: The author shows that the same reasoning would apply to universal images, which are clearly not corporeal, thus the premise is false.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what would follow if imagining a specific winged square makes imagination corporeal?
A) Imagining a universal winged square would also be corporeal.
B) The brain would be too small for the image.
C) The soul would be material.
D) The square would be real.

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that the opponent's logic would force them to admit that the body perceives universals, which contradicts their own position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent's logic would force them to admit that the body perceives universals – a position they reject. Ask yourself: In your daily life, do you ever act as if your thoughts are merely products of your brain, ignoring the reality of your soul? How does this argument challenge you to affirm that your mind transcends the physical?
?
If the body cannot perceive universals, then your awareness of concepts like justice, truth, or mercy must come from something beyond matter. When was the last time you truly felt the weight of that reality? How would your life change if you lived with the certainty that your soul is what knows and understands?

Second Argument: Brain Capacity and Imprinting
The author presents the second argument: brain size limitations disprove corporeal imprinting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument: the small size of the brain cannot fully imprint a large image like a mountain, so the opponent's theory of corporeal imagination fails.

Core Idea
A small brain cannot hold a large image, so imagination must be non-physical.

The second argument uses a physical limitation: the brain is small, yet we can imagine vast scenes like mountains. If imagination were corporeal, the image would need to be imprinted physically, which is impossible given the brain's size.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation for the second argument. Pay attention to the dilemma presented.
The author gives a dilemma with two options.
2
Understand the dilemma
If the brain is the location of imagination, and a mountain image is imprinted, only part fits. The opponent can say either: (a) the rest is not imprinted, which means no image is imprinted at all (absurd), or (b) all parts are imprinted in the same spot, which would mean multiple images coexist. The author then argues that coexistence does not hinder distinction, so allowing the winged squares to coexist is possible.
This undermines the objection that two squares cannot coexist in one imaginal space.
3
Reflect on the outcome
The opponent's objection relied on the impossibility of two squares coexisting, but this argument shows coexistence is possible if we can distinguish them.

Third Argument: Travel and Brain Capacity
The author presents the third argument: world travel proves the brain cannot accommodate all details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument: the vast amount of images from a world tour cannot each have a specific brain part, so the opponent's demand for specific imprinting is unreasonable.

Core Idea
The brain cannot have a dedicated spot for every detail of a journey, so imagination is not limited by physical space.

The third argument appeals to common experience: a person can travel the world and recall countless scenes. If imagination required a separate brain part for each detail, the brain would be insufficient. This shows that the opponent's assumption of one-to-one mapping is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author uses a real-life scenario.
This argument is empirical.
2
Identify the reductio
The opponent would have to claim that every detail of a journey has a specific brain part. But the brain is too small to have that many distinct parts. Hence, the opponent's position leads to an impossibility.
The author concludes 'your statement is invalid.'
3
Consider the implication
The third argument reinforces the idea that imagination is not a physical imprinting process but something non-corporeal.

Answer to the Seventh Objection: Differing Sizes of Imaginal Forms
The author refutes the seventh objection using a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that different sizes of imaginal forms require different sizes of their locus.

Core Idea
The size of an imaginal form does not depend on the size of its locus.

In this passage, the author addresses a specific objection: that the varying sizes of mental images must correspond to varying sizes of their physical substrate. He presents a dilemma: either a small locus forces a small image (which leads to absurd limits on imagination), or it does not (which invalidates the objection). Let's see how he constructs this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure of the objection and the response.
The passage is concise, so read carefully.
2
Identify the two conditional arguments
The author presents a conditional: if smallness of locus forces smallness of form, then...; if not, then... Locate both parts and understand the consequence in each case.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that in either case, the original objection fails. Reflect on why this refutation is effective.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه سابعاً من أن اختلاف الصور الخيالية في المقادير يجب أن يكون لاختلاف الآخذين في المقدار. فنقول: إن كان صغر المحل يقتضي صغر الصورة الحالة فيه، وجب أن لا يقدر الإنسان على تخيل شيء، إلا بمقدار ما يساوي جزءاً من أجزاء دماغه. وإن كان صغر المحل لا يوجب صغر الصورة، ولا يمنع من كبرها، فقد بطل دليلكم.
And the answer to what they mentioned seventhly: that the difference of imaginal forms in sizes must be due to the difference of their loci in size. We say: If the smallness of the locus necessitates the smallness of the form subsisting in it, then it would be necessary that a person cannot imagine anything except to the extent that equals a part of his brain. And if the smallness of the locus does not necessitate the smallness of the form, nor prevent its largeness, then your proof is invalid.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage answers the seventh objection that imaginal images differ in size, so their locus must differ in size. The author argues that if a small locus forces a small image, then humans could only imagine things as small as a brain part, which is false. If a small locus does not force smallness, then the original proof fails. This refutes the objection.

Key Terms
صور خيالية (imaginal forms)
مقادير (sizes)
محل (locus)
صغر (smallness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The size of an imaginal form must correspond to the size of its physical substrate.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to an absurd conclusion (cannot imagine large things), so the correspondence is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would happen if a small locus necessarily produced a small image?
A) We could only imagine small things.
B) We could only imagine brain-sized things.
C) We could imagine large things.
D) The soul would be corporeal.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the seventh objection by showing that the necessary connection between image size and locus size leads to an absurdity, or if no connection, the objection is groundless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture the Kaaba or the vastness of the heavens, your mental image is not physically confined by your brain's size. Does this awareness unsettle your assumption that all your thoughts are mere material processes? If your mind can hold what is immense, what does that imply about the nature of your soul? Will you today begin to treat your thoughts as more than just brain activity, and instead as something that connects you to the unseen?
?
The author exposes a logical flaw: if images were limited by the brain, you could only imagine things as tiny as a brain part. Yet you routinely imagine mountains and galaxies. Have you ever let a small view of yourself—your sins, your limitations—prevent you from imagining God's mercy being wider than the heavens? What would it mean for you to expand your vision of God's attributes right now, and how will you act on that?
?
The dilemma forces a choice: either the image size is tied to the brain (absurd) or it is not. If you choose the second, you accept that mental realities are not bound by physical constraints. Are you willing to live as if your inner world is not chained to your body? What specific habit of thought or sin have you excused as 'just human nature' that you must now confront as a choice of your immaterial soul?

Answer to the Objection on Imagination and Corporeality
The author analogizes imaginal impossibility to intellectual impossibility to refute the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the inability to imagine opposite qualities in one body does not prove that imagination is corporeal, by analyzing the author's analogy with intellectual conception.

Core Idea
If a limit applies to intellect too, it does not prove corporeality.

In this passage, the author addresses the eighth objection that our inability to imagine black and white in one body shows imagination is a bodily faculty. He cleverly turns the argument around by noting that the same impossibility exists in intellectual conception, yet we do not consider the intellect to be corporeal. Therefore, the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once. Notice the structure: the objection is stated, then the answer begins with 'We say'.
Focus on the parallel between imagination and intellect.
2
Identify the analogy
The author says: just as we cannot imagine black and white in one body, we also cannot conceive them intellectually in one body. But we can in two bodies. This is the same for both faculties.
The key is that the limitation is not unique to imagination.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Since intellectual conception has the same limitation but is not corporeal, the objection that imagination must be corporeal due to this limitation is invalid. The analogy refutes the objection.
Connect this to the learning objective.

The Four Types of Predication in Logic
Author lists the four types of predication as agreed upon by logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of predication as classified by logicians and how they relate to the soul's perception of particulars.

Core Idea
Predication types are the logical scaffolding for understanding how the soul knows particulars.

This passage presents a key logical classification used by the author to argue for the soul's perception of particulars. Before we can see the argument, we need to understand the four types of predication agreed upon by logicians. This classification divides predicates and subjects into universal or particular, yielding four combinations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the logical structure come through.
The text is cut off at the end, but the main classification is clear.
2
Identify each type
Find the four types: particular to particular, universal to universal, particular to universal, and universal to particular. Note the examples given.
The third type may be confusing because 'some human' seems particular but is considered universal.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Understand why 'some human' is a universal concept and not a particular. This distinction is crucial for the later argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة على أن النفس مدركة للجزئيات : إن أهل المنطق اتفقوا على أن المحمولات والموضوعات على أربعة أقسام : الأول : حمل الجزئي على الجزئي . كقولك : « زيد هو الذي عمل العمل الفلاني » ، فههنا الموضوع والمحمول جزئيان . والثاني : حمل الكلي على الكلي . كقولك : « الإنسان حيوان » . والثالث : حمل الجزئي على الكلي كقولك : « بعض الإنسان هو زيد » فإن قولك : بعض الإنسان، مفهوم كلي . الرابع : حمل الكلي على الجزئي . كقولك : « زيد إنسان » أو « زيد
The fourth proof that the soul perceives particulars: The logicians agreed that predicates and subjects are of four divisions: First: predication of a particular to a particular, like your saying 'Zayd is the one who did such-and-such action'; here the subject and predicate are both particulars. Second: predication of a universal to a universal, like your saying 'Human is an animal'. Third: predication of a particular to a universal, like your saying 'Some human is Zayd'; for your saying 'some human' is a universal concept. Fourth: predication of a universal to a particular, like your saying 'Zayd is a human' or 'Zayd...'
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth proof for the soul's perception of particulars. It is a classification passage: the author states that logicians (ahl al-manṭiq) have agreed that predicates and subjects are of four types. The first type is predication of a particular to a particular (ḥaml al-juzʾī ʿalā al-juzʾī), exemplified by 'Zayd is the one who did such-and-such action' — here both terms are particular. The second is predication of a universal to a universal, like 'Human is an animal' — both are universals. The third is predication of a particular to a universal, like 'Some human is Zayd' — 'some human' is a universal concept (since it applies to multiple individuals). The fourth is predication of a universal to a particular, like 'Zayd is a human' or 'Zayd...' The text is cut off, so the full argument applying this classification to the soul is not present in this window. However, the enumeration itself is complete for the first three and partially given for the fourth. Typically, this taxonomy is used to analyze how the soul's perception of particulars fits into these categories, but here we only have the list.

Key Terms
حمل (ḥaml) — predication
جزئي (juzʾī) — particular
كلي (kullī) — universal
محمول (maḥmūl) — predicate
موضوع (mawḍūʿ) — subject

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'some human' is a particular term because it refers to a specific individual.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'some human' is a universal concept because it can apply to multiple individuals (the concept of 'some human' is not tied to a single individual).

Checkpoint

A) 'Zayd is the one who did the action'
B) 'Human is an animal'
C) 'Some human is Zayd'
D) 'Zayd is a human'

Key Takeaway
The logicians classify predication into four types based on whether subject and predicate are universal or particular, and this classification is used to analyze the soul's perception of particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am human', you are predicating a universal to a particular. Does this logical structure capture who you truly are, or does it reduce your uniqueness? How does the soul's ability to perceive both your particularity and your universality affect your sense of self?

The Third Objection: Universals and Abstract Forms
Author presents third objection using logical division of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical division of universals into five types is used to argue that intellectual forms cannot be parts of external essences.

Core Idea
The five types of universals create a problem for abstract forms.

This passage presents the third objection, drawn from the science of logic. The author invokes the five types of universals—genus, differentia, species, property, common accident—to argue that if abstract forms are accidents in the soul, they cannot be constitutive parts of external essences. Let's examine this logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first states the five types of universals, then focuses on genus and differentia, and finally on species.
Pay attention to the distinction between universals that are constitutive (genus, differentia) and those that are not.
2
Identify the five types of universals
List the five types: genus, differentia, species, property, common accident. Understand their roles in logic.
Genus and differentia combine to form the essence of a thing.
3
Understand the objection's logic
The objection states that an accident in the soul cannot be part of the essence of an external thing. Therefore, abstract forms (which are accidents in the soul) cannot be the genus or differentia of external things.
Species is the essence itself, not an accident.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This objection challenges the theory of abstract forms by showing a mismatch between mental accidents and real essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنهم بينوا في المنطق : أن الكلي له أنواع خمسة : الجنس والفصل والنوع والخاصة والعرض العام . أما الجنس والفصل فهما جزءا قوام الماهية. والعرض الحال في نفسي يمتنع كونه جزءاً من قوام الماهية الموجودة في الخارج . وأما النوع فهو الماهية ، والماهية الموجودة في الخارج ، كالفلك والشمس
The third: They have explained in logic that the universal has five types: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw'), property (khassa), and common accident (arad 'amm). As for genus and differentia, they are parts of the constitution of the essence. But an accident occurring in a soul (or self) is impossible to be a part of the constitution of the essence existing externally. As for species, it is the essence, and the essence existing externally, such as the celestial sphere and the sun.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection using the logical division of universals. The author states that in logic, the universal has five types: genus, differentia, species, property, and common accident. Genus and differentia are constitutive parts of the essence. But an accident (arad) existing in the soul cannot be a part of the essence of something existing externally. As for species, it is the essence itself, and the essence exists externally, such as the celestial sphere and the sun. The argument implies that if intellectual forms are accidents in the soul, they cannot correspond to the genus and differentia of external things, which are constitutive.

Key Terms

Correction: The objection shows that some universals (genus, differentia) are constitutive of external essences but cannot be accidents in the soul, so intellectual forms cannot be those constitutive parts.

The First Objection Against Abstract Mental Images
Author defines intellect and raises first objection against abstract mental images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the claim that mental images are abstract, and why the author finds it problematic.

Core Idea
A mental image is a personal accident, never free from accompanying states.

Before we can evaluate the status of mental images, we need to understand what is meant by 'abstraction.' The author begins by stating the common view that intellect perceives universals by abstracting from matter. Then he immediately raises a fundamental challenge: if a mental image exists in a particular soul alongside many other attributes, how can it be called abstract? This lesson walks through that objection step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the author first presents the standard view before attacking it.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the three faculties
The author lists sense, imagination, and intellect, each with a different degree of abstraction. Pause to understand the distinction: sense retains matter, imagination partially abstracts, intellect fully abstracts.
3
Trace the objection
Follow the objection: mental image is a personal accident (ʿaraḍ) in a soul, accompanied by many other mental phenomena. Therefore it cannot be abstract. Notice the hypothetical reply and the counter.
Ask yourself: does the reply salvage the claim of abstraction?
4
Reflect on the force of the argument
Consider why the author finds this objection decisive. The mental image is inseparable from its subject and its concomitants, so calling it 'abstract' seems misleading.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إدراك الكليات ، فهو العقل . ثم زعموا : أن الحس يدرك المحسوس ، مع مادته المعينة وأما الخيال فإنه يجرد بعض التجريد عن تلك المادة . ولذلك فإن تخيل الإنسان للأشياء ، لا يتوقف على حضورها في الأعيان . وأما العقل فإنه يجردها عن المادة تجريداً تاماً واعلم : أن هذه كلمات مألوفة معهودة، ولم يبحثوا عنها البتة . والبحث فيها من وجوه : الأول : إن الصورة العقلية عرض شخصي ، حال في نفس شخصية . وتلك النفس قامت بها علوم كثيرة وميول وإرادات وأخلاق . فهذه الصورة العقلية مقارنة لها بأسرها ، ومصاحبة لها . فكيف يمكن أن يقال : إنها صورة مجردة ؟ فإن قالوا : [ المراد من كونها مجردة أنه إذا حذفت عنها هذه المقارنات ] كانت مجردة . قلنا : فلم لا يقال أيضاً : إن الصورة الحسية صورة مجردة بهذا التأويل
Perception of universals is the intellect. Then they claimed: that sense perceives the sensible with its specific matter. As for imagination, it abstracts partially from that matter. Therefore, man's imagination of things does not depend on their presence in external objects. As for the intellect, it abstracts them completely from matter. Know that these are familiar and well-known words, and they have not investigated them at all. The investigation into them has several aspects: The first: that the mental image is a personal accident, subsisting in a particular soul. That soul possesses many sciences, inclinations, wills, and morals. So this mental image is accompanied by all of them and associated with them. How then can it be said that it is an abstract image? If they say: 'What is meant by its being abstract is that if these accompaniments are removed from it,' then it is abstract. We say: Why is it not also said that the sensible image is an abstract image by this interpretation?
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the intellect as the faculty that perceives universals. The author then presents a common view: that sense perceives particular objects with their matter, imagination partially abstracts from matter (so imagining an object does not require its presence), and the intellect abstracts completely. The author notes that these are familiar notions but unexplored. He then presents the first objection: The mental image is an accidental quality (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in a particular soul, which also contains many other sciences, inclinations, wills, and morals. Therefore, this image is accompanied by all these states and cannot be called 'abstract' in the sense of being free from such accompaniments. If the proponents reply that 'abstract' means ignoring these accompaniments, then the same could be said of the sensible image (which is also accompanied by matter), so the term becomes meaningless. The objection thus challenges the coherence of claiming abstraction for mental images.

Key Terms
الصورة العقلية
عرض شخصي
تجريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'abstract' means the mental image is completely independent of any accompanying mental states.
Correction: The author argues that the mental image is always accompanied by other attributes of the soul, so it cannot be abstract in that sense.

Checkpoint

A) The mental image is not a universal.
B) The mental image is an accident in a particular soul and is accompanied by many other mental states, so it cannot be called abstract.
C) The mental image is identical to the object it represents.
D) The mental image is only present when the object is present.

Key Takeaway
The mental image, being a personal accident in a soul full of other attributes, cannot be truly abstract — making the standard threefold division questionable.

The Contradiction of Abstract Images
Author presents the second objection: abstract images are self-contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the concept of an abstract image is self-contradictory because attributing abstraction adds a qualification that destroys abstraction itself.

Core Idea
Calling an image 'abstract' adds a qualification that destroys its abstraction.

In this second objection, the author attacks the very coherence of the notion of abstract images. He argues that the predicate 'abstract' functions as an additional attribute, which contradicts the meaning of abstraction. This brief but incisive argument reveals a logical tension in the position of those who affirm such images.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting how the author structures the objection: a claim, a reason, and a conclusion.
The passage is short but dense; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the contradiction
Break down the argument: The author says 'abstract' is an additional qualification (qayd zā'id). But if something is abstract, it should be free from such qualifications. This creates a self-contradiction.
Think about why the attribute 'abstract' cannot be consistently applied to the image's essence.
3
Reflect on the objection's force
Consider why this objection is significant. It challenges the internal coherence of the concept itself, not just its existence.
A self-contradictory concept cannot be instantiated in reality, according to this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن القول بإثبات الصور المجردة : قول متناقض . لان كونها مجردة : قيد زائد على ماهياتها ، مقارن لها . فالقول بكونها مجردة يمنع من كونها مجردة .
The second: The claim of affirming abstract images is a contradictory statement. Because their being abstract is an additional qualification upon their essences, concomitant to them. So the statement that they are abstract prevents them from being abstract.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a brief second objection. It is an argument that the very concept of an 'abstract image' is self-contradictory. The property 'abstract' (mujarrada) is an additional attribute (qayd zāʾid) that accompanies the essence of the image. If the image is 'abstract', that attribute must be something added to its essence. But being abstract means being free from such additional attributes. Thus, saying an image is abstract introduces a qualification that negates the possibility of that image being truly abstract. The statement therefore undermines itself.

Key Terms
مجردة
قيد زائد
متناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'abstract' is a simple, non-relational property that can be consistently attributed to mental images.
Correction: The author shows that 'abstract' is actually a relational qualification: it adds the condition of being free from qualifications, which leads to a self-contradiction when applied as a property.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that affirming abstract images is contradictory?
A) Because the concept of abstraction is not well-defined.
B) Because the property 'abstract' is an additional qualification that accompanies the image, yet being abstract means having no such qualifications.
C) Because the image cannot be both mental and abstract.
D) Because abstraction only applies to universals, not particulars.

Key Takeaway
The very act of labeling an image 'abstract' introduces a qualification that contradicts the meaning of abstraction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold that, like the abstract image, is self-contradictory — where one part of what you affirm undermines another? Will you examine it today?
?
The author shows that adding a qualification can destroy the meaning. In your life, have you added conditions to your faith or values that actually negate their essence? What condition needs to be removed?
?
This lesson uncovers a logical trap. Are there comfortable contradictions you live with — in your heart, your habits, or your words — that you have avoided facing? What would it cost you to resolve them?

Third Objection: Logical Universals Cannot Be Mental Images
Author uses five logical universals to refute abstract mental images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the five logical universals cannot be identical to mental images, and how this refutes the claim of abstract mental essences.

Core Idea
The mental image is a particular accident, not the universal essence itself.

In this third objection, the author draws on the five logical universals from Aristotelian logic to challenge the claim that mental images are abstract universal essences. By examining each universal—genus, differentia, species, property, and general accident—he shows that none can be identified with the mental image. This passage sharpens our understanding of the nature of knowledge and the particularity of mental states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a sense of the argument without analyzing it immediately.
Notice the logical terms: genus, differentia, species, property, general accident.
2
Identify the five logical universals
The author lists five kinds of universals. Find each one and notice how he groups them for his argument.
He first considers genus and differentia together, then species, then property and general accident.
3
Follow the argument for each universal
For each universal, ask: Why can it not be a mental image? The author gives specific reasons for each group.
For genus and differentia: they are parts of an external essence, while the mental image is an accident in the soul.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the mental image is not a universal essence but rather a particular accident of knowledge. Reflect on the significance.
This refutes the idea that knowledge consists of universal abstract images in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنهم بينوا في المنطق : أن الكلي له أنواع خمسة : الجنس والفصل والنوع والخاصة والعرض العام . أما الجنس والفصل فهما جزءا قوام الماهية. والعرض الحال في نفسي يمتنع كونه جزءاً من قوام الماهية الموجودة في الخارج . وأما النوع فهو الماهية ، والماهية الموجودة في الخارج ، كالفلك والشمس والقمر : يمتنع أن تكون هي عين العرض الحال في نفسي ، أو أن يكون مساوياً . وأما الخاصة والعرض العام فهما من الصفات القائمة بالماهيات الخارجية ، فيمتنع أن تكون تلك الصفات عين العرض الحال في نفسي . فثبت : أن القول بأن الصورة الحالة في النفس ماهية كلية مجردة : كلام ليس له البتة حقيقة ولا أصل . بل الحق : أن الحال في النفس : هو العلم بهذه الماهيات والشعور بها . وهذا العلم عرض معين وصفة معينة حالة في نفس معينة في زمان معين . فكان القول بأن تلك الصورة : صورة كلية مجردة : كلاماً فارغاً عن المعنى
The third: They have explained in logic that the universal has five kinds: genus, differentia, species, property, and general accident. As for genus and differentia, they are the two constituent parts of the essence. An accident subsisting in a soul cannot be a part of the essence of something existing externally. As for species, it is the essence itself, and the essence existing externally, such as the sphere, the sun, and the moon, cannot be identical to the accident subsisting in my soul, nor be equal to it. As for property and general accident, they are attributes inhering in external essences, so it is impossible for those attributes to be identical to the accident subsisting in my soul. So it is established that the statement that the form subsisting in the soul is a universal abstract essence is speech that has absolutely no reality or basis. Rather, the truth is that what subsists in the soul is knowledge (ʿilm) and awareness of these essences. This knowledge is a specific accident, a specific attribute, subsisting in a specific soul at a specific time. So the statement that that form is a universal abstract form is speech devoid of meaning.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is the third objection, a detailed logical argument. The author recalls the five logical universals (genus, differentia, species, property, general accident) from Aristotelian logic. He argues that none of these can be identical to the mental image (a personal accident in the soul). Genus and differentia are constituent parts of an external essence, but an accident in the soul cannot be a part of an external essence. Species is the external essence itself (e.g., the sun, the moon), which cannot be the same as an accident in the soul. Property and general accident are attributes of external essences, not the accident in the soul. Therefore, the idea that the mental image is a universal essence is baseless. Instead, what exists in the soul is knowledge (ʻilm) and awareness of those essences—a specific accident at a specific time. This refutes the claim of abstract universal mental images.
