Two Conditions for Intellectual Perception by Analogy with Sight
Author enumerates two conditions for intellectual insight by analogy with sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conditions required for intellectual perception as analogized from physical sight, and why active mental movement is necessary.

Core Idea
Intellectual insight is an active process requiring both a suitable object and deliberate motion of the mind.

The author begins by laying down a powerful analogy between physical sight and intellectual perception. Just as the eye requires specific conditions to see, the mind requires analogous conditions to understand. In this passage, he identifies two such conditions: the object's clarity and the necessity of mental movement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation, to grasp the overall analogy between physical sight and intellectual insight.
Notice that the author explicitly draws the analogy: 'fakadhā al-baṣīra'.
2
Identify the first condition
Find the first condition: moderate clarity. The object should not be too bright or too dim. Apply this to intelligibles.
Compare the examples: sun vs. separate intellects; atoms vs. complex qualities.
3
Identify the second condition
Find the second condition: movement. The eye must move its pupil to see; the mind must move its 'spiritual eye' from one intelligible to another.
Note the terms: al-fikr, al-rawwiya, naẓar al-ʿaql.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this analogy affects your own study: intellectual perception requires active thought, not passive reception.
Read the common misunderstanding to avoid the passive view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتقسيم الثالث : أن نقول : الإبصار يتوقف على شرائط . فكذا البصيرة . والشرط الأول : [ للإبصار ] أن لا يكون المبصر في غاية الجلاء . كالشمس . فإن البصر يتحير فيها لقوتها . ولا في غاية الخفاء . كالذرات ، ومراتب الممتزجات . وكذا المعقول إذا كان في غاية الجلاء مثل المفارقات . فإن العقل يعجز عن إدراكه . وإن كان في غاية الخفاء ، كمراتب الأنواع الأخيرة من الكيفيات البسيطة والمركبة ، فالعقول عاجزة عن الوقوف عليها بالتمام . فأما المرتبة المتوسطة. وهي الكميات والكيفيات الظاهرة . فالعقول تقدر على الوصول إليها . والشرط الثاني : إن المبصر إذا كان حاضراً ، فلما لم يحرك الإنسان حدقته من جانب إلى جانب ، تحريكات كثيرة : لم ير المبصر . فكذلك النفس الناطقة ، ما لم تحرك عينها الروحانية من معقول إلى معقول : لم تتمكن من الإبصار ، وتلك التحريكات هي المسماة بالفكر والروية ونظر العقل . وكما أن نظر العين عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة ، من جهة إلى جهة طلباً للرؤية فكذلك نظر العقل عبارة عن تقليب حدقة العقل من جانب إلى جانب طلباً لإدراك المعقول
And the third division: We say: Sight (al-ibṣār) depends on conditions. Similarly, inner insight (al-baṣīra). The first condition: for sight, that the seen object should not be in the utmost brightness, like the sun, for the eye is dazzled by it due to its strength; nor in the utmost obscurity, like atoms and the levels of mixtures. Similarly, the intelligible (al-maʿqūl) if it is in the utmost clarity, like the separate intelligibles, then the intellect is incapable of perceiving it; and if it is in the utmost obscurity, like the levels of the last types of simple and composite qualities, then intellects are incapable of fully comprehending them. As for the intermediate level, which are the quantities and apparent qualities, intellects are able to attain them. The second condition: When the seen object is present, if a person does not move his pupil from side to side with many movements, he will not see the seen object. Similarly, the rational soul, as long as it does not move its spiritual eye from one intelligible to another, cannot see (perceive). Those movements are called thought (al-fikr), reflection (al-rawwiya), and reason's consideration (naẓar al-ʿaql). And just as the act of seeing (naẓar al-ʿayn) is the turning of the pupil from one direction to another seeking sight, so too the act of reason (naẓar al-ʿaql) is the turning of the pupil of the intellect from one side to another seeking the perception of the intelligible.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is the third division (al-taqsīm al-thālith) of the discussion. It draws an analogy between physical sight (al-ibṣār) and intellectual insight (al-baṣīra), stating that both depend on conditions. The author presents two conditions for sight and then applies them analogically to the intellect. The first condition is that the object should be of moderate clarity—not too bright (like the sun) nor too dim (like atoms and mixtures). For the intellect, similarly, intelligible objects that are too clear (separate intelligibles) or too obscure (very complex qualities) cannot be fully grasped; only those of intermediate level (quantities and apparent qualities) are accessible. The second condition is that the eye must move (turning the pupil) to see; without such motion, sight does not occur. Analogously, the rational soul must move its 'spiritual eye' from one intelligible to another via thought (al-fikr), reflection (al-rawwiya), and reason's consideration (naẓar al-ʿaql). This moving is like the turning of the pupil in physical sight. Thus, intellectual perception requires both a suitable object (moderate intelligibility) and active mental movement.

البصيرة
المعقول
فكر
روية
نظر العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellectual insight is a passive reception of knowledge.
Correction: The author argues that intellectual perception requires active movement of the mind, analogous to the eye's movement to see. Without deliberate thought (fikr) and reflection (rawwiya), the intellect cannot perceive.

Checkpoint

A) The intellect perceives it easily.
B) The intellect is dazzled and cannot perceive it.
C) The intellect needs to move its spiritual eye.
D) The intellect requires a teacher to understand it.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual perception requires both a suitable object of moderate clarity and the active movement of the mind through thought and reflection.

?

?
The second condition demands movement of the spiritual eye. In your daily life, do you actively reflect on your beliefs, or are they just static? What would change if you started moving your mind from one truth to another?

The Mental Form is Not a Universal, But Particular Knowledge
Refutes the idea that mental form is a universal abstract essence and affirms it as particular knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the form in the soul cannot be a universal abstract essence but is instead a particular accident of knowledge.

Core Idea
The form in the soul is particular knowledge, not a universal abstract essence.

This passage addresses a common philosophical confusion: whether the mental representation of an external object is a universal abstract entity or something particular. The author argues that what we have in our minds is not a universal essence but specific knowledge tied to a specific time and self. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Read the opening of the passage where the author presents the claim that the mental form is a universal abstract essence. This is the position he will reject.
The phrase 'ماهية كلية مجردة' is the target.
2
Follow the argument against identity with external accidents
Notice how the author uses the example of the moon to show the impossibility of the mental form being identical to an external accident or equal to it. Then he distinguishes between الخاصّة and العرض العام as attributes of external things.
These distinctions are key to the refutation.
3
See the conclusion that mental form is knowledge
After refuting the opposing view, the author states the truth: what subsists in the soul is knowledge (علم) and awareness (شعور), which are particular accidents.
Note the emphasis on specificity: 'عرض معين', 'صفة معينة', 'نفس معينة', 'زمان معين'.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why calling the mental form a universal abstract is 'empty of meaning'. How does this affect our understanding of knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقمر : يمتنع أن تكون هي عين العرض الحال في نفسي ، أو أن يكون مساوياً وأما الخاصة والعرض العام فهما من الصفات القائمة بالماهيات الخارجية ، فيمتنع أن تكون تلك الصفات عين العرض الحال في نفسي . فثبت : أن القول بأن الصورة الحالة في النفس ماهية كلية مجردة : كلام ليس له البئة حقيقة ولا أصل . بل الحق : أن الحال في النفس : هو العلم بهذه الماهيات والشعور بها وهذا العلم عرض معين وصفة معينة حالة في نفس معينة في زمان معين فكان القول بأن تلك الصورة : صورة كلية مجردة : كلاماً فارغاً عن المعنى
And the moon: it is impossible that it should be the very accident subsisting in my self, or that it should be equal. As for the property and the general accident, they are among the attributes subsisting in external essences, so it is impossible that those attributes should be the very accident subsisting in my self. Thus it is established: the statement that the form subsisting in the soul is a universal abstract essence is speech having no reality or foundation at all. Rather, the truth is: what subsists in the soul is knowledge of these essences and awareness of them, and this knowledge is a specific accident and a specific attribute subsisting in a specific self at a specific time. So the statement that that form is a universal abstract form is empty of meaning.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the mental form (الصورة الحالة في النفس) is a universal abstract essence (ماهية كلية مجردة). The author argues that such a form cannot be identical to an accident in the self (العرض الحال في النفس) because accidents are particular. He distinguishes between the property (الخاصة) and the general accident (العرض العام), which are attributes of external essences, and the accident in the self. Therefore, the mental form is not a universal but rather specific knowledge (علم) and awareness (شعور) that is particular to a given self and time. The conclusion is that calling it a universal abstract form is meaningless.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The form in the soul is a universal abstract essence.
Correction: It is actually a particular accident of knowledge (علم) and awareness (شعور) tied to a specific self and time.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The mental form is not a universal abstract but a specific instance of knowledge tied to a particular self and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author says that calling the mental form a universal abstract is 'empty of meaning.' Heart as Ruler: Analogy of the Kingdom
Analogy of the heart as a ruler in a kingdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of the heart as a ruler illustrates the role of the intellect in governing the soul's faculties.

Core Idea
The rational soul must rule the body like a wise king guided by intellect.

Sanusi presents a vivid analogy comparing the heart to a ruler and the faculties to officials. This metaphor helps us grasp how the intellect should govern desire and anger. The passage concludes with a warning about those who abandon rational guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the imagery of a kingdom settle in your mind.
Notice how each faculty is given a specific role.
2
Identify the roles of each faculty
Find the four key elements: heart (ruler), intellectual faculty (sincere advisor), desire (slave bringing food), anger (chief of police). Understand their relationships.
Desire is portrayed as potentially deceptive.
3
Connect the analogy to the rational soul's governance
Reflect on how the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqah) uses the light of intellect to subjugate desire and anger, bringing order to corporeal life.
The Quranic reference warns against taking desire as one's god.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال الثاني : قالوا : القلب في البدن يشبه الوالي في مملكته ، وقواه وجوارحه بمنزلة الصناع، والقوة العقلية المفكرة كالمشير الناصح ، والشهوة كالعبد (٢) الذي يجلب الطعام إلى المدينة ، والغضب كصاحب الشرطة . ثم إن الشهوة التي كالعبد الجالب للطعام إلى المدينة قد يكون خبيثاً مكاراً ، مخادعاً ، يتمثل بصورة الناصح، إلا أن تحت نصحه : كل شر هائل ، وسم قاتل ، وتكون عادته منازعة الوزير الناصح ، في كل تدبير يدبره . وكما أنه يجب على الملك العاقل، أن يسلط وزيره الناصح، على عبده الجالب للطعام، وعلى صاحب الشرطة، وأن لا يلتفت إلى تخليطهما في حق الوزير، ليستقيم أمر المدينة . فكذلك النفس الناطقة متى استعانت بنــور العقل ، واستضاءت بضوء العلم والحكمة ، وجعلت الشهوة والغضب مقهورين ، استقام أمر هذه الحياة الجسمانية. ومن عدل عن هذه الطريقة، كان كمن قال الله في حقه : ( أفرأيت من اتخذ إله هواه (١) :
The second example: They said: The heart in the body resembles the ruler in his kingdom, and its powers and limbs are like the craftsmen, and the intellectual thinking faculty is like the sincere advisor, and desire is like the slave who brings food to the city, and anger is like the chief of police. Then indeed desire, which is like the slave bringing food to the city, may be wicked, deceitful, cunning, appearing in the form of an advisor, but under his advice is every terrible evil and deadly poison, and his habit is to oppose the sincere vizier in every plan he devises. And just as it is incumbent upon the intelligent king to empower his sincere vizier over the slave who brings food and over the chief of police, and not to pay attention to their confusion regarding the vizier, so that the city's affairs are set right, likewise the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqah), when it seeks help from the light of the intellect and is illuminated by the light of knowledge and wisdom, and makes desire and anger subjugated, the affairs of this corporeal life are set right. And whoever deviates from this way is like one of whom God said: 'Have you seen him who has taken his own desire as his god?'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the second analogy (al-mithāl al-thānī) comparing the heart to a ruler in a kingdom. The heart's powers and limbs are like craftsmen. The intellectual faculty (al-quwwah al-ʿaqliyyah al-mufakkirah) is the sincere advisor (al-mushīr al-nāṣiḥ). Desire (shahwah) is like a slave who brings food; anger (ghaḍab) is like the chief of police. Desire may disguise itself as an advisor but brings evil. The ruler must empower the sincere vizier over them. Similarly, the rational soul must use the light of intellect (nūr al-ʿaql) and the light of knowledge and wisdom (ḍawʾ al-ʿilm wa-l-ḥikmah) to subjugate desire and anger, thereby setting right corporeal life. Deviation leads to taking one's own desire as a god, as referenced in Quran 45:23.

القوة العقلية المفكرة
الشهوة
الغضب
نور العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Desire and anger are inherently evil and must be eliminated.
Correction: The analogy shows they have legitimate roles (slave brings food, police keeps order) but become harmful when not governed by intellect.

Body as City, Soul as King
Compares the body to a city and the soul to a king with enemies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul must actively govern desire and anger to maintain spiritual well-being.

Core Idea
The soul's active mastery over desire and anger is essential for stability.

This third analogy vividly depicts the internal struggle of the soul. By likening the body to a city and the soul to its king, the text illustrates the consequences of either subduing or neglecting inner enemies. Let's examine the elements and the lesson they convey.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy elements
Read the passage and note each element: body=city, soul=king, senses=soldiers, limbs=subjects, desire&anger=enemy.
2
Understand the consequence of action
Notice what happens if the king subdues the enemy: stability and end of conflict.
3
Understand the consequence of inaction
Contrast with the outcome if the king neglects the enemy: waste, occupation, destruction.
4
Apply the moral lesson
Reflect that the rational soul must actively control desire and anger to avoid ruin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال الثالث : البدن كالمدينة ، والنفس الناطقة كالملك ، والحواس الظاهرة والباطنة كالجنود ، والأعضاء كالرعية ، والشهوة والغضب كعدو ينازعه في مملكته ، ويسعى في إهلاك رعيته . فإن قصد الملك قهر ذلك العدو ، استقامت المملكة، وارتفعت الخصومة . وإن لم ينازع عدوه ضيع مملكته ، واحتلت بلدته ، وصارت عاقبة أمره إلى الهلاك
The third example: The body is like a city, and the rational soul is like a king, and the external and internal senses are like soldiers, and the limbs are like subjects, and desire and anger are like an enemy who contends with him in his kingdom and seeks to destroy his subjects. If the king intends to subdue that enemy, the kingdom becomes stable, and conflict is lifted. But if he does not contend with his enemy, he wastes his kingdom, his town is occupied, and his end result is destruction.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage gives the third analogy: the body is a city (madīnah), the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqah) is the king (malik), the external and internal senses (al-ḥawāss al-ẓāhirah wa-l-bāṭinah) are soldiers (junūd), the limbs (aʿḍāʾ) are subjects (raʿiyyah), and desire and anger (shahwah wa ghaḍab) are an enemy (ʿaduww) who contends for the kingdom and seeks to destroy the subjects. If the king subdues the enemy, the kingdom is stable and conflict ends. If he does not, he wastes his kingdom, it is occupied, and he ends in destruction. The lesson is that the soul must actively control desire and anger.

Key Terms

الملك
الجنود
الرعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the enemy is external, but it is internal — desire and anger within the soul.
Correction: The passage identifies desire and anger as the enemy, which are internal faculties of the soul, not external forces.

Checkpoint
In the third analogy, what happens if the king does not contend with the enemy?
A) The kingdom becomes stronger.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul must actively control desire and anger to prevent spiritual destruction.

Fourth Analogy: Soul as Rider, Desire as Horse, Anger as Dog
Analogy comparing soul to rider, desire to horse, anger to dog.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy of the soul as a rider with desire as horse and anger as dog, and how training these faculties leads to success.

Core Idea
The soul's success depends on training desire and anger, not eliminating them.

This passage presents a powerful analogy that illustrates the relationship between the rational soul, desire, and anger. By comparing the soul to a rider, desire to a horse, and anger to a dog, it shows that these faculties are not inherently harmful but require proper training. Let's explore the analogy and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the analogy. Notice the key terms: rider, horse, dog, success, perishing.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if... then... else...'
2
Identify the components of the analogy
Identify what each element represents: the rational soul is the rider, desire is the horse, anger is the dog. Understand their roles.
3
Analyze the conditions for success and failure
The passage describes two scenarios: a skilled rider with trained horse and dog leads to success; a clumsy rider with untrained horse and dog leads to failure. Consider why both desire and anger need training.
Training (ta'dib) is key – the horse and dog are not bad in themselves.
4
Reflect on the practical lesson
Reflect on how this analogy applies to your own spiritual and moral development. How can you train your desires and anger to align with your rational soul's goals?
This analogy is often used in Islamic ethics to discuss self-discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثال الرابع : مثل النفس الناطقة ، مثل : فارس ركب لأجل الصيد . فشهوته فرسه ، وغضبه كلبه . فإن كان الفارس حاذقاً ، وفرسه مرتاضـاً منقاداً ، وكلبه معلماً ، كان جديراً بالنجح. ومتى كان هو في نفسه أخرق ، وكان الفرس في نفسه جموحاً ، والكلب غير معلم (٣) فلا فرسه تنبعث تحته على حسب إرادته ، ولا كلبه يسترسل بإشارته . فهو خليق بأن يعطب ، فضلا عن أن ينال ذلك الذي طلب
The fourth example: The rational soul is like a rider who mounts for the purpose of hunting. So his desire is his horse, and his anger is his dog. If the rider is skillful, and his horse is trained and obedient, and his dog is trained, he is worthy of success. But whenever he himself is clumsy, and the horse itself is headstrong, and the dog is untrained, then neither does his horse set off under him according to his will, nor does his dog respond to his signal. So he is likely to perish, let alone attain what he sought.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage gives the fourth analogy: the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqah) is like a rider (fāris) who mounts for hunting. Desire (shahwah) is his horse (faras), and anger (ghaḍab) is his dog (kalb). If the rider is skillful (ḥādhiq), the horse trained and obedient (murtaḍan munqādan), and the dog trained (muʿallam), he is worthy of success (jadīr bil-najḥ). If the rider is clumsy (akhraq), the horse headstrong (jamūḥ), and the dog untrained, neither responds, and he is likely to perish (yaʿṭib), let alone attain what he sought. The condition of the soul depends on proper training of desire and anger.

Misconception: One might think that desire and anger are inherently bad and should be eliminated.
Correction: The analogy shows that desire and anger are like a horse and dog: useful if trained, harmful if untrained. They are not evil in themselves but need proper discipline.

Fifth Analogy: The Body as a Complete House
Elaborate analogy comparing body parts to rooms and features of a house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed analogy that the human body resembles a complete house, with each body part corresponding to a specific architectural feature, illustrating purposeful design.

Core Idea
The body's parts mirror a house's rooms and functions, showing intentional creation.

This final analogy presents an elaborate comparison between the human body and a fully equipped house. The author lists correspondences from the head down to the liver, each labeled with an Arabic letter. As you read, notice how each body part's function is mapped onto a house's feature, reinforcing the theme of purposeful design in creation. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intended pattern is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy's setup
Start by reading the opening statement that introduces the comparison between the body and a complete house. This sets the context for the detailed list.
The term 'complete house' implies all necessary parts are present.
2
Follow the correspondences from head to torso
Work through the list from (A) to (O). Note how each body part is paired with a house feature, such as the head as an upper room or the lung as a summer house.
The letters run from alif to yaa, but the list is incomplete after 'yā'.'
3
Reflect on the analogy's purpose
Consider why the author uses this detailed analogy. It is not merely poetic but demonstrates the intricate design and interdependence of body parts, each with a specific function.
Think about how the house analogy helps explain the body's design.
4
Note the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off at the liver correspondence. This may indicate the author intended to continue, but the available text ends here.

المثال الخامس : اعلم : أن هذا البدن يشبه الدار الكاملة ، التي بنيت ، وأكملت بيوتها وخزائنها ، وأقيمت أبوابها ، وأعد فيها كل ما يحتاج إليه صاحب الدار . أ - فالرأس كالغرفة في أعلا الدار . ب - والثقب في الرأس كالروازن في غرفة الدار . ج - ووسط دماغه كالأبواب في الدار . د - والفم كباب الغرفة . هـ - والأنف كالطاق الذي فوق باب الدار . و - والشفتان كمصراعي الباب . ز - والأسنان كالبوابين . ح - واللسان كالحاجب . ط - والظهر كالجدار القوي ، الذي هو حصن الدار - ي - والوجه كصدر الدار . يا - والرئة التي هي الجاذبة للنفس البارد كالبيت الصيفي . يب - وجريان النفس فيها ، كالهواء الذي يجري في البيت الصيفي . يج - والقلب مع حرارته الغريزية كالبيت الشتوي . يد - وبيت المعدة مع نضج الغذاء فيها كالمطبخ . يه - والكبد مع حصول الدم فيها

A) The head is like the upper room in the top of the house. B) The holes in the head are like the windows in the upper room. C) The middle of his brain is like the doors in the house. D) The mouth is like the door of the room. E) The nose is like the arch above the door of the house. F) The two lips are like the two leaves of the door. G) The teeth are like the doorkeepers. M) The heart, with its innate heat, is like the winter house. N) The stomach house, with the ripening of food in it, is like the kitchen. O) The liver, with the formation of blood in it.

Commentary

(the text cuts off). The analogy emphasizes the purposeful design and interdependence of body parts.

The Superiority of Composite Sounds as Conventional Signs
Explains why composite sounds are the best conventional signs and their physical basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why composite sounds (speech) are the most suitable and noble conventional signs, based on their physical origin in the human body and their practical advantages.

Core Idea
Speech is the noblest sign because it is easy, efficient, and vanishes after use.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah argues that speech, composed of letters and words, is superior to other conventional signs like gestures. The argument begins with the design of the human body for breathing and sound production, then highlights three practical advantages that make speech the most effective means of definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the author moves from classification to physical explanation to enumeration of advantages.
2
Understand the physical basis
Follow the explanation: the heart needs cool air, breath is expelled, sound is produced, then divided in articulatory chambers to form letters and words. See how this shows divine wisdom.
The 'chambers' (المحابس) refer to places of articulation in the mouth and throat.
3
Identify the three advantages
Find the three aspects (وجوه) that make composite sounds easy: ease of production, ease of forming many words, and temporary existence. These are listed explicitly in the text.
4
Compare with gesture
The passage ends by mentioning gesture as the second type but states speech is better for reasons. Reflect on why speech might be superior.
The 'reasons' are not given here but are implied by the advantages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أقسام : فالأول : وهو أصلحها وأشرفها : الأصوات المركبة . والسبب في شرفها : أن بدن الإنسان لا يتم ولا يكمل إلا بالقلب ، الذي هو معدن الحرارة الغريزية. ولا بد من وصول النسيم البارد إليه ساعة بعد ساعة حتى يبقى على اعتداله ولا يحترق. فخلقت آلات في بدنه ، بحيث يقدر الإنسان بها على استدخال النسيم البارد في قلبه . فإذا مكث ذلك النسيم لحظة ، تسخن وفسد ، فوجب إخراجه . فالصانع الحكيم جعل النفس الخارج سبياً لحدوث الخروق [؟] فلا جرم سهل تحصيل [؟] الصوت بهذا الطريق ثم إن الصوت يسهل تقطيعه في المحابس المختلفة ، فحصلت هيات مخصوصة ، بسبب تقطيع ذلك الصوت في تلك المحابس وتلك الهيات المخصوصة هى الحروف فحصلت الحروف والأصوات بهذا الطريق ثم ركبوا الحروف فحصلت الكلمات بهذا الطريق ثم جعلوا كل كلمة مخصوصة ، معرفة لكل معنى مخصوص . فلا جرم صار تعريف المعاني المخصوصة بهذا الطريق في غاية السهولة . من وجوه : الأول : إن إدخالها في الوجود في غاية السهولة والثاني : إن تكون الكلمات الكثيرة الواقعة في مقابلة المعلومات الكثيرة في غاية السهولة الثالث : إنه عند الحاجة إلى التعريف ، تدخل في الوجود ، وعند الاستغناء عن ذكرها تعدم . لأن الأصوات لا تبقى والقسم الثاني من طرق التعريف : الإشارة إلا أن النطق أفضل من الإشارة لوجوه
And they are divisions: the first – and it is the most suitable and noblest of them – is composite sounds. The reason for their nobility is that the human body is not complete nor perfect except through the heart, which is the source of innate heat. And it is necessary for cool breeze to reach it hour after hour so that it remains in balance and does not burn. So organs were created in his body such that man is able by them to draw the cool breeze into his heart. And when that breeze lingers for a moment, it heats up and spoils, so its expulsion becomes necessary. So the Wise Maker made the outward breath a cause for the occurrence of breaths [؟], so inevitably the acquisition of sound is facilitated by this way. Then sound is easily divided in the various [articulatory] chambers, so specific states result from the division of that sound in those chambers, and those specific states are the letters. Thus letters and sounds are obtained by this way. Then they combined the letters to obtain words by this way. Then they made each specific word a signifier for each specific meaning. So inevitably the definition of specific meanings by this way became extremely easy from several aspects: first: bringing them into existence is extremely easy; second: the formation of many words corresponding to many known things is extremely easy; third: when there is need for definition, they come into existence, and when one has no need to mention them, they cease to exist, because sounds do not remain. And the second division of the methods of definition is gesture, except that speech is better than gesture for reasons.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of conventional signs and defends the superiority of composite sounds (speech). It begins by stating that the first and best type is composite sounds (al-aṣwāt al-murakkaba). Then it explains the physical basis: the heart requires cool air; the breath is expelled; by the Wise Maker's design, the breath is modified to produce sound. The sound is then divided in the articulatory chambers (maḥābis) to produce letters (ḥurūf), which are combined to form words (kalimāt), and each word is assigned to a specific meaning. This system has three advantages: (1) ease of producing the sounds, (2) ease of forming many words for many meanings, and (3) sounds exist only when needed and disappear afterward. Finally, the second type of sign is gesture (ishāra), but speech is superior for several unlisted reasons. The passage thus provides both a theological (design of the body) and practical (ease, efficiency) argument for the nobility of speech.

Key Terms
الأصوات المركبة
الحروف
الكلمات
الإشارة
علامة وضعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think gesture is equally efficient as a conventional sign.
Correction: The passage highlights that speech has unique advantages: ease of producing many distinct signs, ease of forming many words, and temporary existence, making it superior to gesture.

Checkpoint
What is the first advantage of composite sounds as a means of definition?
A) They are easy to combine into words.
B) Bringing them into existence is extremely easy.
C) They correspond to the heart's need for air.
D) They can be written down.

Key Takeaway
Composite sounds (speech) are the most suitable conventional signs because they are easy to produce, can form many words for many meanings, and exist only when needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If speech is the noblest sign, and you have the ability to speak, how might you misuse this gift? Commit to one specific change in your speech today.

Refuting the Objection to the Definition of Human
Defends the definition of human as rational animal against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classical definition of human as 'rational animal' is defended against the objection that some animals speak and some humans do not.

Core Idea
Rational speech is the ability to communicate inner states uniquely to humans.

The classic definition of a human as a 'rational animal' (ḥayawān nāṭiq) seems straightforward until objections arise. Critics point out that some animals can vocalize and some humans cannot speak. How can the definition stand? In this passage, the author addresses these objections by clarifying what 'speech' really means in this context — not sound, but the rational capacity to convey meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and objection
Read the opening lines where the standard definition is stated, and note the two-part objection: 'application' (ṭard) and 'reverse' (`aks).
2
Understand the initial answer
The objection is answered by specifying 'rational speech' (al-nuṭq al-`aqlī). Notice that this answer was considered insufficient because it lacked explanation.
3
Follow the author's clarification
The author divides animals into two types: those that cannot make others know their inner states, and humans who can. This is the real meaning of 'nāṭiq'.
4
See why the definition stands
The author concludes that with this clarification, the original objections no longer apply. Reflect on how this resolves both the 'application' and 'reverse' problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني فيما يتعلق بهذا الباب: إن المشهور أن يقال في حد الإنسان: إنه حيوان ناطق. فقال بعضهم: إن هذا التعريف باطل، طرداً وعكساً. أما الطرد فلان بعض الحيوان ينطق. وأما العكس فهو أن بعض الناس لا ينطق. ولما أجيب عنه: بأن المراد منه: النطق العقلي، لم يذكروا لهذا النطق العقلي تفسيراً ملخصاً. ونقول: الحيوان نوعان: منه ما إذا عرف شيئاً، فإنه لا يقدر على أن يعرف غيره، حال نفسه. مثل: البهائم وغيرها. فإنها إذا وجدت من أنفسها أحوالاً مخصوصة، فإنها لا تقدر على أن تعرف غيرها تلك الأحوال. وأما الإنسان فإنه إذا وجد من نفسه حالة مخصوصة، قدر على أن يعرف غيره تلك الحالة الموجودة في نفسه. فالناطق الذي جعل فصلاً مقوماً، هو هذا المعنى. والسبب فيه: إنا بينا: أن أكمل طرق التعريف هو النطق، المعبر عن هذه القدرة بأكمل الطرق الدالة عليها. وبهذا التقدير، فإن تلك السؤالات لا تتوجه والله أعلم.
The second investigation concerning this chapter: It is well-known that in the definition of human it is said: He is a rational speaking animal. Some said: This definition is invalid, both in its application and its reverse. As for the application, because some animals speak. As for the reverse, because some humans do not speak. And when it was answered that what is meant is rational speech, they did not give a succinct explanation of this rational speech. We say: Animals are of two types: Some, when they know something, are not able to make others know that same state of theirs; like beasts and others. For if they experience particular states in themselves, they are not able to make others know those states. As for humans, when he experiences a particular state in himself, he is able to make others know that state existing in himself. So the 'speaking' that is made a constitutive differentia is this meaning. The reason for it: We have shown that the most complete method of definition is speech, which expresses this ability through the most complete means indicative of it. On this assessment, those objections do not arise. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage addresses a common objection to the classic definition of a human as 'rational animal'. Critics claim the definition fails both in application (since some animals like parrots can vocalize) and in reverse (since mute humans cannot speak). The response is that 'speaking' here means rational speech (al-nuṭq al-`aqlī), not mere vocalization. The author then clarifies this by dividing animals into two kinds: those that cannot communicate their inner states to others (beasts) and humans who can. This ability to make others know one's inner states is the essence of rational speech, and it is this that serves as the constitutive differentia (faṣl muqawwim) of humans. Therefore, the original objection does not apply.

Key Terms
حد (definition)
حيوان ناطق (rational animal)
طرد وعكس (application and reverse)
النطق العقلي (rational speech)
فصل مقوم (constitutive differentia)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of human as 'rational animal' is invalid because parrots can mimic speech and mute humans cannot speak.
Correction: The 'speech' in the definition is not vocalization but the rational ability to communicate inner states, which is unique to humans and does not depend on physical ability to vocalize.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that some animals speak and some humans do not?
A) He denies that animals can make sounds.
B) He redefines 'speaking' as the rational ability to communicate inner states, which is unique to humans.
C) He claims that mute humans are not actually human.
D) He says the definition applies only to adults.

Key Takeaway
The definition of human as rational animal is valid because rational speech is the unique ability to communicate inner states, which belongs only to humans.

Defending the Definition of Human as Rational Animal
Author defends 'rational animal' definition against two-directional objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of human as 'rational animal' is defended against objections that some animals speak and some humans do not, by clarifying the meaning of 'rational' as the ability to communicate inner states.

Core Idea
The human's defining rational capacity is not speech but the ability to make others aware of one's inner states.

This passage defends the classical definition of human as a 'rational animal' by addressing a common logical objection. Critics point out that some animals speak (so 'rational' is too broad) and some humans do not speak (so it is too narrow). The author clarifies that 'rational' here refers to a deeper capacity: the ability to communicate one's internal experiences to others, which only humans possess.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and objection
Read the opening where the definition is given and the objection on inclusion and exclusion. Note the terms 'ṭard' (inclusion) and 'ʿaks' (exclusion).
The objection is a classic logical critique of definitions.
2
Understand the author's classification of animals
The author divides animals into two types: those who cannot make others know their inner states (beasts) and humans who can. This classification is key to the defense.
Identify the two groups described in the passage.
3
See how the clarified meaning answers the objection
Realize that 'rational' (al-nuṭq al-ʿaqli) means the ability to communicate inner states. This meaning is unique to humans, so the definition is neither too broad nor too narrow.
The author emphasizes that the most perfect way of definition is speech expressing this ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني فيما يتعلق بهذا الباب : إن المشهور أن يقال في حد الإنسان : إنه حيوان ناطق . فقال بعضهم : إن هذا التعريف باطل، طرداً وعكساً . أما الطرد فلان بعض الحيوان ينطق، وأما العكس فهو أن بعض الناس لا ينطق، ولما أجيب عنه : بأن المراد منه : النطق العقلي، لم يذكروا لهذا النطق العقلي تفسيراً ملخصاً . ونقول : الحيوان نوعان : منه ما إذا عرف شيئاً، فإنه لا يقدر على أن يعرف غيره، حال نفسه . مثل : البهائم وغيرها . فإنها إذا وجدت من أنفسها أحوالاً مخصوصة . فإنها لا تقدر على أن تعرف غيرها تلك الأحوال. وأما الإنسان فإنه إذا وجد من نفسه حالة مخصوصة، قدر على أن يعرف غيره تلك الحالة الموجودة في نفسه، فالناطق الذي جعل فصلاً مقوماً، هو هذا المعنى . والسبب فيه : إنا بينا : أن أكمل طرق التعريف هو النطق، المعبر عن هذه القدرة بأكمل الطرق الدالة عليها . وبهذا الطريق والتقدير، فإن تلك السؤالات لا تتوجه والله أعلم .
The second research concerning this chapter: It is well-known that it is said in the definition of the human: He is a rational animal. Some said: This definition is invalid, both in inclusion and exclusion. As for inclusion, because some animals speak; as for exclusion, because some people do not speak. And when it was answered that what is meant is rational speech, they did not provide a concise interpretation of this rational speech. And we say: Animals are of two types: Among them is that which, when it knows something, it cannot make another know its own state—like beasts and others. For when they find within themselves specific states, they cannot make others know those states. As for the human, when he finds within himself a specific state, he can make another know that state present in himself. So the 'rational' that is made a constitutive differentia is this meaning. The reason for it is that we have explained that the most perfect of the ways of definition is speech, which expresses this ability by the most perfect of the ways indicating it. With this method and estimation, those objections do not apply. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-answer: it presents the famous definition of human as 'rational animal' (hayawān nāṭiq), then raises an objection against it, and finally answers the objection by clarifying what 'rational' means. The author first states the definition and notes that some critics claim it is invalid from both directions: in 'ṭard' (inclusion) because some animals (like birds) actually speak, and in 'ʿaks' (exclusion) because some humans (like infants) do not speak. The initial response was that 'rational' here means 'rational speech' (al-nuṭq al-ʿaqli), but the critics found this too vague. So the author provides his own explanation: animals fall into two types. The first type (beasts) cannot make others aware of their inner states even when they experience specific conditions. The second type (humans) can make others aware of those inner states. Therefore, 'rational' as a constitutive differentia (faṣl muqawwim) refers precisely to this ability to communicate internal experiences. The author then adds that the most perfect method of definition (taʿrīf) is speech, which expresses this ability in the most complete way. With this clarification, the original objections no longer apply.

Correction: The author explains that 'rational' refers to the ability to make others aware of one's inner states, which is unique to humans.

The Four Names of Speech: Etymology and Significance
Enumeration of four names for speech with etymological justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct terms for speech (lafẓ, kalām, ʿibāra, qawl) and the etymological reasons for their naming.

Core Idea
Each name of speech reveals a different dimension: physical, psychological, and spiritual.

In this passage, the author explores why human speech is called by four different names. Each name highlights a unique aspect: the physical act of uttering, the impact on the listener, the crossing of meaning, or the inherent power of words. Understanding these distinctions enriches our appreciation of language and its role in conveying meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analysis. Notice the four names are presented in sequence.
The author uses a research (baḥth) style.
2
Identify each name and its etymology
For each of the four names, note the root word and the reason given. The author uses a 'two aspects' pattern for some.
Compare the different explanations.
3
Reflect on the common thread
Consider how all four names relate to the process of communication from speaker to listener.
Think about the physical and psychological dimensions.
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you understand the derivation of kalām.
Return to the passage if uncertain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث : إن هذه الألفاظ والكلمات، لها أسماء كثيرة : فالأول : اللفظ وفيه وجهان : أحدهما : إن هذه الألفاظ إنما تولدت بسبب أن ذلك الإنسان لفظ ذلك الهواء من حلقه . ولما كان سبب حدوث هذه الأصوات، هو لفظ ذلك الهواء، لا جرم سميناه باللفظ . والثاني : إن تلك المعاني كانت كامنة في قلب ذلك الإنسان، فلما ذكر هذه الألفاظ صارت تلك المعاني الكامنة معلومة، فكان ذلك الإنسان لفظها من الداخل إلى الخارج . والإسم الثاني : الكلام . واشتقاق هذه اللفظة من الكلم، وهو الجرح. والسبب فيه : أن الإنسان إذا سمع تلك اللفظة، فقد تأثر حسه بسماعها، وتأثر عقله يفهم معناها . فلهذا السبب سمي بالكلمة . والإسم الثالث : العبارة. وهي مأخوذة من العبور والمجاوزة . وفيه وجهان : الأول : إن ذلك النفس لما خرج منه، فكأنه جاوزه وعبر عليه . والثاني : إن ذلك المعنى عبر من القائل، إلى فهم المستمع . الإسم الرابع : القول . وهذا التركيب يفيد الشدة والقوة . ولا شك أن تلك اللفظة لها قوة . إما بسبب خروجها إلى الخارج، وإما بسبب أنها تقوى على التأثير في السمع، وعلى التأثير في العقل والله أعلم .
The third research: These utterances and words have many names: The first: al-lafẓ (utterance), and it has two aspects. One: These utterances are generated only because that human expelled that air from his throat. And since the cause of the occurrence of these sounds is the expulsion of that air, we necessarily call it lafẓ. The second: Those meanings were latent in the heart of that human, and when he mentioned these utterances, those latent meanings became known, so that human expelled them from the interior to the exterior. The second name: al-kalām (speech). The derivation of this word is from kalim, which is a wound. The reason for it: When a human hears that utterance, his sense is affected by hearing it, and his intellect is affected by understanding its meaning. For this reason it is called kalimah (word). The third name: al-ʿibāra (expression). It is taken from ʿubūr and mujāwaza (crossing). It has two aspects: The first: When that breath comes out of him, it is as if it crosses and passes over him. The second: That meaning crosses from the speaker to the understanding of the listener. The fourth name: al-qawl (saying). This formation indicates severity and strength. There is no doubt that that utterance has strength, either due to its emergence to the exterior, or because it has power to affect the hearing and to affect the intellect. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of four names for speech (al-alfāẓ wa al-kalimāt). The author lists them in order: (1) al-lafẓ (utterance), (2) al-kalām (speech), (3) al-ʿibāra (expression), and (4) al-qawl (saying). For each he gives etymological and conceptual justifications. For al-lafẓ, two aspects: first, it originates from the expulsion of air from the throat, hence 'lafẓ' literally means 'expelling'. Second, it brings latent inner meanings to outward expression. For al-kalām, it derives from 'kalim' meaning a wound, because the utterance affects the listener's sense (hearing) and intellect (understanding), like a wound. For al-ʿibāra, it comes from 'crossing over' (ʿubūr), with two aspects: the breath crosses outward from the speaker, and the meaning crosses from the speaker to the listener's understanding. For al-qawl, the root q-w-l indicates strength and power; the utterance has power either by emerging outward or by its effect on hearing and intellect. The author thus covers both physical and semantic dimensions of speech.

Key Terms
اللفظ
الكلام
العبارة
القول
الكلم (الجرح)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These four terms are often used interchangeably without distinction.
Correction: Each term has a unique etymological basis and emphasizes a different aspect of speech.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is composed of letters
C) Because it is loud
D) Because it is a divine attribute

Key Takeaway
The four names of speech—lafẓ, kalām, ʿibāra, qawl—each highlight a unique aspect of the speech act, from physical production to intellectual impact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every word you speak is a 'lafẓ' that expels something from your inner self. What does the content of your speech reveal about what is latent in your heart? Today, consciously choose words that reflect truth and goodness, knowing they expose your inner reality.
?
The word 'kalām' is rooted in wounding. Think of a recent conversation where your words may have hurt someone. Will you seek to repair that wound? How can you use the awareness that your speech has power to affect others' senses and intellects to speak more carefully?
?
When you speak, your meaning 'crosses' (ʿibāra) to the listener. Are you sure that the meaning they receive matches what you intend? Consider a time when misunderstanding occurred. What responsibility do you have to ensure your words truly convey your intended meaning?

Core Idea
Imagination is the engine of human craft and invention.

The author now turns to a second distinctive human capacity: the ability to create wonderful crafts. He explains that this ability has both a principle and an instrument, and identifies the principle as the imagination. This lesson unpacks that claim and prepares you to consider the instrument.

The Four Names of Speech and Their Derivations
Enumerates the four names of speech and explains their derivations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four Arabic terms for speech—اللفظ, الكلام, العبارة, القول—and the etymological rationale behind each name.

Core Idea
Each name for speech reveals a different perspective on the act of speaking.

Language is not arbitrary; even the words we use to talk about speech have deep roots. In this passage, a classical scholar enumerates four names for speech and explains why each term was chosen. By understanding these derivations, you gain insight into how Arabic reflects the nature of communication.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of four names
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the structure: four names are introduced sequentially with their derivations.
The names appear in order: اللفظ, الكلام, العبارة, القول.
2
Examine the first name: اللفظ
Understand that اللفظ derives from the act of expelling air from the throat, which generates sound.
The cause of sound is the expulsion of air, so the utterance is called lafẓ.
3
Understand the second name: الكلام
See that الكلام is derived from كلم (wounding) because speech affects the hearer’s sense and intellect.
The word kalām shares a root with wounding, indicating impact.
4
Explore the third name: العبارة
Learn that العبارة comes from عبور (crossing) because breath crosses from speaker to listener, and meaning crosses too.
Two aspects: breath crossing the speaker, meaning crossing to the listener.
5
Consider the fourth name: القول
Recognize that القول implies strength and power, as speech has force in affecting hearing and intellect.
The word qawl has connotations of intensity and efficacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : إن هذه الألفاظ إنما تولدت بسبب أن ذلك الإنسان لفظ ذلك الهواء من حلقه ولما كان سبب حدوث هذه الأصوات هو لفظ ذلك الهواء لا جرم سميناه باللفظ والثاني : إن تلك المعاني كانت كامنة في قلب ذلك الإنسان فلما ذكر هذه الألفاظ صارت تلك المعاني الكامنة معلومة فكان ذلك الإنسان لفظها من الداخل إلى الخارج والإسم الثاني : الكلام واشتقاق هذه اللفظة من الكلم وهو الجرح والسبب فيه أن الإنسان إذا سمع تلك اللفظة فقد تأثر حسه بسماعها وتأثر عقله يفهم معناها فلهذا السبب سمي بالكلمة والإسم الثالث : العبارة وهي مأخوذة من العبور والمجاوزة وفيه وجهان الأول إن ذلك النفس لما خرج منه فكأنه جاوزه وعبر عليه والثاني إن ذلك المعنى عير من القائل إلى فهم المستمع الإسم الرابع : القول وهذا التركيب يفيد الشدة والقوة ولا شك أن تلك اللفظة لها قوة إما بسبب خروجها إلى الخارج وإما بسبب أنها تقوى على التأثير في السمع وعلى التأثير في العقل والله أعلم
One of them: that these utterances are generated only because that man expelled that air from his throat. And since the cause of the occurrence of these sounds is the expulsion of that air, without doubt we named it lafẓ (utterance). And the second: that those meanings were latent in the heart of that man, and when he mentioned these utterances, those latent meanings became known, so that man uttered them from the interior to the exterior. And the second name: al-kalām (speech). The derivation of this word is from kalām (wounding), which is the wound. The reason for it is that when a person hears that utterance, his sense is affected by hearing it, and his intellect is affected by understanding its meaning; for this reason it was named kalimah (word). And the third name: al-ʿibārah (expression). It is taken from al-ʿubūr (crossing) and al-mujāwazah (passing over). There are two aspects in it: first, that the breath when it exits from him, it is as if it passes over and crosses him; second, that the meaning crosses from the speaker to the understanding of the listener. The fourth name: al-qawl (saying). This construction indicates intensity and strength. There is no doubt that that utterance has strength: either because of its exiting to the exterior, or because it is capable of affecting hearing and affecting the intellect. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage enumerates four names for speech and explains the derivation of each. It begins by addressing the first name, lafẓ (utterance), giving two reasons for its naming: first, because utterances are produced by expelling air from the throat; second, because latent meanings in the heart become manifest when uttered, moving from inside to outside. The second name, kalām (speech), is derived from the root k-l-m meaning 'to wound', because hearing speech affects both the sense and the mind. The third name, ʿibārah (expression), comes from the root ʿ-b-r meaning 'to cross over', with two aspects: the breath 'crosses' from the speaker, and the meaning 'crosses' to the listener. The fourth name, qawl (saying), implies strength and power, as the utterance exerts influence on hearing and intellect. The passage thus shows that Arabic has rich terminology for the concept of speech, each with a unique etymological basis.

Key Terms
اللفظ
الكلام
العبارة
القول
اشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These four terms are synonyms meaning the same thing.
Correction: Each term has a distinct etymology focusing on different aspects of speech: utterance, effect, expression, and power.

Key Takeaway
Arabic's four names for speech—اللفظ, الكلام, العبارة, القول—each highlight a unique aspect: the physical act, the impact, the transmission, and the strength of speech.

Definition of the Instrument and Critique of Instinct
Defines the instrument as the body and criticizes the view on animal instinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of the 'instrument' as the body and the distinction between human reasoning and animal instinct.

Core Idea
The instrument is the body; animal instinct is not reasoning but divine subjugation.

This passage from Al-Matālib al-‘Āliyah introduces a foundational term in philosophical theology: the 'instrument' (ālāt). The author not only defines it as the body but also contrasts the way humans use instruments through deduction with the way animals like bees act instinctively. This distinction is critical for understanding later arguments about causation and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the instrument
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the instrument is explicitly identified as 'the body' (al-badan).
The author references Aristotle's term 'permitted instrument' – this will be explained later.
2
Compare human and animal action
Observe how the author contrasts human deduction (istinbāt) and reasoning (qiyās) with animal instinct (ilhām) and subjugation (taskhīr). The example of bees building hexagonal houses is used.
The key point is that animals do not vary their actions because they lack reasoning.
3
Examine the refutation hinted at
The author states that the view attributing instinct to animals is 'refuted by celestial motion' (al-ḥarakat al-falakiyya). Consider why regular celestial motion poses a problem for this explanation.
A full chapter on this refutation is promised later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآلة : فهي البدن . وقد سماها الحكيم أرسطاطاليس الآلة المباحة . وسنذكر تفسير هذه اللفظة في علم التشريح إن شاء الله . وقد يحصل ما يشبه هذه الحالة للحيوانات الأخس كالنحل في بناء البيوت المسدسة ، إلا أن ذلك لا يُصدر عن استنباط وقياس ، بل عن إلهام وتسخير ، ولذلك لا تختلف ولا تتنوع . هكذا قاله الشيخ . وهو منقوض بالحركة الفلكية . وسنفرد لهذا البحث فصلاً على الاستقصاء
As for the instrument: it is the body. The philosopher Aristotle called it the permitted instrument. We will mention the interpretation of this term in anatomy, God willing. And something resembling this state occurs for the lower animals like bees in building hexagonal houses, except that that does not come about through deduction and reasoning, but through inspiration and subjugation, therefore they do not differ nor vary. Thus said the Shaykh. But it is refuted by celestial motion. And we will devote a chapter to this investigation exhaustively.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'instrument' (al-ala) as the body, referencing Aristotle's designation 'permitted instrument'. It then contrasts human reasoning with animal instinct, using bees as an example. The author notes that the Shaykh's explanation (instinct without reasoning) is refuted by the observed regular motion of celestial bodies, which suggests a different kind of determination. The passage concludes with a promise of a detailed discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that animal behavior and human reasoning are essentially the same kind of intelligent action.
Correction: The passage clarifies that animal actions like bees' building come from divine inspiration and subjugation, not from deduction or reasoning, and are therefore uniform, unlike human actions which can vary.

Checkpoint

A) The mind
B) The body

D) The intellect

Key Takeaway
The body is the instrument; animal instinct is divinely inspired and uniform, unlike human reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what area of your life are you most prone to acting without reasoning, like the bees in the passage? Commit to one decision this week that you will make through deliberate reasoning guided by revelation.
?
The passage notes that animal behavior is uniform and unchanging. In contrast, human reasoning allows variety and growth. Where in your spiritual life are you settling for uniformity and routine instead of actively engaging your intellect and will? What is one habitual practice you can transform from a mindless routine into a conscious act of worship?
?
The Shaykh's explanation is refuted by celestial motion. Is there a belief or assumption you hold about God's creation that you have never subjected to rigorous scrutiny? What would it take for you to challenge it as the author challenges the Shaykh's view? Identify one such belief and write down a question you can ask a knowledgeable person this week.

The Four Psychological States: Wonder, Laughter, Shyness, Modesty
Enumerates and defines four psychological states as human properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of four psychological states as uniquely human properties, including their specific triggers and bodily manifestations.

Core Idea
Psychological states are uniquely human responses to specific stimuli, not mere bodily reactions.

In this passage, the author introduces the third type of human properties: psychological states. He enumerates four distinct states—wonder, laughter/weeping, shyness, and modesty—each triggered by a specific perception or belief. This classification highlights the connection between inner experiences and outward bodily manifestations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the author's structured enumeration.
2
Identify the four states
The passage lists four psychological states. For each, identify the trigger (what causes it) and any bodily changes mentioned.
The states are: wonder, laughter/weeping, shyness, modesty.
3
Reflect on the classification
Consider why these are considered 'human properties' and how they differ from purely physiological reflexes. The common misunderstanding is that laughter and weeping are purely physical; the passage ties them to psychological states.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث من خواص الإنسان : الأعراض النفسانية المختلفة على أقسام : فأحدها : أنه إذا رأى شيئاً لم يعرف سببه ، حصل له حالة مخصوصة في نفسه ، سماها بالتعجب . وثانيها : إنه إذا أحس بحصول الملائم ، حصلت له حالة مخصوصة تتبعها أحوال جسمانية . وهي : تمدد في عضلات الوجه ، مع أصوات مخصوصة وهي : الضحك . وإن أحس بحصول المنافي والمؤذي ، حزن . فانعصر دم قلبه في الداخل، فينعصر أيضاً دماغه ، وتنفصل عنه قطرات من الماء. ويخرج من العين ما يسمى بالبكاء . وثالثها : أن الإنسان إذا اعتقد في غيره، أنه اعتقد فيه أنه أقدم على شيء من القبائح ، حصلت حالة مخصوصة تسمى بالخجل . ورابعها : إنه إذا اعتقد في فعل مخصوص أنه قبيح ، فامتنع عنه لقبحه ، حصلت هناك حالة مخصوصة وهي الحياء . وبالجملة : فاستقصاء القول في تعديد الأحوال النفسانية : مذكور في باب الكيفيات النفسانية
The third type of human properties: the various psychological states, divided into sections: First: that when he sees something whose cause he does not know, a specific state occurs in his soul, which he calls wonder. Second: when he senses the occurrence of something agreeable, a specific state occurs followed by bodily states: extension of the facial muscles with specific sounds, which is laughter. And if he senses the occurrence of something harmful or painful, he becomes sad. His heart's blood constricts internally, so his brain also constricts, and drops of water separate from it and emerge from the eye, which is called weeping. Third: when a human believes concerning another that he believes he has committed something disgraceful, a specific state occurs called shyness. Fourth: when he believes concerning a specific action that it is ugly, and refrains from it because of its ugliness, a specific state occurs, which is modesty. In summary: the exhaustive treatment of enumerating psychological states is mentioned in the chapter on psychological qualities.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage enumerates four psychological states as the third type of human properties. It defines wonder as the response to an unknown cause, laughter as response to agreeable sensations with physical manifestations, and weeping as response to harm. Shyness (khajal) arises from belief that another thinks one has done something disgraceful, while modesty (haya') arises from refraining from a bad action due to its ugliness. The author refers to a fuller treatment in another chapter.

Classification of Thought and the First Type
Defines thought and classifies it into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how thought is classified into two types and that the first type (reflection for self-knowledge) is possible in all times.

Core Idea
Thought for self-knowledge is unrestricted in time.

This passage introduces the sixth human power, thought, and immediately divides it into two kinds. The first kind is reflection aimed at knowing one's own state, and it can concern past, present, or future. This foundational distinction sets the stage for understanding what kind of reflective thinking is permissible in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two types
Read the Arabic passage once and note that the author first defines thought then splits it into two types.
2
Focus on the first type and its scope
Examine the description of the first type: reflection for self-knowledge. The author says it is possible in the past, future, and present.
3
Notice the unrestricted time
Observe that unlike other types, this first type has no temporal restrictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع السادس : الفكر والروية . وهذا الفكر على نوعين : أحدهما : أن يتفكر لأجل أن يعرف حالة في نفسه - وهذا النوع من الفكر : ممكن في الماضي والمستقبل والحاضر - والنوع الثاني : التفكر في كيفية إيجاده وتكوينه . وهذا النوع من الفكر لا يمكن في الواجب والممتنع ، وإنما يمكن في الممكن . ثم لا يمكن في الممكن الماضي والحاضر وإنما يمكن في الممكن الاستقبالي . وإذا حكمت هذه القوة ، تبع حكمها حصول الإرادة الجازمة ، ويتبعها تأثير القوة والقدرة في تحريك البدن . وهل لشيء من الحيوانات إدراك شيء من الكليات ؟ المشهور :
And the sixth type: thought and deliberation. This thought is of two kinds: one: that he reflects in order to know a state in himself – and this kind of thought is possible in the past, future, and present. The second kind: reflection on how something is brought into existence and formed. This kind of thought is not possible regarding the necessary and the impossible, but only regarding the possible. Then it is not possible regarding the possible in the past and present, but only regarding the possible in the future. When this power judges, its judgment is followed by the occurrence of decisive will, and that is followed by the effect of power and ability in moving the body. And does any animal perceive any universals? The well-known [view]...
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage describes the sixth human property: thought (fikr) and deliberation (rawiya). It divides thought into two types: (1) reflection for self-knowledge, which can concern past, present, or future; and (2) reflection on how something is brought into existence, which is only possible concerning possible (mumkin) things in the future, not the necessary (wajib) or impossible (mumtani'), nor past or present possible things. The passage explains that when this power judges, it produces decisive will, which then activates the body's power to move. It ends with an open question about whether animals perceive universals (kulliyat), indicating the commonly held view is mentioned but the text cuts off.

Correction: The first type of thought (self-knowledge) is possible in past, present, and future.

Key Takeaway
Thought for self-knowledge can span all temporal dimensions.

Second Type of Thought: Scope and Restrictions
Describes the second type of thought and its temporal and modal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the second type of thought (reflection on creation) is only possible regarding possible future things, not necessary or impossible.

Core Idea
Reflection on causation is limited to possible futures.

Having introduced the first type, the author now presents the second type: reflection on how something is brought into existence. This type is more restricted. It cannot apply to necessary beings or impossible things, only to possible contingent things. Furthermore, among possibles, it only concerns future things, not past or present. This is critical for understanding what can be the subject of reasoned theological reflection.

The Refutation of the Opponent's Dilemma
Sanusi refutes an opponent with a dilemma on soul's perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a dilemma to refute the opponent's claims about the rational soul's management of the body.

Core Idea
A dilemma shows that both alternatives defeat the opponent.

The author begins by stating that the rational soul is a simple substance managing the body. He then constructs a powerful dilemma: either it manages by universal perceptions and intention, or by particular ones. Each horn of the dilemma directly refutes a key claim of the opponent. This brief but decisive argument demonstrates the strength of the rational soul's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the author presents two clear alternatives: either the soul uses universal perception/intention or particular perception/intention.
The passage begins with 'Third' (الثالث) indicating it is part of a series.
2
Analyze each horn of the dilemma
For the first horn, the opponent's claim that universal intention cannot produce particular actions is refuted. For the second horn, the claim that a simple substance cannot have particular perceptions is refuted.
The author uses the same structure for both: 'If the first, then your statement is nullified.'
3
Appreciate the author's astonishment
After presenting the dilemma, the author expresses amazement that the opponents overlook such clear reasoning. This highlights the evident force of his argument.
The phrase 'I am greatly astonished' underscores the clarity of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن النفس الناطقة جوهر مجرد . وهي مدبرة لهذا البدن المعين فإما أن تدير هذا البدن بإدراكات كليه وقصد كلي . وإما أن تدبره بإدراكات جزئية وقصد جزئي . فإن كان الأول ، فقد بطل قولكم : إن القصد الكلي لا يصدر عنه أفعال جزئية . وإن كان الثاني ، فقد بطل قولكم : إن الجوهر المجرد لا يكون صاحب الإدراكات الجزئية. وعلى كلا التقديرين ، فإنه يبطل كلامكم . وأقول : حقاً إني شديد التعجب من غفلة هؤلاء الأقوام عن هذه الكلمات الظاهرة . فهذا هو الكلام على دليلهم والمختار عندي : أن النفس الفلكية جوهر مجرد عن الجسمية وعلائقها . هذا ، فإنها موصوفة بالإدراكات الجزئية، والإرادات الجزئية . وكذلك أيضاً موصوفة بالإدراكات الكلية، والإرادات الكلية . فهي عارفة بربها ، وقاصدة بهذه الحركات : عبادة ربها وخالقها ومدبرها. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فقد زالت الشكوك والشبهات. ويتفرع على ما ذكرنا : فروع . وهي : أن المسمى بنفس الكل(١) هي نفس الفلك الأعظم. وذلك لأن سائر الأفلاك موجودة في باطن الفلك الأعظم . فهي كالأجزاء من الفلك الأعظم . ونفوسهـا كالقوى المتشعبة من نفس الفلك الأعظم ، كما أن القوى المتشعبة عن النفس الناطقة الموجودة في كل واحد من الأعضاء المخصوصة كالنتائج والآثار والشعب لجوهر النفس الناطقة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن تدبير الحق سبحانه لعالم الأجسام ، إنما ابتدأ من الفلك الأعظم، الذي هو العرش. وذلك الابتداء إنما حصل بواسطة تلك النفس التي هي النفس الفلكية . وأما سائر النفوس الفلكية والعنصرية ، نتائجها وشعبها وأولادها. وإليه الإشارة في الدعاء المشهور وهو قوله عليه السلام : اللهم إني أسألك بمعاقد .. على أركان عرشك ، فالمراد من معاقد العز : هو جوهر تلك النفس الكلية وشعبها ، المنبثة منها في أجزاء العرش .
Third: The rational soul is a simple substance. It is the manager of this specific body. So either it manages this body by universal perceptions and universal intention, or it manages it by particular perceptions and particular intention. If the first, then your statement is nullified: that universal intention does not issue in particular actions. And if the second, then your statement is nullified: that the simple substance cannot possess particular perceptions. And on both suppositions, your statement is nullified. And I say: Truly I am greatly astonished at the heedlessness of these people concerning these manifest words. This then is the discussion on their proof. And the chosen view with me is that the celestial soul is a simple substance free from corporeality and its attachments. This is so, for it is described by particular perceptions and particular volitions, and also described by universal perceptions and universal volitions. Thus it knows its Lord, and intends by these movements the worship of its Lord, its Creator, and its Manager. And on this supposition, the doubts and ambiguities have been removed. And there branch from what we have mentioned branches. They are: that what is called the soul of the whole is the soul of the greatest sphere. This is because the other spheres exist in the interior of the greatest sphere. Thus they are like parts of the greatest sphere. And their souls are like the powers branching from the soul of the greatest sphere, just as the powers branching from the rational soul present in each of the particular organs are like the results, traces, and branches of the substance of the rational soul. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the management of the Truth, exalted be He, of the world of bodies began from the greatest sphere, which is the Throne. And that beginning happened by means of that soul which is the celestial soul. As for the other celestial and elemental souls, they are its results, branches, and children. And to this points the well-known supplication, his saying, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, I ask You by the ties of glory upon the pillars of Your Throne.' So what is meant by the ties of glory is the substance of that universal soul and its branches, spread out from it in the parts of the Throne.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a proof combined with a statement of the author's chosen view. The author begins by positing the rational soul as a simple substance that manages the body. He presents a dilemma: either the soul manages with universal perceptions and intention, or with particular perceptions and intention. If the first, then universal intention can produce particular actions, which refutes the opponent who claimed it cannot. If the second, then a simple substance can have particular perceptions, refuting the opponent who denied that. Either way the opponent's position is invalidated. The author then expresses his astonishment at their oversight and presents his own view: the celestial soul is a simple substance free from corporeality, and it possesses both particular and universal perceptions and volitions, allowing it to know God and worship Him through its movements. This resolves doubts. He then elaborates a branch: the soul of the whole is the soul of the greatest sphere (the Throne). Other spheres are like parts, and their souls are like powers branching from that soul, analogous to the powers branching from the human rational soul. Thus, God's management of the physical world begins from the Throne via that celestial soul. Other souls are its offshoots. He concludes by referencing a prophetic supplication, interpreting 'the ties of glory' as that universal soul and its branches.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
جوهر مجرد
إدراكات جزئية
إدراكات كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a simple substance cannot have particular perceptions.
Correction: The author argues that if the soul manages the body by particular perceptions, then a simple substance can have particular perceptions, refuting that belief.

Checkpoint
What happens if the rational soul manages the body by universal perceptions and intention, according to the passage?
A) The opponent's statement is nullified because universal intention cannot produce particular actions.
B) The opponent's statement is nullified because universal intention can produce particular actions.
C) The opponent's statement is confirmed.
D) The soul's management is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that regardless of whether the soul uses universal or particular perceptions, the opponent's position is refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a dilemma between two opposing arguments, are you quick to take one side and ignore the other? Or do you examine whether both possibilities might actually refute your own assumptions? Which habit is more spiritually honest?
?
Sanusi says he is 'greatly astonished' at the heedlessness of those who miss such clear reasoning. In what area of your life have you been overlooking an obvious truth because you were attached to a comfortable belief? Will you confront it today?
?
This lesson affirms that your rational soul manages your body. Do you truly live as if you are a soul using a body, or does your body's desires dictate your choices? What one decision this week can you make to assert your soul's authority over your body?
